The Indicating Essential: Correspondence and Inclusion
Author defines indicating essential and gives examples of indication modes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between indication by correspondence (muṭābaqah) and indication by inclusion (taḍammun) in definitions, and identify which mode is used in given examples from Islamic definitions.

Core Idea
A definition can indicate the essence fully by correspondence or by inclusion, as in 'man' or 'rational animal'.

In logic, when we answer 'What is it?', we use terms that indicate the essence. The author explains that this indication can happen in two ways: by correspondence, where the word's meaning exactly matches the essence, and by inclusion, where the word implies more than it directly states. Understanding these modes is crucial for constructing proper definitions in Islamic sciences, such as in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum (e.g., 'man' in the passage). Note whether it is a single word or a compound expression.
2
Determine indication by correspondence
Check if the word or phrase directly matches the complete essence. For example, 'man' as a single word indicates the whole essence by correspondence.
Muṭābaqah means the word and the meaning correspond exactly.
3
Determine indication by inclusion
See if the word implies additional essentials beyond its direct meaning. For instance, 'man' implies all its essential parts (e.g., animality, rationality) by inclusion.
Taḍammun means the word contains these essentials implicitly.
4
Test with a compound definition
Analyze 'rational animal': each part ('animal', 'rational') indicates its own meaning by correspondence, and the whole indicates the essence of man by inclusion of all parts.
Compound definitions often combine both modes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في الدال وغير الدال ]
ولما كان الذاتي ينقسم إلى الدال على الماهية وإلى غير الدال؛ فالتعرض للدال على الماهية من اللوازم، وذلك هو الذي يجاب به حين يسأل عن الشيء أنه ما هو أي ما ذاته وحقيقته. والصالح لهذا الجواب هو اللفظ المطابق لمعناه المتضمن لجميع ذاتياته أو المقول الدال عليه. فإذا سئل عن الإنسان مثلا أنه «ما هو ؟ فيقال: إنه إنسان». والإنسان لفظ مفرد دال على تمام معناه بالمطابقة، وعلى جميع ذاتياته بالتضمن ولو قلت: «إنه حيوان ناطق» فهذا قول يدل على تمام معنى الحيوان والناطق بالمطابقة على جميع ذاتيات هذا، وذلك بالتضمن فيدل على حقيقة الإنسان كذلك. ولو قلت في الجواب: «إنه جوهر ذو أبعاد ثلاثة متنفس حساس متحرك بالإرادة ناطق» وقد أجبت على وجه الصواب أيضًا.
وعن البعض من القدماء أنه كان يكتفي في تعريف الدال على الماهية بأنه الذاتي الأعم وهذا باطل، فإنه لا يطابق أصلا لا بالوضع اللغوي ولا بالوضع المنطقي : [أ] أما اللغوي فإنه بطلت حقيقة الشيء وماهيته، وحقيقة الشيء لا يحصل بذاتي يعم هذا وغيره؛ بل بما يختص أيضًا إن كان له أمر خاص يتقوم به كالناطق مثلا، ولا يظن أن الناطق في معنى الإنسان، وكيف وقد كان الإنسان نوعا للحيوان دون الناطق ؟ [ب] وأما المنطقي فإن أهل المنطق اتفقوا على أنه لا يجاب عن هذا بأشياء يسمونها بفصول الأجناس، وهي كما تعرفها بعد ذاتيات تعم الأنواع المختلفة لكنه وهو الذاتي الأعم وإن لم يكن دالا على الماهية ولا مقولاً في جواب ما هو ؟»، فإنه داخل في الماهية ومقول في الطريق كذلك. وذلك لأن المقول في الجواب إما أن يكون تمام الماهية: وهذا هو المقول في جواب «ما هو؟» ، كقولنا حيوان ناطق مثلا؛ أو داخلا فيها وأنه إما أن يكون بطريق المطابقة كالحيوان والناطق في قولنا حيوان ناطق»، وهذا هو المقول في طريق ما هو ؟»، أو بالتضمن كالجسم والجوهر فيه، وهذا هو الداخل في جواب «ما هو؟». فقولنا ما هو بحسب ما يوجبه كل لغة هو أنه ما ذاته وما مفهوم اسمه وما يدل على المطلوب فيه، فذلك على ثلاثة أقسام: [1] منها ما يدل بالخصوصية المطلقة نحو الحيوان الناطق على الإنسان وستعرف بعد أن هذه الدلالة هي دلالة الحد على المحدود. [ب] ومنها ما يدل بالشركة المطلقة، كما إذا سئل عن أشياء مختلفة الماهيات مثل إنسان وفرس وثور مثلا ما هي؟»، فيقال في الجواب ما يصلح له وهو الذي يدل على الماهية المشتركة بينها، وذلك هو الحيوان فأما ما هو الأعم من الحيوان مثل الجوهر والجسم فليس لكمال الماهية المشتركة بينها، وما هو الأخص منه مثل الإنسان والفرس والثور فينطوي كل لفظ منه على خصوصية زائدة
Chapter: On the Indicating and Non-Indicating
Since the essential is divided into that which indicates the essence and that which does not indicate; then addressing that which indicates the essence is among the necessary matters, and it is that by which one answers when asked about a thing 'What is it?', i.e., what is its essence and reality. And suitable for this answer is the word that corresponds to its meaning, encompassing all its essentials, or the predicate indicating it. So if one is asked about man, for example, 'What is he?', it is said 'He is a man.' And 'man' is a single word indicating its complete meaning by correspondence, and all its essentials by inclusion. And if you say 'He is a rational animal', this is a statement indicating the complete meaning of 'animal' and 'rational' by correspondence, and all the essentials of this [combined] by inclusion, thus indicating the reality of man similarly. And if you say in the answer 'He is a substance possessing three dimensions, breathing, sentient, moving by will, rational', you have answered correctly as well.
And concerning some of the ancients, that they were content in defining the indicator of the essence as the broader essential, and this is false, because it does not correspond at all, neither linguistically nor logically. [A] As for linguistically, it nullifies the reality of the thing and its essence, and the reality of the thing is not obtained by an essential that includes this and others; rather by what is specific as well, if it has a particular matter by which it is constituted, like 'rational' for example. And do not think that 'rational' is the meaning of man; how could it be, when man is a species of animal without 'rational'? [B] As for logically, the logicians have agreed that one does not answer with things they call 'differential of genera', which, as you will know later, are essentials that include different species, but it—i.e., the broader essential—even if it does not indicate the essence nor is it a predicate in the answer to 'What is it?', is still included in the essence and is a predicate in the [general] way. That is because the predicate in the answer is either the complete essence: this is the predicate in the answer to 'What is it?', like our saying 'rational animal' for example; or it is included in it, and it is either by way of correspondence, like 'animal' and 'rational' in our saying 'rational animal', and this is the predicate in the way of 'What is it?', or by inclusion, like 'body' and 'substance' in it, and this is the included in the answer to 'What is it?'. So our saying 'What is it?' according to what every language requires is: it is asking about its essence, and the meaning of its name, and what indicates the desired [meaning] in it. So it is of three divisions: [1] Among them is that which indicates by absolute specificity, like 'rational animal' indicating man, and you will know later that this indication is the indication of the definition (hadd) of the defined. [B] Among them is that which indicates by absolute commonality, as if one is asked about things of different essences, like man, horse, and ox, for example, 'What are they?', then in the answer one says what is suitable for them, which is that which indicates the common essence among them, and that is 'animal'. As for what is broader than 'animal', like 'substance' and 'body', it is not for the completeness of the common essence among them. And what is more specific than it, like 'man', 'horse', and 'ox', each word contains an additional specificity.
Source pages p. 128

Commentary
This passage defines the 'indicating essential' (al-dāllu ‘alā al-māhiyyah) and the different ways of answering the question 'What is it?' (mā huwa). The author begins by stating that the essential (al-dhātī) is divided into that which indicates the essence and that which does not. The indicating essential is what is used in answer to the question about a thing's essence and reality. The suitable answer is a word that either corresponds to the meaning and encompasses all essences (like the single word 'man' when asked about man, indicating the complete essence by correspondence and all essences by inclusion) or a predicate that indicates the essence (like 'rational animal'). The author gives examples: saying 'man' alone suffices for the question, but more detailed answers like 'rational animal' or even a full list like 'substance possessing three dimensions...' are also correct.

Next, the author refutes the view of some ancient philosophers who limited the definition of the indicating essential to merely the broader essential (al-dhātī al-a‘amm). This view is rejected both linguistically and logically. Linguistically, such a broader term does not capture the specific reality of a thing; for example, 'animal' alone does not give the essence of man, as man is a species of animal with a specific difference 'rational'. Logically, the logicians agree that one does not answer with such broad terms (called 'differential of genera'), as they do not directly indicate the essence. However, the author clarifies that even if the broader essential is not a direct answer to 'What is it?', it is still included in the essence and can be considered a predicate in a broader sense.

The author then classifies the predicate in the answer: either the complete essence (like 'rational animal') which is the direct answer to 'What is it?', or something included in the essence. Inclusion can be by correspondence (like 'animal' in 'rational animal' corresponds to part of the essence) or by inclusion (like 'body' in 'animal' is included). Finally, the answers are divided into three types: (1) indication by absolute specificity (like 'rational animal' for man), which is the definition (hadd) of the defined; (2) indication by absolute commonality (like 'animal' for man, horse, and ox), which is the common essence; and (3) what is broader than the common essence (like 'substance') is not sufficient for the complete common essence, and what is more specific (like 'man') has an additional specificity. This classification helps in understanding how definitions work in logic.

المطابقة
التضمن
الحد

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how does the single word 'man' indicate the essence of man?
A) Only by correspondence, not by inclusion.
B) Only by inclusion, not by correspondence.
C) By correspondence of the complete meaning and by inclusion of all essentials.
D) It does not indicate the essence at all.

Key Takeaway
The indicating essential works through two modes: correspondence (direct match) and inclusion (implied parts); both are necessary for complete definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify whether the term 'zakāt' indicates by correspondence or inclusion when defined as 'a specific portion of wealth given to specific recipients'. Explain your reasoning.
?
2. Examine the definition of 'wudū'' as 'washing specific limbs with water'. Does it indicate the essence by correspondence, inclusion, or both? Justify.

Classifying Answers to 'What Is It?' and Refuting the Broader Essential
Author refutes broader essential and classifies answer types

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify answers to 'What is it?' into complete essence, included by correspondence, and included by inclusion, and evaluate why the broader essential alone is insufficient.

Core Idea
The broader essential cannot answer 'What is it?' as it lacks specificity; only the full definition or its included parts work.

The author refutes the view that the broader essential (like 'animal') suffices to answer 'What is it?'. He then classifies the predicate in the answer into three types: the complete essence, included by correspondence, and included by inclusion. This classification is foundational for understanding how definitions are structured in Islamic logic, especially in theological discussions about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the position being refuted
Locate the claim that the broader essential (al-dhātī al-a‘amm) is sufficient to answer 'What is it?'. The author rejects this both linguistically and logically.
The broader essential includes multiple species, e.g., 'animal' for man, horse, ox.
2
Understand the linguistic refutation
The broader essential nullifies the specific reality; e.g., 'animal' does not give the essence of man because it lacks the specific difference 'rational'.
Do not think 'rational' means the same as 'man'.
3
Understand the logical refutation
Logicians agree that one does not answer with 'differentials of genera' (fūṣūl al-ajnās). The broader essential does not directly indicate the essence, but it is still included in the essence.
4
Classify predicates in the answer
The predicate is either the complete essence (like 'rational animal') or included in it, either by correspondence (like 'animal' in 'rational animal') or by inclusion (like 'body' in 'animal').
This leads to three types of indication: absolute specificity (hadd), absolute commonality (like 'animal'), and broader/narrower terms.

[ب] ومنها ما يدل بالشركة المطلقة، كما إذا سئل عن أشياء مختلفة الماهيات مثل إنسان وفرس وثور مثلا ما هي؟»، فيقال في الجواب ما يصلح له وهو الذي يدل على الماهية المشتركة بينها، وذلك هو الحيوان فأما ما هو الأعم من الحيوان مثل الجوهر والجسم فليس لكمال الماهية المشتركة بينها، وما هو الأخص منه مثل الإنسان والفرس والثور فينطوي كل لفظ منه على خصوصية زائدة
Chapter: On the Indicating and Non-Indicating
Since the essential is divided into that which indicates the essence and that which does not indicate; then addressing that which indicates the essence is among the necessary matters, and it is that by which one answers when asked about a thing 'What is it?', i.e., what is its essence and reality. And suitable for this answer is the word that corresponds to its meaning, encompassing all its essentials, or the predicate indicating it. So if one is asked about man, for example, 'What is he?', it is said 'He is a man.' And 'man' is a single word indicating its complete meaning by correspondence, and all its essentials by inclusion. And if you say 'He is a rational animal', this is a statement indicating the complete meaning of 'animal' and 'rational' by correspondence, and all the essentials of this [combined] by inclusion, thus indicating the reality of man similarly. And if you say in the answer 'He is a substance possessing three dimensions, breathing, sentient, moving by will, rational', you have answered correctly as well.
And concerning some of the ancients, that they were content in defining the indicator of the essence as the broader essential, and this is false, because it does not correspond at all, neither linguistically nor logically. [A] As for linguistically, it nullifies the reality of the thing and its essence, and the reality of the thing is not obtained by an essential that includes this and others; rather by what is specific as well, if it has a particular matter by which it is constituted, like 'rational' for example. And do not think that 'rational' is the meaning of man; how could it be, when man is a species of animal without 'rational'? [B] As for logically, the logicians have agreed that one does not answer with things they call 'differential of genera', which, as you will know later, are essentials that include different species, but it—i.e., the broader essential—even if it does not indicate the essence nor is it a predicate in the answer to 'What is it?', is still included in the essence and is a predicate in the [general] way. That is because the predicate in the answer is either the complete essence: this is the predicate in the answer to 'What is it?', like our saying 'rational animal' for example; or it is included in it, and it is either by way of correspondence, like 'animal' and 'rational' in our saying 'rational animal', and this is the predicate in the way of 'What is it?', or by inclusion, like 'body' and 'substance' in it, and this is the included in the answer to 'What is it?'. So our saying 'What is it?' according to what every language requires is: it is asking about its essence, and the meaning of its name, and what indicates the desired [meaning] in it. So it is of three divisions: [1] Among them is that which indicates by absolute specificity, like 'rational animal' indicating man, and you will know later that this indication is the indication of the definition (hadd) of the defined. [B] Among them is that which indicates by absolute commonality, as if one is asked about things of different essences, like man, horse, and ox, for example, 'What are they?', then in the answer one says what is suitable for them, which is that which indicates the common essence among them, and that is 'animal'. As for what is broader than 'animal', like 'substance' and 'body', it is not for the completeness of the common essence among them. And what is more specific than it, like 'man', 'horse', and 'ox', each word contains an additional specificity.

Commentary
This passage defines the 'indicating essential' (al-dāllu ‘alā al-māhiyyah) and the different ways of answering the question 'What is it?' (mā huwa). The author begins by stating that the essential (al-dhātī) is divided into that which indicates the essence and that which does not. The indicating essential is what is used in answer to the question about a thing's essence and reality. The suitable answer is a word that either corresponds to the meaning and encompasses all essences (like the single word 'man' when asked about man, indicating the complete essence by correspondence and all essences by inclusion) or a predicate that indicates the essence (like 'rational animal'). The author gives examples: saying 'man' alone suffices for the question, but more detailed answers like 'rational animal' or even a full list like 'substance possessing three dimensions...' are also correct.

Next, the author refutes the view of some ancient philosophers who limited the definition of the indicating essential to merely the broader essential (al-dhātī al-a‘amm). This view is rejected both linguistically and logically. Linguistically, such a broader term does not capture the specific reality of a thing; for example, 'animal' alone does not give the essence of man, as man is a species of animal with a specific difference 'rational'. Logically, the logicians agree that one does not answer with such broad terms (called 'differential of genera'), as they do not directly indicate the essence. However, the author clarifies that even if the broader essential is not a direct answer to 'What is it?', it is still included in the essence and can be considered a predicate in a broader sense.

The author then classifies the predicate in the answer: either the complete essence (like 'rational animal') which is the direct answer to 'What is it?', or something included in the essence. Inclusion can be by correspondence (like 'animal' in 'rational animal' corresponds to part of the essence) or by inclusion (like 'body' in 'animal' is included). Finally, the answers are divided into three types: (1) indication by absolute specificity (like 'rational animal' for man), which is the definition (hadd) of the defined; (2) indication by absolute commonality (like 'animal' for man, horse, and ox), which is the common essence; and (3) what is broader than the common essence (like 'substance') is not sufficient for the complete common essence, and what is more specific (like 'man') has an additional specificity. This classification helps in understanding how definitions work in logic.

According to the author, why is the broader essential (e.g., 'animal') not sufficient to answer 'What is man?'?

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Direct vs Indirect Indication in Definitions
Author explains direct vs indirect indication of essence in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish direct indication (mutabaqa) from indirect indication (iltizam) in a definition and determine which type is appropriate for answering 'what is it?'.

Core Idea
Only terms indicating the full common essence by direct correspondence can answer 'what is it?'

When someone asks 'What is it?', the answer must name the essence directly. This passage shows why terms like 'the sensitive' fail—they only imply the essence indirectly—whereas 'animal' succeeds because it directly matches the shared essential attributes. Understanding this distinction is crucial for constructing sound definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the question type
Note that the author is discussing how to properly answer a 'what is it?' (ma huwa) question, which seeks the essence. Contrast this with 'who is it?' which seeks an individual.
This distinction is foundational for definition theory.
2
Classify the candidate terms
List the terms mentioned: al-hassas (the sensitive), al-mutaharrik bil-irada (the voluntarily moving), al-hayawan (the animal). Determine which indicate the essence directly and which by implication.
The author says al-hassas and al-mutaharrik only imply the body, not directly indicate it.
3
Understand al-hayawan's direct indication
Al-hayawan indicates 'a body with a sensitive soul moving voluntarily' by direct correspondence (mutabaqa). It encompasses all the essential attributes shared among human, horse, ox.
Direct indication means the term's meaning exactly covers the intended essence.
4
Contrast with indirect indication (iltizam)
Al-hassas indicates only sense, and the mind must infer that it must be a body. This rational implication is not allowed in scientific definitions according to the logicians.
The author emphasizes that such implied indications are 'prohibited from use in the sciences'.
5
Apply the rule to new examples
Test whether a given term would be a proper answer to 'what is it?' by checking if it directly states the common essence without needing extra inference.
For instance, 'the one who prays' does not directly indicate 'human'; it implies it.

على ما فيه الاشتراك فلا يكون مطابقا للسؤال بل زائدا عليه وما هو المساوي له مثل الحساس والمتحرك بالإرادة فليس يصلح للدلالة على ماهيتها. وذلك لأن الحساس إنما يدل بالوضع اللغوي على شيء ما له حس فقط، وليس له دلالة على الجسمية إلا بطريق الالتزام وهو شعور الذهن بأن الحساس لا يكون في الوجود إلا وأن يكون جسما، ومثل هذه الدلالة دلالة عقلية هي محجورة الاستعمال في العلوم على ما عرف وليس لأهل المنطق في أمثال هذه الألفاظ وضع ما عدا الوضع اللغوي. وأما الحيوان فإنه يدل بحسب اللغة على تمام ما به المشاركة بينها وهو جسم ذو نفس حساس متحرك بالإرادة دلالة هي بطريق المطابقة فلا يشذ شيء منها؛ بل يتناول جميع الذاتيات المشتركة بينها، أعني بين الإنسان والفرس والثور فيكون دالا على ماهيتها بالضرورة ومنها ما يدل بالشركة والخصوصية أيضًا، كما إذا سئل عن جماعة هم زيد وعمرو وخالد ما هم؟، كان الذي يصلح الجواب هو الذي يدل على الماهية المشتركة بينهم، وذلك هو الإنسان. وقد يقال في الجواب: إنهم ناس ، وكذلك إذا سئل عن زيد وحده ما هو؟، لا أن يقال من هو كان الحيوان، إنه إنسان فإن ماهية زيد وحده هي الماهية المشتركة بينه وبين غيره من أشخاص هذا النوع، وما يفضل في زيد على الإنسانية فذلك من العوارض اللازمة أو المفارقة. ثم اللازمة وإن كان لا يفارقه من أول كونه لاقتران أمور عارضة بمادته التي هو منها خلق، أو كانت عارضة لها في رحم أمه، يمكن في الوهم فرض عدمها وعروض أضدادها في مبدأ الخلقة ويكون هو بعينه ذلك الإنسان. وأما نسبة الإنسانية إلى ذلك الحيوان فليس كذلك؛ إذ لا يمكن أن يفرض بقاء ذلك الحيوان بعينه عند فرض زوال الإنسانية وحصول الفرسية؛ بل ذلك الحيوان في الوجود هو ذلك الإنسان. وما هو الأقرب إلى الفهم، فذلك أن يقال إن ذلك الحيوان الذي هو الإنسان إنما يكون من مادة وصورة بحسبه. وأما أن يتم بكونه منهما فيكون ذلك الإنسان بعينه، أو لا يتم فلا يكون لا ذلك الإنسان ولا ذلك الحيوان وليس يحتمل التقدير الآخر، وهو أنه إنما يصير إنسانًا بلواحق تلحق مادته إذا فرضنا عدمها وعروض أضدادها ليكون حيوانا غير إنسان. فإنه لا يمكن أن يصير إنسانًا بما يعرض في مادته المستعدة للحيوانية حتى إذا لم يكن هو لم يكن إنسانًا؛ بل إنما جعله إنسانًا عين ما جعله إنسانًا، فجعله للحيوانية هو جعله للإنسانية، أعني حيوانية زيد وإنسانيته. وعلى هذا في كل فرد من كل نوع : فإن الحيوان الذي هو الفرس مثلا، إنما يكونه من مادة وصورة بحسبه كما في الإنسان. فالحيوان الذي هو الفرس لا يمكن أن تتبدل فرسيته بالإنسانية، حتى إذا فرضنا زوال الفرسية وعروض الإنسانية فذلك الحيوان هو ذلك الحيوان بعينه ; بل الذي هو الفرس فهو بعينه ذلك الفرس. وذلك الحيوان كذلك فلا يمكن أن يكون على خلاف ذلك أصلا، يعني أن يكون إنسانًا يدل كونه فرسًا أو فرسا يدل كونه إنسانًا. وكيف يمكن؟ وإنه لا يمكن أن يوجد إلا وأن يوجد إنسانًا أو فرسًا أو غير ذلك من أنواعه فلو وجد إنسانًا مثلا، فلا يمكن أن يوجد فرسًا وهو إنسان، وكذلك إذا وجد فرسًا أو غير ذلك. وأما التعرض للمثال في كل نوع من كل جنس فليس ذلك على المنطقي؛ بل عليه إعطاء القانون الذي يعتدي به في الأمثلة وأجزاء حكمه فيها إن كانت على وفق موجبها.
On what has commonality, so it does not correspond to the question but exceeds it, and what is equal to it like 'the sensitive' and 'the voluntarily moving' is not suitable for indicating its quiddity. That is because 'the sensitive' indicates linguistically only a thing that has sense, and it has no indication of corporeality except by way of implication, which is the mind's awareness that the sensitive does not exist except as a body. This kind of indication is a rational indication that is prohibited from use in the sciences as is known, and the logicians have no convention for such expressions beyond the linguistic convention. As for 'animal', it indicates according to language the entirety of what constitutes the commonality among them, which is a body with a sensitive soul moving voluntarily, in a way that is by direct correspondence, so nothing escapes it; rather it encompasses all the essential attributes shared among them, I mean between human, horse, and ox, so it indicates their quiddity necessarily. And among them is what indicates by commonality and specificity as well, as when one asks about a group of Zayd, Amr, and Khalid 'What are they?', the appropriate answer is that which indicates the common quiddity among them, and that is 'human'. And it may be said in answer: 'They are people.' Likewise, when one asks about Zayd alone 'What is he?' — not 'Who is he?' — then 'animal' would not be said, rather 'he is a human', for the quiddity of Zayd alone is the common quiddity between him and other individuals of this species, and what is additional in Zayd over humanity are the inseparable or separable accidents. Then the inseparable, even if it does not separate from him from the beginning of his existence due to the conjunction of accidental matters with his matter from which he was created, or they were accidental to it in his mother's womb, it is possible in the imagination to suppose their non-existence and the occurrence of their opposites at the beginning of creation, and he would be that same human. As for the relation of humanity to that animal, it is not so; for it is not possible to suppose the survival of that same animal while supposing the removal of humanity and the acquisition of horseness; rather that animal in existence is that human. And what is closer to understanding, that is to say that that animal which is the human is only constituted from matter and form according to it. And if it is completed by being from them, then that human is the same, or if it is not completed, then neither that human nor that animal exists, and it does not admit the other estimation, which is that it becomes human only by appendages attached to its matter if we suppose their non-existence and the occurrence of their opposites to be an animal other than human. For it is not possible for it to become human by what occurs in its matter prepared for animality, such that if it were not it would not be human; rather what made it human is exactly what made it human, so making it for animality is making it for humanity, I mean the animality of Zayd and his humanity. And likewise in every individual of every species: for the animal that is the horse, for example, is only constituted from matter and form according to it, as in the human. So the animal that is the horse cannot have its horseness changed to humanity, such that if we suppose the removal of horseness and the occurrence of humanity, that animal would be that same animal; rather that which is the horse is exactly that horse. And that animal is likewise, so it cannot be otherwise at all, meaning that it would be a human indicating its being a horse or a horse indicating its being a human. How can that be? And it cannot exist except as a human or a horse or other of its species, so if it exists as a human, it cannot exist as a horse while it is human, and likewise if it exists as a horse or other. As for engaging in the example for every species of every genus, that is not incumbent on the logician; rather he must give the law by which one proceeds in the examples and the parts of its judgment therein if they are in accordance with its requirement.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage explains the proper way to answer a 'what is it?' (ma huwa) question in logic. The author distinguishes between terms that indicate the essence directly (bi-tariq al-mutabaqa) and those that indicate it only indirectly (bi-tariq al-iltizam). Using examples, he shows that 'al-hassas' (the sensitive) does not directly indicate 'body' but only implies it, whereas 'al-hayawan' (animal) directly indicates the common essence of a sensitive, voluntary-moving body. He then discusses the distinction between essential and accidental attributes, and how the species (e.g., human) is inseparable from the individual. The author argues that the animal which is human is constituted by its specific matter and form, and cannot change to another species while remaining the same individual. This refutes the idea that a human could become a horse by acquiring accidental attributes. The passage ends by emphasizing that the logician's job is to provide the general law, not to enumerate examples for every species.

Key Terms
المطابقة
الالتزام
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is 'al-hassas' (the sensitive) not an appropriate answer to 'what is it?' for a human?
A) Because it indicates only an accident, not the essence.
B) Because it indicates the essence only by implication (iltizam), not directly.
C) Because it is a synonym for 'animal'.
D) Because it is a genus that includes other species.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must directly (mutabaqa) indicate the common essence without relying on implied meanings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following terms as indicating by mutabaqa or iltizam when answering 'what is humanity?': 'natiq (rational)', 'dahik (laughing)', 'bashar (human being)'. Explain your reasoning using the author's criteria.
?
In a fiqh debate, someone defines 'riba' (usury) as 'excess in exchange of two similar items'. Is this a direct definition (hadd) or a description (rasm)? Use the passage's concept of direct indication to justify your answer.

Hadd and Rasm
advanced
10 min
Species Essence Is Inseparable from Individuals
Author argues species essence is inseparable from individual identity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why an individual cannot change species while remaining the same individual, using the author's argument about essential vs. accidental attributes.

Core Idea
The species essence is constitutive of the individual; changing species would destroy the individual.

After establishing that 'animal' directly indicates the common essence, the author tackles a deeper question: can an individual of one species (e.g., a human) become another species (e.g., a horse) while remaining the same individual? He argues no, because the species essence is not an accident but part of what makes the individual what it is. This lesson unpacks that metaphysical insight and its implications for understanding identity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The author asserts that for a human, the animality and humanity are inseparable: 'making it for animality is making it for humanity'. The individual human cannot persist if its humanity is replaced by horseness.
This claim is about the identity of individuals across possible changes.
2
Distinguish essential vs. accidental attributes
Recall the earlier distinction: what is additional to humanity in Zayd (e.g., his specific complexion) are accidents (inseparable or separable). But humanity itself is not an accident; it is essential.
The author says one can imagine removing accidental traits but not the species essence.
3
Understand the thought experiment
The author asks: could we suppose Zayd loses humanity and gains horseness while remaining the same individual? He answers no, because the animal that is Zayd is constituted by human matter and form; if you replace that, you have a different individual.
The matter and form specific to the species are what make the individual what it is.
4
Apply to other species
The same logic applies to every species: the animal that is a horse cannot become a human while remaining the same horse. The horse's 'horseness' is not an accidental property that can be swapped.
The author generalizes: 'in every individual of every species'.
5
Recognize the logician's role
The author concludes that the logician's job is to provide the general law, not to enumerate examples for every species. This is a methodological point about logic's scope.
The law is that species essence is indivisible from individual identity.

Distinguishing Composite from Simple Quiddity
Author defines composite and simple quiddity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish composite quiddity from simple quiddity based on divisibility into parts of different natures.

Core Idea
Quiddity divides into composite (multiple natures) and simple (single nature).

In logic, understanding quiddity (mahiyya) is essential for defining things correctly. The author here introduces two fundamental types: composite quiddity, which has parts of different natures, and simple quiddity, which does not. This distinction is critical because it affects how we define and analyze things in Islamic sciences, especially in kalam and usul al-fiqh when discussing essences.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two types of quiddity
Find the two categories explicitly named in the Arabic: مركبة and بسيطة. The author states that quiddity may be composite or simple.
2
Extract the defining property
Composite quiddity is defined by divisibility into parts of different natures. Simple quiddity is the opposite: not divisible into such parts.
The criterion is 'different natures', not just any division.
3
Apply to a concrete example
The commentary gives 'human' as composite (animality and rationality). Think of a simple quiddity like 'whiteness' (assuming it is not further composed of different natures).
In kalam, the divine essence is said to be simple.
4
Verify the classification
Check that the property (divisibility into different natures) is both necessary and sufficient for composite quiddity. Does every composite quiddity have parts of different natures? Does anything with such parts count as composite?
The text is cut off, but the definition as given is meant to be exhaustive.

Composite and Simple Quiddity Defined
Author defines composite vs simple quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define composite and simple quiddity based on the criteria of divisibility into parts of different natures, and classify examples from Islamic philosophy.

Core Idea
Quiddity is either composite (divisible into different-natured parts) or simple (not so divisible).

This passage opens with a foundational distinction in Islamic logic and metaphysics: quiddity (mahiyya) is either composite (murakkaba) or simple (basīta). The author provides precise criteria: a composite quiddity divides into parts of different natures, while a simple does not. Understanding this division is essential for later discussions about definitions and the structure of reality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find the term being defined. Here it is 'al-māhiyya' (quiddity). The passage divides it into two types.
The definiendum is the whole genus of quiddity.
2
Extract the genus and differentia for each type
The genus is 'quiddity' itself. The differentia for composite is 'divides into parts of different natures'; for simple, 'does not so divide'.
This is a complete description (rasm tāmm) because it gives the distinguishing feature.
3
Test the definition's completeness
Does the definition cover all quiddities? Yes, every quiddity is either composite or simple. Does it exclude non-quiddities? It only talks about quiddities.
4
Apply to a concrete Islamic example
Consider 'human' (insān): it is composed of animal and rational – two different natures. Thus it is composite. Consider 'existence' (wujūd) according to many philosophers: it is simple because it has no parts of different natures.
These examples help internalise the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في الماهية ] أما الماهية فإنه قد تكون مركبة وقد لا تكون بل تكون بسيطة. والمركبة عبارة عما ينقسم في ذاته إلى أقسام مختلفة الطباع، والبسيطة في مقابلتها لا محالة فتكون عبارة عما لا ينقسم إلى تلك الأقسام. ثم المركبة إذا كانت متحققة كانت البسائط متحققة بالضرورة، وإلا يلزم التسلسل لا مرة واحدة؛ بل مرارًا غير متناهية . وإنها أي البسائط أجزاء الماهية لما كانت الماهية مركبة منها. فأما الأجزاء فإنها قد تكون من البسائط وقد لا تكون بل تكون من المركبات كلا أو بعضًا. ثم الأجزاء متناهية وإلا لا تكون معقولة ولا يمكن أن يكون البعض منها عدميًّا إلا وأن تكون الماهية ماهية اعتبارية كالأعمى، فإن فيه عدم البصر عما من شأنه أن يبصر . ولا يمكن أن يكون الجزء قابلا للأشد والأضعف، وإلا لكانت الماهية أي ماهية كانت قابلة للأشد والأضعف كذلك، وذلك محال. فإن ما هو الزائل عن الماهية ، فذلك إما أن يكون معتبراً في الماهية وحينئذ لا يمكن اعتبار الماهية بدون ذلك؛ أو لا يكون معتبراً، وحينئذ يمكن اعتبارها بدونه بل يلزم في كون الماهية ماهية بالضرورة. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن تكون الماهية قابلة للأشد والأضعف، ولا يمكن أن يكون جزء الماهية أخفى من الكل وإلا لا يمكن معرفة الكل أي الماهية كما هي بدون ذلك الجزء، وذلك لا يمكن. ولا يقال لو كانت الماهية مفتقرة في الوجود إلى الأجزاء لكانت مفتقرة في الوجود إلى نفسها، وحينئذ يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه؛ إذ الفرق بين بين الكل وكل واحد ، وقد كان الكل عبارة عن المجموع بخلاف كل واحد. فالذي له ماهية مركبة فذلك لا يمكن أن يتحقق موجودًا في الأعيان أو متصورًا في الأذهان إلا وأن تكون أجزاؤه حاضرة معه. فإن كانت حقيقته غير كونه موجودًا أحد الوجودين، أعني الوجود الذهني أو الخارجي، فالوجود من المعاني المضافة إلى حقيقته مثل الإنسانية فإنها في نفسها حقيقة ما، والوجود خارج عنها فلا يكون وجودها في الخارج مقوما لها، ولا في الذهن كذلك ؛ إذ لو كان الوجود مقوما لها لاستحال تصور الإنسانية خارجة عن هذا الجزء وهو الوجود، فجميع مقومات الماهية داخلة مع الماهية في التصور إما
Chapter: On Quiddity. As for quiddity, it may be composite or not, but be simple. The composite is that which divides in its essence into parts of different natures, and the simple is in contrast to it necessarily, so it is that which does not divide into those parts. Then, if the composite is actualized, the simples are necessarily actualized; otherwise an infinite regress follows, not once but infinite times. And the simples are the parts of the quiddity when the quiddity is composed of them. As for the parts, they may be from the simples or not, but be from composites, wholly or partially. Then the parts are finite; otherwise they would not be intelligible. And it is not possible that some of them be privative unless the quiddity is a considative quiddity like the blind, for in it there is lack of sight from what is apt to see. And it is not possible that a part admits intensity and weakness; otherwise every quiddity would admit intensity and weakness, and that is impossible. For what is other than the quiddity is either considered in the quiddity, in which case the quiddity cannot be considered without it; or not considered, in which case it can be considered without it, rather it is necessary for the quiddity to be what it is. And if it were like that, then the quiddity cannot admit intensity and weakness. And it is not possible that a part of the quiddity be more hidden than the whole; otherwise it would not be possible to know the whole, i.e., the quiddity as it is, without that part, and that is not possible. And do not say: if the quiddity were dependent in existence on the parts, then it would be dependent in existence on itself, and then the thing would precede itself; for there is a difference between the whole and each one, and the whole is the sum as opposed to each one. He who has a composite quiddity cannot be actualized existing in external reality or conceived in minds unless its parts are present with it. For if his essence is other than being existent in one of the two modes of existence, namely mental or external existence, then existence is among the meanings added to the essence, like humanity, for it is in itself a certain quiddity, and existence is outside it, so its existence in the external is not a constituent for it, nor in the mind likewise. For if existence were a constituent for it, it would be impossible to conceive humanity outside this part, namely existence. Thus all the constituents of the quiddity are included with the quiddity in conception, either...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of quiddity (mahiyya), beginning with the basic division into composite and simple. The author defines composite quiddity as that which divides into parts of different natures, while the simple does not. He then argues that if a composite quiddity actually exists, its simple parts must also exist simultaneously, otherwise an infinite regress would result. The parts themselves may be simple or composite. Importantly, the parts must be finite for the quiddity to be intelligible. The author also rules out privative parts unless the quiddity is a considative (i'itibari) one, such as blindness which involves the absence of sight. He further argues that no part can admit degree (intensity/weakness), as that would entail the whole quiddity admitting degree, which is impossible. The discussion then addresses the relationship between quiddity and its parts: if something is other than the quiddity, either it is considered part of it (in which case it cannot be omitted) or it is not (in which case it can be omitted). The author also responds to a potential objection: if the quiddity depends on its parts for existence, it would depend on itself, leading to self-precedence; he clarifies that the whole is a sum distinct from each part. Finally, he states that a composite quiddity cannot be actualized (in mind or external world) without its parts being present. He then distinguishes the quiddity from existence: existence (whether mental or external) is not a constituent of the essence, using humanity as an example. If existence were a constituent, we could not conceive humanity without existence. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that existence is part of the quiddity's definition.

Key Terms
الماهية
مركبة
بسيطة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the defining characteristic of a composite quiddity?
A) It has parts that are not intelligible.
B) It divides in its essence into parts of different natures.
C) It is the same as simple quiddity.
D) It cannot be conceived without existence.

Key Takeaway
Quiddity is either composite (divisible into parts of different natures) or simple (not so divisible).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Parts Must Be Finite and Non-Privative
Author argues parts must be finite and non-privative

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that parts of a composite quiddity must be finite and cannot be privative, and apply it to examples from Islamic ontology.

Core Idea
Parts of a quiddity are finite (otherwise unintelligible) and cannot be privative (otherwise the quiddity is merely consderative).

The passage continues with important constraints on the parts of composite quiddities: they must be finite and cannot be privative (i.e., mere absences). The author argues that infinite parts would make the quiddity unintelligible, and a privative part would turn the quiddity into a mere mental consideration (i'tibārī). These constraints are foundational for the Islamic metaphysical view that real essences have positive, finite constituents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about finiteness
The author states: 'Then the parts are finite; otherwise they would not be intelligible.'
This is a direct assertion with a conditional justification.
2
Reconstruct the implicit argument for finiteness
Assume parts are infinite. Then the quiddity cannot be fully grasped (intelligible). But quiddities are intelligible (they are objects of knowledge). Therefore parts are finite.
This is a modus tollens argument: if infinite then not intelligible; but it is intelligible; so not infinite.
3
Analyse the condition about privative parts
The author says a part cannot be privative unless the quiddity is 'considative' like blindness, which involves absence of sight in something that should have sight. That makes the quiddity not fully real.
Privative = non-existent (ʿadamī). Real quiddities have positive parts.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the quiddity of 'knowledge': it has parts like 'conception' and 'assent' – both positive. If one part were 'ignorance' (absence), the quiddity would be i'tibārī, not a real essence.

Existence Is Not a Constituent of Quiddity
Author argues existence is not a constituent of quiddity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why existence (mental or external) is not a constituent (muqawwim) of the quiddity, and apply this distinction to the example of humanity.

Core Idea
Existence is an accident added to the essence, not part of its definition.

In the final part of the passage (as far as preserved), the author addresses a crucial ontological issue: whether existence is part of the quiddity's definition. He argues that existence – whether mental or external – is not a constituent (muqawwim) of the essence. Using the example of humanity (insāniyya), he explains that we can conceive of humanity without conceiving its existence, proving existence is an added meaning. This distinction is a hallmark of Avicennan metaphysics and essential for later Islamic theological debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about existence
The author states: 'existence is among the meanings added to the essence', and 'not a constituent'.
The claim directly addresses the relationship between essence and existence.
2
Extract the example of humanity
Humanity is a quiddity in itself; existence is outside it. If existence were a constituent, we could not conceive humanity without existence. But we can.
The example tests the claim: can we think of 'humanity' without thinking of whether it exists? Yes.
3
Reconstruct the logical argument
If existence were a constituent of the quiddity, then any conception of the quiddity would require conceiving existence. However, we can conceive the quiddity without existence (e.g., humanity as a mere essence). Therefore, existence is not a constituent. This is modus tollens.
This argument assumes conceivability implies non-necessity.
4
Contrast with the opposite view
Some philosophers (like Mullā Ṣadrā) argue existence is primary. The passage represents the Peripatetic position.
This step places the claim in its historical context.

فصل : [في الماهية ] أما الماهية فإنه قد تكون مركبة وقد لا تكون بل تكون بسيطة. والمركبة عبارة عما ينقسم في ذاته إلى أقسام مختلفة الطباع، والبسيطة في مقابلتها لا محالة فتكون عبارة عما لا ينقسم إلى تلك الأقسام. ثم المركبة إذا كانت متحققة كانت البسائط متحققة بالضرورة، وإلا يلزم التسلسل لا مرة واحدة؛ بل مرارًا غير متناهية . وإنها أي البسائط أجزاء الماهية لما كانت الماهية مركبة منها. فأما الأجزاء فإنها قد تكون من البسائط وقد لا تكون بل تكون من المركبات كلا أو بعضًا. ثم الأجزاء متناهية وإلا لا تكون معقولة ولا يمكن أن يكون البعض منها عدميًّا إلا وأن تكون الماهية ماهية اعتبارية كالأعمى، فإن فيه عدم البصر عما من شأنه أن يبصر . ولا يمكن أن يكون الجزء قابلا للأشد والأضعف، وإلا لكانت الماهية أي ماهية كانت قابلة للأشد والأضعف كذلك، وذلك محال. فإن ما هو الزائل عن الماهية ، فذلك إما أن يكون معتبراً في الماهية وحينئذ لا يمكن اعتبار الماهية بدون ذلك؛ أو لا يكون معتبراً، وحينئذ يمكن اعتبارها بدونه بل يلزم في كون الماهية ماهية بالضرورة. ولو كان كذلك فلا يمكن أن تكون الماهية قابلة للأشد والأضعف، ولا يمكن أن يكون جزء الماهية أخفى من الكل وإلا لا يمكن معرفة الكل أي الماهية كما هي بدون ذلك الجزء، وذلك لا يمكن. ولا يقال لو كانت الماهية مفتقرة في الوجود إلى الأجزاء لكانت مفتقرة في الوجود إلى نفسها، وحينئذ يلزم تقدم الشيء على نفسه؛ إذ الفرق بين بين الكل وكل واحد ، وقد كان الكل عبارة عن المجموع بخلاف كل واحد. فالذي له ماهية مركبة فذلك لا يمكن أن يتحقق موجودًا في الأعيان أو متصورًا في الأذهان إلا وأن تكون أجزاؤه حاضرة معه. فإن كانت حقيقته غير كونه موجودًا أحد الوجودين، أعني الوجود الذهني أو الخارجي، فالوجود من المعاني المضافة إلى حقيقته مثل الإنسانية فإنها في نفسها حقيقة ما، والوجود خارج عنها فلا يكون وجودها في الخارج مقوما لها، ولا في الذهن كذلك ؛ إذ لو كان الوجود مقوما لها لاستحال تصور الإنسانية خارجة عن هذا الجزء وهو الوجود، فجميع مقومات الماهية داخلة مع الماهية في التصور إما
Chapter: On Quiddity. As for quiddity, it may be composite or not, but be simple. The composite is that which divides in its essence into parts of different natures, and the simple is in contrast to it necessarily, so it is that which does not divide into those parts. Then, if the composite is actualized, the simples are necessarily actualized; otherwise an infinite regress follows, not once but infinite times. And the simples are the parts of the quiddity when the quiddity is composed of them. As for the parts, they may be from the simples or not, but be from composites, wholly or partially. Then the parts are finite; otherwise they would not be intelligible. And it is not possible that some of them be privative unless the quiddity is a considative quiddity like the blind, for in it there is lack of sight from what is apt to see. And it is not possible that a part admits intensity and weakness; otherwise every quiddity would admit intensity and weakness, and that is impossible. For what is other than the quiddity is either considered in the quiddity, in which case the quiddity cannot be considered without it; or not considered, in which case it can be considered without it, rather it is necessary for the quiddity to be what it is. And if it were like that, then the quiddity cannot admit intensity and weakness. And it is not possible that a part of the quiddity be more hidden than the whole; otherwise it would not be possible to know the whole, i.e., the quiddity as it is, without that part, and that is not possible. And do not say: if the quiddity were dependent in existence on the parts, then it would be dependent in existence on itself, and then the thing would precede itself; for there is a difference between the whole and each one, and the whole is the sum as opposed to each one. He who has a composite quiddity cannot be actualized existing in external reality or conceived in minds unless its parts are present with it. For if his essence is other than being existent in one of the two modes of existence, namely mental or external existence, then existence is among the meanings added to the essence, like humanity, for it is in itself a certain quiddity, and existence is outside it, so its existence in the external is not a constituent for it, nor in the mind likewise. For if existence were a constituent for it, it would be impossible to conceive humanity outside this part, namely existence. Thus all the constituents of the quiddity are included with the quiddity in conception, either...
Source pages p. 131

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition of quiddity (mahiyya), beginning with the basic division into composite and simple. The author defines composite quiddity as that which divides into parts of different natures, while the simple does not. He then argues that if a composite quiddity actually exists, its simple parts must also exist simultaneously, otherwise an infinite regress would result. The parts themselves may be simple or composite. Importantly, the parts must be finite for the quiddity to be intelligible. The author also rules out privative parts unless the quiddity is a considative (i'itibari) one, such as blindness which involves the absence of sight. He further argues that no part can admit degree (intensity/weakness), as that would entail the whole quiddity admitting degree, which is impossible. The discussion then addresses the relationship between quiddity and its parts: if something is other than the quiddity, either it is considered part of it (in which case it cannot be omitted) or it is not (in which case it can be omitted). The author also responds to a potential objection: if the quiddity depends on its parts for existence, it would depend on itself, leading to self-precedence; he clarifies that the whole is a sum distinct from each part. Finally, he states that a composite quiddity cannot be actualized (in mind or external world) without its parts being present. He then distinguishes the quiddity from existence: existence (whether mental or external) is not a constituent of the essence, using humanity as an example. If existence were a constituent, we could not conceive humanity without existence. This passage guards against the misunderstanding that existence is part of the quiddity's definition.

Key Terms
الوجود
الوجود الذهني
الوجود الخارجي
مقوم
إنسانية

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is existence not a constituent of the quiddity of humanity?
A) Because humanity cannot exist without existence, so existence must be added.
B) Because if existence were a constituent, we could not conceive humanity without it; but we can.
C) Because the author says so without argument.
D) Because existence is external only, not mental.

Key Takeaway
Existence is an added meaning to the quiddity, not a part of its definition, as shown by the conceivability of the essence without existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the author's method, test whether 'existence' is a constituent of the quiddity of 'angel'. Write a short argument: can you conceive of an angel's essence without its existence? If yes, what does that imply?
?
Explain how the example of humanity supports the claim. Be sure to state the conditional and the modus tollens form clearly.

Essential vs Accidental Attributes
Distinguishing essential from accidental attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any attribute as essential (dhātī) or accidental (ʿaraḍ) using the criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
Essential attributes constitute the quiddity; accidents attach only after its realization.

In this passage, the author clarifies a foundational distinction in logic: which attributes truly belong to a thing's essence and which are merely accompanying. This distinction is crucial for constructing correct definitions (ḥadd) and avoiding fallacies in reasoning. Let's parse the criteria.

Guided Path
1
Identify the quiddity under discussion
Determine the thing (e.g., three, animal) whose attributes you are evaluating. The passage uses 'three' and 'animal' as examples.
2
Determine the relationship of the attribute to the quiddity
Is the attribute more general (aʿamm), more specific (akhaṣṣ), or equal (musāwī) to the quiddity? For example, 'movement' is more general than 'animal'; 'parity' is more general than 'three'.
3
Check if the attribute enters the quiddity by itself
Does the attribute attach to the quiddity purely by virtue of its essence (bi-dhātihi), or does it come after the quiddity is realized? The passage states: essential attributes are those that enter the quiddity, not those that are merely more general or more specific without being constituents.
4
Classify as essential or accidental
If the attribute enters the quiddity, it is essential (dhātī). If it attaches only after the quiddity is realized (even if necessarily), it is accidental (ʿaraḍ). Example: parity is essential to three; movement is accidental to animal.
5
Test with examples from the passage
Apply the criteria to the attributes mentioned: movement (for animal) is accidental; thought (for animal) is accidental; parity (for three) is essential; unity (for existent) is essential. Confirm the author's classification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بالفعل وإما بالقوة القريبة من الفعل، وإن لم يخطر بالبال ما هو منها مفصلًا. وتلك المقومات هي الذاتيات لما إن الذاتي بحسب هذا الوضع هو الذي يدخل في الماهية على خلاف ما يكون في غير هذا الوضع، وهو الذي يدخل الموضوع في ماهيته كما في كتاب البرهان. وذلك يكون للماهية لا الأمر أعم كالتحرك للحيوان، ولا الأمر أخص كالتفكر له كذلك بل الوصف الذي يلحق الماهية لذاتها. وذلك هو الأعم كالفردية للثلاثة أو الأخص كالوحدة للموجود، والكثرة له كذلك. فإنه لذاته يقتضي الاتصاف بأحدهما لا بعينه، أو المساوي كالاختيار للممكن مثلا، وما يلحق الماهية لا لذاتها من الأوصاف فذلك لما هو الأعم منها كامتناع التداخل في الأجسام بواسطة الأوضاع، أو لما هو الأخص المتحرك بالفعل بواسطة التعجب أو لما هو المساوي لها المتحرك بالقوة ونحو ذلك. ولا يستراب في أن ما يلحق الماهية من الأوصاف التي يسمونها أعراضًا، فذلك إنما يلحق الماهية بعد تحققها خاصًا كان ذلك العرض أو عاما، لازمًا كان أو مفارقًا، لحوق المثلّث بعد التقوم بأضلاعه؛ إذ لو كانت هي وأمثالها مقومات لكان المثلث وما يجري مجراه مركباً من مقومات غير متناهية. فلو كان لزومها بغير وسط لكانت معلومة واجبة اللزوم فكانت ممتنعة الرفع وإن كان بوسط فكذلك فإنه أي الوسط هو اللازم البين بلا وسط وإلا يلزم التسلسل مقوما كان ذلك الوسط أو لم يكن مقوما. فإذن لا بد وأن يكون من اللوازم إما بوسط كما مر أو لا بوسط، ولا يقال لا يكون ذلك الوسط لازما بينا إلا وأن يكون العلم بالماهية علة العلم بلازمها القريب، وذلك ممتنع وإلا لكانت اللوازم بأسرها معلومة بينة اللزوم، فإن يكون قريبا من اللوازم فذلك لا يكون بين اللزوم لملزومه إلا وأن يحضر في الذهن، وذلك يعرف بالتأمل. ثم العلم بالماهية بسيطة كانت الماهية أو مركبة قد يكون بحسب ذاتها وقد لا يكون بل يكون بحسب ما يتعلق بها. فتكون معلومة إذن: إما بذاتها وإما بتوابعها وصفاتها. فإنا إذا علمنا الحرارة بالحس مثلا، فذلك العلم بالشيء من حيث حقيقته المخصوصة وماهيته المعينة وهو كمال درجات العلم عند الأكثر. فأما إذا علمنا أن العالم حادث بالدلائل الدالة عليه فلا نعلم أن المحدث ما هو وإن كان معلوماً بأن العالم حادث بإحداثه. ولو كان كذلك لكان المطلوب في السؤال عن الماهية البسيطة: إما هو الأول منهما كما إذا سئل عن الحرارة فيقال ما يدركه الإنسان بحس اللمس عند مماسة جرم النار وإما السؤال الثاني كما إذا سئل عن المحدث فيقال المحدث هو الذي لأجله انتقل الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود. وعلى هذا في المركب؛ إذ المطلوب إما هو الأول منهما أو الثاني، وذلك يعرف بتمامه في التعريفات الحدية والرسمية من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى.
Either in actuality or in potency near to actuality, even if what is detailed of it does not occur to the mind. Those constituents are the essential attributes, for the essential according to this usage is that which enters into the quiddity, contrary to what is in other usages, and it is that which enters the subject into its quiddity as in the Book of Demonstration. That is for the quiddity, not the more general like movement for animal, nor the more specific like thought for it, but rather the attribute that attaches to the quiddity by itself. That is either the more general like parity for three, or the more specific like unity for existent, and multiplicity for it likewise; for it by itself necessitates being described by one of them not specifically, or the equal like choice for the possible, for example. And what attaches to the quiddity not by itself among the attributes, that is for what is more general than it, like impossibility of interpenetration in bodies by means of positions, or for what is more specific, like being actually moving by means of wonder, or for what is equal to it, like being potentially moving, and the like. There is no doubt that what attaches to the quiddity from among the attributes which they call accidents—that only attaches to the quiddity after its realization, whether that accident is specific or general, necessary or separable, like the attachment of triangle after its constitution by its sides; for if they and their like were constituents, then triangle and what proceeds in its course would be composed of infinite constituents. So if their necessity were without a middle term, they would be known and necessary of necessity, so they would be impossible to remove; and if with a middle term, likewise, for that middle is the necessary evident without a middle term, otherwise an infinite regress would follow, whether that middle is a constituent or not. Thus it is necessary that among the necessary concomitants there be either with a middle as mentioned or without a middle, and it cannot be said that that middle is not a necessary evident without the knowledge of the quiddity being the cause of knowledge of its proximate necessary concomitant, and that is impossible, otherwise all necessary concomitants would be known and evident of necessity. For if it is a proximate one of the necessary concomitants, that one is not evident in necessity to its necessitated except when it is present in the mind, and that is known by reflection. Then knowledge of the quiddity, whether the quiddity is simple or composite, may be according to itself and may not be, but rather according to what pertains to it. So it is known either by itself or by its consequences and attributes. For if we know heat by sensation, for example, that is knowledge of the thing in terms of its specific reality and particular quiddity, and it is the perfection of degrees of knowledge according to most. But if we know that the world is originated by proofs indicating it, we do not know what the originator is, even if it is known that the world is originated by His bringing it into existence. And if that were the case, then what is sought in the question about the simple quiddity is either the first of the two, as when asked about heat it is said 'what humans perceive by the sense of touch when touching a fiery body', or the second question, as when asked about the originator it is said 'the originator is that because of which something passed from non-existence to existence'. And similarly for the composite, for what is sought is either the first of the two or the second; that is known in full in the definitional and descriptive definitions later, God willing.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage discusses the distinction between essential (dhātī) and accidental (ʿaraḍ) attributes and how quiddity (māhiyya) is known. The author begins by defining the essential attribute as that which enters the quiddity, as opposed to other uses where it may enter the subject's quiddity. Essential attributes are not more general than the quiddity (like movement for animal) nor more specific (like thought for animal), but rather those that attach to the quiddity by itself. These can be more general (like parity for three), more specific (like unity for existent), or equal (like choice for the possible). Attributes that attach to the quiddity not by itself are accidents, which attach after the quiddity is realized, whether necessary or separable. The example of triangle shows that if such attributes were constituents, there would be infinite regress. The author then discusses the necessity of concomitants (lawāzim): they must be either immediately evident or via a middle term, otherwise infinite regress. Knowledge of the quiddity may be by itself (e.g., knowing heat by sensation) or by its consequences (e.g., knowing the world is created without knowing the creator's essence). The passage concludes by stating that the full treatment of definitional and descriptive definitions will come later. This passage is careful to distinguish between what is essential to a thing's quiddity and what is accidental, a foundational point in logic for evaluating definitions.

Key Terms
الذاتي (al-dhātī): That which enters into the quiddity of a thing, as opposed to accidental (ʿaraḍī). [Note: In some later texts, al-dhātī can also refer to the subject's essence in predication; here it specifically means constituent of quiddity.]
الماهية (al-māhiyya): The essence or quiddity of a thing, that which constitutes its reality. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
العرض (al-ʿaraḍ): An attribute that attaches to the quiddity after its realization, not essential. [Note: In later ontology, ʿaraḍ may be divided into necessary (lāzim) and separable (mufāriq); here it includes both.]
اللازم (al-lāzim): A necessary concomitant of the quiddity, which may or may not be known through a middle term. [Note: Used in logical contexts to denote inseparable accidents; in later texts often tied to demonstration.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The movement of an animal
B) The thought of a human
C) Parity for the number three
D) The color of a body

Key Takeaway
Essential attributes constitute the quiddity; accidents attach only after its realization, whether necessary or separable.

Knowledge of Quiddity: Direct vs Inferential
Explaining how quiddity can be known.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between knowing a quiddity by itself (e.g., heat by sensation) and knowing it through its consequences (e.g., the originator through creation).

Core Idea
Quiddity is known either directly by its reality or indirectly through its attributes and effects.

The passage shifts from attributes to epistemology: how do we grasp a thing's essence? The author presents two modes: direct acquaintance (e.g., sensing heat) and proof-based inference (e.g., knowing the world's creation implies a creator, but not his essence). This distinction is vital for understanding the limits of human knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify whether the quiddity is simple or composite
The passage explicitly mentions simple (basīṭa) and composite (murakkaba) quiddities. Determine which you are dealing with; this affects how it is known.
2
Ascertain the source of knowledge
Is the knowledge derived from direct perception (sensation, intuition) or from indirect evidence (proofs, effects)? The passage uses heat (sensation) and the world's creation (proof) as examples.
3
Classify the mode of knowledge
If direct, you know the quiddity by itself (bi-dhātihā) – this is the highest degree. If indirect, you know it by its attributes (ṣifāt) or effects (āthār).
4
Apply to the examples given
For heat: we know it by sensation (direct). For the originator (al-muḥdith): we know that the world is created, but we do not know His essence directly – only through His act.

بذاتها وإما بتوابعها وصفاتها. فإنا إذا علمنا الحرارة بالحس مثلا، فذلك العلم بالشيء من حيث حقيقته المخصوصة وماهيته المعينة وهو كمال درجات العلم عند الأكثر. فأما إذا علمنا أن العالم حادث بالدلائل الدالة عليه فلا نعلم أن المحدث ما هو وإن كان معلوماً بأن العالم حادث بإحداثه. ولو كان كذلك لكان المطلوب في السؤال عن الماهية البسيطة: إما هو الأول منهما كما إذا سئل عن الحرارة فيقال ما يدركه الإنسان بحس اللمس عند مماسة جرم النار وإما السؤال الثاني كما إذا سئل عن المحدث فيقال المحدث هو الذي لأجله انتقل الشيء من العدم إلى الوجود. وعلى هذا في المركب؛ إذ المطلوب إما هو الأول منهما أو الثاني، وذلك يعرف بتمامه في التعريفات الحدية والرسمية من بعد إن شاء الله تعالى.
Either in actuality or in potency near to actuality, even if what is detailed of it does not occur to the mind. Those constituents are the essential attributes, for the essential according to this usage is that which enters into the quiddity, contrary to what is in other usages, and it is that which enters the subject into its quiddity as in the Book of Demonstration. That is for the quiddity, not the more general like movement for animal, nor the more specific like thought for it, but rather the attribute that attaches to the quiddity by itself. That is either the more general like parity for three, or the more specific like unity for existent, and multiplicity for it likewise; for it by itself necessitates being described by one of them not specifically, or the equal like choice for the possible, for example. And what attaches to the quiddity not by itself among the attributes, that is for what is more general than it, like impossibility of interpenetration in bodies by means of positions, or for what is more specific, like being actually moving by means of wonder, or for what is equal to it, like being potentially moving, and the like. There is no doubt that what attaches to the quiddity from among the attributes which they call accidents—that only attaches to the quiddity after its realization, whether that accident is specific or general, necessary or separable, like the attachment of triangle after its constitution by its sides; for if they and their like were constituents, then triangle and what proceeds in its course would be composed of infinite constituents. So if their necessity were without a middle term, they would be known and necessary of necessity, so they would be impossible to remove; and if with a middle term, likewise, for that middle is the necessary evident without a middle term, otherwise an infinite regress would follow, whether that middle is a constituent or not. Thus it is necessary that among the necessary concomitants there be either with a middle as mentioned or without a middle, and it cannot be said that that middle is not a necessary evident without the knowledge of the quiddity being the cause of knowledge of its proximate necessary concomitant, and that is impossible, otherwise all necessary concomitants would be known and evident of necessity. For if it is a proximate one of the necessary concomitants, that one is not evident in necessity to its necessitated except when it is present in the mind, and that is known by reflection. Then knowledge of the quiddity, whether the quiddity is simple or composite, may be according to itself and may not be, but rather according to what pertains to it. So it is known either by itself or by its consequences and attributes. For if we know heat by sensation, for example, that is knowledge of the thing in terms of its specific reality and particular quiddity, and it is the perfection of degrees of knowledge according to most. But if we know that the world is originated by proofs indicating it, we do not know what the originator is, even if it is known that the world is originated by His bringing it into existence. And if that were the case, then what is sought in the question about the simple quiddity is either the first of the two, as when asked about heat it is said 'what humans perceive by the sense of touch when touching a fiery body', or the second question, as when asked about the originator it is said 'the originator is that because of which something passed from non-existence to existence'. And similarly for the composite, for what is sought is either the first of the two or the second; that is known in full in the definitional and descriptive definitions later, God willing.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage discusses the distinction between essential (dhātī) and accidental (ʿaraḍ) attributes and how quiddity (māhiyya) is known. The author begins by defining the essential attribute as that which enters the quiddity, as opposed to other uses where it may enter the subject's quiddity. Essential attributes are not more general than the quiddity (like movement for animal) nor more specific (like thought for animal), but rather those that attach to the quiddity by itself. These can be more general (like parity for three), more specific (like unity for existent), or equal (like choice for the possible). Attributes that attach to the quiddity not by itself are accidents, which attach after the quiddity is realized, whether necessary or separable. The example of triangle shows that if such attributes were constituents, there would be infinite regress. The author then discusses the necessity of concomitants (lawāzim): they must be either immediately evident or via a middle term, otherwise infinite regress. Knowledge of the quiddity may be by itself (e.g., knowing heat by sensation) or by its consequences (e.g., knowing the world is created without knowing the creator's essence). The passage concludes by stating that the full treatment of definitional and descriptive definitions will come later. This passage is careful to distinguish between what is essential to a thing's quiddity and what is accidental, a foundational point in logic for evaluating definitions.

Key Terms
الماهية (al-māhiyya): The essence or quiddity of a thing, that which constitutes its reality. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
العلم (al-ʿilm): Knowledge, here distinguished as knowing the quiddity directly or indirectly. [Note: In this passage, 'ilm is used in a broad epistemological sense.]
بالذات (bi-dhātihā): By itself; direct knowledge of a thing's quiddity without mediation. [Note: Contrasted with 'by its consequences' (bi-tawābiʿihā).]
بالعرض (bil-ʿaraḍ): Accidentally; knowledge of a quiddity through its attributes or effects. [Note: Here it refers to indirect knowledge, not the attribute itself.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, knowledge of a simple quiddity like heat is attained by:
A) Its effects
B) Direct sensation
C) Both
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Quiddity may be known directly or through its necessary concomitants; only the first yields full knowledge of essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the term 'fire'. How can we know its quiddity? Use the passage's two modes: by sensation (heat, light) and by effects (smoke, ash). Then, for 'God', why can we not know His quiddity by itself? Explain using the passage.
