The Essential Definition (Hadd): Genus and Differentia
Author defines hadd as composed of genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a hadd (essential definition) for any composite essence by identifying its genus and differentia and verifying that it indicates the essence by conformity and implication.

Core Idea
Hadd reveals inner essence through genus and differentia.

In this passage, the author introduces the concept of hadd, the essential definition that lays bare the quiddity of a thing. Unlike a mere name or description, a hadd must be composed of the thing's essential attributes—its genus and differentia. Understanding this distinction is the first step to mastering logical definition, a tool indispensable in every Islamic science from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definiendum in the passage. The author gives 'human' (insan) as the example. What is being defined?
The definiendum is always a species or composite essence.
2
Extract the genus (جنس)
Find the broader category to which the thing belongs. In 'human', the genus is 'animal' (hayawan). The genus answers 'what is it?' in a general sense.
The genus must be a universal that includes the species.
3
Extract the differentia (فصل)
Find what distinguishes the species within the genus. For human, the differentia is 'rational' (natiq). The differentia narrows the genus to the exact species.
The differentia should be essential, not accidental.
4
Compose the hadd
Combine genus and differentia: 'rational animal'. This statement indicates the entire essence by conformity (mutabaqa) and its parts by implication (tadamun).
A complete hadd must include all essential attributes.
5
Test the definition's completeness
Check that the hadd applies to all instances of the species and only to them. Does 'rational animal' include all humans and exclude all non-humans? Verify using the passage's criteria.
A valid hadd is convertible with the definiendum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمسطور في البعض من الكتب أنه هو المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالحقائق في جواب ما هو ؟ والمعنى بهذا هو المعنى بذلك كذلك «حدا» كان كل واحد من هذين التعريفين كما ذهب إليه البعض، أو «رسما»، والمشهور أنه هو الرسم وقيل إن يشرع في شرح هذا التعريف يشير إلى معنى الحد والرسم إشارة حقيقة يفهم منها ما هو من اللوازم فهمه في هذا الموضع؛ ونؤخر بحقيقته إلى ما هو المختص به من المواضع فنقول إن الغرض الأول في التعريف هو الدلالة باللفظ على ماهية الشيء. فإن كانت الماهية معنى مفردًا فلن يصلح أن يدل على ذاته إلا بلفظ يتناول ذلك الذات وحده، ويكون هو اسمه لا غير. وربما أتى باسم مرادف يكون أكثر شرحًا له، لكن دلالة الاسم إذا لم يعد علما بمجهول احتاج إلى بيان آخر لا يتناول ذاته فقط؛ بل يتناول شيئًا ولواحق لذاته إذا فهمته تنبه الذهن حينئذ لمعناه مستقلاً منها إلى معناه، أو يقتصر على العلامات دون الماهية فلا ينتقل إليها وعلى ما أقرب إلى فهمك في هذا الوقت بمثل هذا الشيء لا حد له ؛ بل لفظ يشرح لواحقه من أعراضه ولوازمه. وإن كانت معنى مؤلفًا من معانٍ فله حد وهو القول الذي يؤلف من المعاني التي منها تحصل ماهيته. ولأن أخص الذاتيات بالشيء إما جنسه وإما فصله وإما فصل الفصل وجنس الجنس وما يتركب من ذلك، فهو له بواسطته وهو في ضمن الجنس والفصل فيجب أن يكون الحد مؤلفًا من الجنس والفصل، كما نقول في حد الإنسان حيوان ناطق. وإن لم يكن للجنس اسم أتى بحده وكذلك للفصل، فالحد بالجملة يشتمل على جميع المعاني الذاتية للشيء ويدل عليها بالمطابقة والتضمن. أما المطابقة فعلى المعنى الواحد المحصل من الجملة، وأما التضمن فعلى الأجزاء.

وأما «الرسم»، فإنما يتوخى فيه أن يؤلف قول من لواحق الشيء يساوي ذلك الشيء فيكون لجميع ما يدخل بحسب ذلك الشيء لا لشيء غيره حتى يدل عليه دلالة العلامة وأحسن أحواله أن يرتب فيه أو لا جنس، إما قريب وإما بعيد. مثال ذلك أن يقال إن الإنسان حيوان عريض الأظفار منصب القامة بادي البشرة ضحاك، وقد يقال من هذا المجموع ما عدا الحيوان.
And it is written in some books that it is the predicated of many differing in realities in answer to 'what is it?' And the meaning with this is the meaning with that likewise, either a 'hadd' as some held, or a 'rasm', and the well-known is that it is the rasm, and it is said: if one proceeds to explain this definition, it indicates the meaning of hadd and rasm with a true indication from which is understood what is necessary to understand in this place; and we postpone its reality to what is specific to it among places. So we say: the first purpose in definition is the indication by the word of the essence of the thing. If the essence is a simple meaning, then it will not be suitable to indicate its essence except by a word that encompasses that essence alone, and it is its name only. And perhaps a synonym comes that is more explanatory for it, but the indication of the name, if it is not a mark for something unknown, needs another statement that does not encompass only its essence, but encompasses something and concomitants of its essence; when you understand it, the mind then becomes aware of its meaning independently from them to its meaning, or it confines itself to the marks without the essence and thus does not transition to it. And on what is closer to your understanding at this time, such a thing has no hadd, but rather a word that explains its concomitants from its accidents and inseparable properties. And if it is a composite meaning from meanings, then it has a hadd, and it is the statement composed of the meanings from which its essence is obtained. And because the most specific of the essential attributes of a thing are either its genus or its differentia or the differentia of the differentia and the genus of the genus and what is composed from that, so it is for it by means of it and it is within the genus and differentia. So the hadd must be composed of the genus and differentia, as we say in the hadd of man: 'rational animal'. And if the genus has no name, its hadd is brought, and likewise for the differentia. So the hadd in sum includes all the essential meanings of the thing and indicates them by conformity and implication. As for conformity, it indicates the single meaning obtained from the whole; as for implication, it indicates the parts. 

As for the 'rasm', it aims to compose a statement from the concomitants of the thing that are equal to that thing, so that it is for everything that enters under that thing and not for anything else, so that it indicates it as a mark. The best of its states is that a genus is arranged in it, either proximate or remote. An example of that is that it is said: 'Man is an animal with broad nails, upright stature, bare skin, laughing.' And sometimes what is other than animal is said from this collection.
Source pages p. 138

Commentary
This passage defines two types of definition: hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition). The author begins by noting that some books define the subject (presumably genus) as 'the predicated of many differing in realities in answer to 'what is it?'' and that this definition can be taken as a hadd or a rasm, though the well-known view is that it is a rasm. He postpones full discussion of hadd and rasm to later. He then states that the primary purpose of definition is to indicate the essence (mahiyya) of a thing by a word. If the essence is simple, it can only be indicated by its own name; a synonym may be more explanatory but still requires further clarification. For a composite essence, a hadd is given, which is a statement composed of the meanings from which the essence is formed. Since the most specific essential attributes are genus, differentia, and their combinations, the hadd must be composed of genus and differentia, as in the hadd of 'human': 'rational animal'. If the genus or differentia has no common name, their definitions are given. The hadd indicates the essence by conformity (mutabaqa) to the whole and by implication (tadamun) to the parts. The rasm, by contrast, aims to compose a statement from the concomitants (lawahiq) of the thing that are equal to it, serving as a distinguishing mark. The best rasm includes a genus, either proximate or remote. Example: 'Man is an animal with broad nails, upright stature, bare skin, laughing.' Sometimes the rasm is given without the genus. This distinction is fundamental: hadd reveals the inner essence, while rasm identifies the thing through its accidents and properties.

Key Terms
الحد
الماهية
الجنس
الفصل
المطابقة
التضمن

A) Genus alone
B) Differentia alone
C) Genus and differentia
D) Accidental concomitants

Key Takeaway
A hadd (essential definition) is a composite statement of genus and differentia that indicates the essence of a thing by conformity and implication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a hadd for 'sawm' (fasting in Ramadan) using genus and differentia. Identify a suitable genus (e.g., 'act of worship') and differentia (e.g., 'abstention from food, drink, and marital relations from dawn to sunset with intention'). Ensure your definition is convertible.
?
Examine the following definition of 'zakat': 'a specific portion of wealth given to specific recipients at a specific time'. Is this a hadd or a rasm? Justify your answer using the passage's criteria.

Hadd and Rasm

The Descriptive Definition (Rasm): Marking through Accidents
Author defines rasm as a statement of equal accidental concomitants.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish rasm from hadd and construct a valid rasm for any thing using its accidental concomitants that are coextensive with it.

Core Idea
Rasm identifies through marks, not essence.

While hadd reveals the inner reality of a thing, rasm provides a descriptive identification through external signs. The author explains that rasm aims to compose a statement from the concomitants (lawahiq) that are equal to the thing. This is especially useful when the essence is unknown or too abstract. In Islamic sciences, rasm is often used in fiqh and kalam to define complex concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thing to be described
Pick a thing that needs a distinguishing mark. The author uses 'human' again for rasm. What are we describing?
Rasm can be used for both simple and composite essences.
2
List the accidental concomitants (لواحق)
Find features that are always present with the thing but not part of its essence. For humans: 'broad nails, upright stature, bare skin, laughing.' These are accidents, not essentials.
The concomitants must be inseparable (lazim) but not essential (dhati).
3
Ensure the list is coextensive (مساوٍ)
Check that the set of concomitants applies to every instance of the thing and only to it. 'Broad nails' alone may not be unique; the combination with other features creates uniqueness.
Coextensiveness is crucial: the rasm must equal the thing in extension.
4
Include a genus (جنس) if possible
The author says the best rasm includes a genus, e.g., 'animal' for human. This places the thing in a broader category, making the description clearer.
If no genus is given, the rasm may still be valid but less informative.
5
Distinguish rasm from hadd
A hadd uses essential attributes (genus and differentia) to reveal the essence, while a rasm uses accidental attributes to mark the thing. In the passage, the hadd for human is 'rational animal'; the rasm is 'animal with broad nails, upright stature, bare skin, laughing'.
The same thing can have both a hadd and a rasm, serving different purposes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Construct a rasm for 'dates' (tamr) using accidental concomitants. Include a genus if possible. Ensure your list is coextensive with dates.
?
Compare the hadd and rasm of 'human' from the passage. Explain why 'rational animal' is a hadd and 'animal with broad nails, upright stature, bare skin, laughing' is a rasm. Which one reveals the essence?

Understanding al-Rasm: Descriptive Definition
Author defines al-rasm and provides an example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a rasm (description) and distinguish it from a hadd (true definition) by analyzing its components.

Core Idea
A rasm describes by accidental properties; it points, but does not reveal essence.

In Islamic logic, definitions are of two types: hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition). While hadd captures the essence, rasm uses accidental yet distinctive properties to identify a thing. This lesson unpacks the nature of rasm, its components, and its purpose as a distinguishing sign, using the classic example of man.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the definiendum in the passage—here, 'man' (al-insān). The rasm is built around this term.
The example given is the classical description of man.
2
Extract the components of the rasm
List all the properties mentioned: 'animal' (genus), 'laughing', 'flat-nailed', 'erect in stature', 'exposed skin'. Note that 'animal' is the genus and the rest are accidental properties (lawāḥiq).
The author says the best rasm includes a genus, but it's not required.
3
Check the condition of equality (musāwāt)
Determine if the rasm applies to all instances of the thing and only to those instances. For 'laughing animal', does it exclude non-humans? Yes, only humans laugh. Does it include all humans? Yes, all humans have the potential to laugh.
The properties must be peculiar to the thing (khāṣṣah) to serve as a sign.
4
Compare rasm with hadd
A hadd uses essential differentia (e.g., 'rational' for man), while a rasm uses accidental ones. Ask: does this definition reveal the essence? No, it only distinguishes. That is the key difference.
Rasm is a 'sign' (ʿalāmah) not an explanation of quiddity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما «الرسم»، فإنما يتوخى فيه أن يؤلف قول من لواحق الشيء يساوي ذلك الشيء فيكون لجميع ما يدخل بحسب ذلك الشيء لا لشيء غيره حتى يدل عليه دلالة العلامة وأحسن أحواله أن يرتب فيه أو لا جنس، إما قريب وإما بعيد. مثال ذلك أن يقال إن الإنسان حيوان عريض الأظفار منصب القامة بادي البشرة ضحاك، وقد يقال من هذا المجموع ما عدا الحيوان.
And as for the description, what is sought in it is that a statement is composed of the concomitants of the thing that are equal to that thing, so that it applies to everything that enters according to that thing and not to anything else, such that it indicates it by the indication of a sign. The best of its states is that a genus is arranged in it, either close or distant. An example of that is that it is said: 'Man is a laughing animal, flat-nailed, erect in stature, with exposed skin.' And part of this aggregate may be said apart from 'animal'.
Source pages p. 139

Commentary
This passage defines al-rasm, which is a descriptive definition as opposed to a true definition (hadd). The author explains that a rasm is composed of accidental properties (lawāḥiq) that are peculiar to the thing (musāwī lahu). Its purpose is to serve as a distinctive sign, not to reveal the essence. The best rasm includes a genus (jins), either proximate or remote, but it is not required. The example given is the classical definition of man as 'a laughing animal, flat-nailed, erect in stature, with exposed skin.' This includes 'animal' as the genus and the other properties as differentiae that are not essential but distinctive. The phrase 'may be said apart from animal' indicates that some consider the aggregate without the genus also a valid rasm.

Key Takeaway
A rasm is a descriptive definition using accidental yet distinctive properties to identify a thing without revealing its essence.

Generic vs. Specific Nature: The Role of Condition
Author defines 'nature' and distinguishes generic from specific nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish generic nature from specific nature and identify when generic nature serves as a material cause for specific nature, using the concepts of bi-sharṭ and lā bi-sharṭ.

Core Idea
Generic nature alone causes nothing; it only matters when conditioned.

This passage introduces a crucial ontological distinction: generic nature (e.g., animality) and specific nature (e.g., humanity) are not the same, and the former does not automatically produce the latter. But when taken with a particular condition—excluding the differentia—generic nature becomes a material cause. Understanding this distinction will sharpen your ability to analyze definitions and causal claims in Islamic philosophy and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two natures
Locate the terms 'generic nature' (الطبيعة الجنسية) and 'specific nature' (الطبيعة النوعية) in the Arabic. Notice that the author asserts they are necessarily different (غير الطبيعة النوعية لا محالة).
Generic nature = the genus-level essence (e.g., animality); specific nature = the species-level essence (e.g., humanity).
2
Understand the causal claim
Read the line: 'الطبيعة الجنسية من حيث هي الطبيعة الجنسية لا تكون سببًا لوجود الطبيعة النوعية البتة.' Generic nature as such is not at all a cause for specific nature. This is a clear negation.
3
Examine the conditional mode
Now see the exception: 'إذا أخذت بمعنى المادة كما إذا أخذت الطبيعة الحيوانية بشرط أن لا تدخل فيها الناطقية، فإنها تكون سببًا ما.' When generic nature is taken as matter (mādda) with the condition (bi-sharṭ) that the differentia is excluded, it becomes a cause.
Example: animality, when the condition 'without rationality' is added, becomes the material substrate for humanity.
4
Contrast the absolute mode
Finally, note the phrase 'لا بشرط' (without condition): generic nature taken absolutely, neither including nor excluding the differentia. This is nature in itself, not a cause.
This threefold distinction (as such, with condition, without condition) is a key analytical tool in later Islamic philosophy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في الطبيعة] وأما «الطبيعة» فقد تعرف أنها قوة والطبيعة الجنسية غير الطبيعة النوعية لا محالة. وإنها أي الطبيعة الجنسية من حيث هي الطبيعة الجنسية لا تكون سببًا لوجود الطبيعة النوعية البتة. فأما إذا أخذت بمعنى المادة كما إذا أخذت الطبيعة الحيوانية بشرط أن لا تدخل فيها الناطقية، فإنها تكون سببًا ما لوجود الطبيعة الإنسانية. فإن من المحال أن يوجد الإنسان بدونها فالطبيعة الجنسية وهي التي أخذت لا بشرط أن تدخل الناطقية أو لا تدخل، فهي لذاتها ذات
Section: [On Nature]. And as for 'nature', it is sometimes defined as a power. And generic nature is necessarily other than specific nature. And it—i.e., generic nature, inasmuch as it is generic nature—is not at all a cause for the existence of specific nature. But if it is taken in the meaning of matter, as when animal nature is taken with the condition that rationality does not enter into it, then it is some cause for the existence of human nature. For it is impossible that man exist without it. So generic nature—and it is that which is taken without the condition that rationality enters or does not enter—it is in itself ...
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage opens a new section titled 'On Nature' (fī al-ṭabīʿa). The author first notes that 'nature' is sometimes defined as a power (quwwa). Then the key distinction is drawn: generic nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-jinsiyya) is necessarily different from specific nature (al-ṭabīʿa al-nawʿiyya). Generic nature, considered purely as generic nature, is not at all a cause for the existence of specific nature. However, if generic nature is taken in the sense of matter (mādda)—for example, animal nature taken with the condition (bi-sharṭ) that rationality (al-nāṭiqiyya) does not enter into it—then it does serve as some cause for the existence of human nature. This is because it is impossible for man to exist without animal nature. Finally, the author distinguishes generic nature taken 'without the condition' (lā bi-sharṭ) that rationality enters or does not enter; such nature is considered in itself (li-dhātihā). The passage breaks off here, but the argument contrasts three modes: generic nature as such (not a cause), generic nature as matter with the condition of excluding the differentia (a cause), and generic nature taken absolutely (lā bi-sharṭ), which is neither purely generic nor specific but the underlying substrate.

Key Terms

الطبيعة الجنسية
الطبيعة النوعية
بشرط
لا بشرط

Key Takeaway
Generic nature is not a cause for specific nature unless taken with the condition that the differentia is excluded, where it serves as a material cause.

The Five Universals: Essential Tools for Definitions and Syllogisms
Five universals are essential for logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why knowledge of the five universals is necessary or near-necessary for logic, using examples from Islamic definitions.

Core Idea
The five universals are the backbone of every definition and many syllogisms.

The passage opens by emphasizing the role of division (al-qisma) in acquiring knowledge and then highlights that the five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, accident) are essential for definitions and syllogisms. The author argues that before moving to compound expressions, understanding these states of singular expressions is either necessary or near-necessary. This lesson explores why the five universals are indispensable for the logician.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main uses of the five universals
The passage states that knowledge of the states of the five is beneficial in syllogisms, but even more so in definitions (ḥudūd) and descriptions (rusūm). Definitions are built from genus and differentia; descriptions from genus and property.
This shows the direct link between the five universals and the core logical activities of defining and reasoning.
2
Distinguish necessary vs. optional knowledge
The author classifies knowing these states as 'either necessary or near-necessary' for the student of logic before learning compound expressions. Compare this with the categories, which are later deemed unnecessary.
Understanding this hierarchy helps prioritize what to study in logic.
3
Apply to a real Islamic definition
Consider the definition of 'ṣalāh' in fiqh: 'statements and actions that begin with takbīr and end with taslīm, with specific conditions.' Identify the genus ('statements and actions') and differentia ('begin with takbīr and end with taslīm'). This illustrates how the five universals operate in a real definition.
Practice extracting genus and differentia from Islamic legal definitions.
4
Assess the necessity claim
Reflect on why the author says this knowledge is 'near-necessary': without the five universals, you cannot construct or evaluate definitions, which are fundamental to all Islamic sciences.

تدرخل في البرهان. والقسمة أيضًا هي من الطرق الموصلة إلى اكتساب العلم كما هي للأجناس إلى الأنواع بالفصول محفوظا فيها الترتيب لأن لا يقع طفرة من درجة إلى غير التي يليها. وقد يكون أيضًا بالخواص والأعراض، فمعرفة أحوال الخمسة نافعة في القياسات ومنفعتها في الحدود والرسوم أظهر؛ إذ الحدود من الأجناس والفصول، والرسوم من الأجناس والخواص. فتقديم تعرف هذه الأحوال اللاحقة للألفاظ المفردة قبل الشروع في معرفة مما لا ينتفع به في شيء من العلوم أصلا، فضلا عن المنطق. ولا من جهة حال دلالتها على الأنواع، فإن صناعة المنطق بمعزل عن ذلك. وقد تمت بدونه ولا من حال دلالتها على الأجناس العالية المسماة بالمقولات. فإن يخطر بباله أن ههنا مقولات عشر أو غير عشر ولا يعرض من أعمال ذلك خلل يعتد به ومن أنها هي الأمور التي توصف بالجنسية لا محالة والأمور الأخرى توصف بالنوعية؛ بل معرفة هذه إما من جهة كيفية الوجود فإلى الفلسفة الأولى، وإما من جهة تصور نفس لها فإلى حد من العلم الطبيعي يصاقب الفلسفة الأولى ومعرفة أنها تستحق ألفاظًا يوضع بإزائها وضعًا أولًا أو ثانيًا فإلى صناعة اللغة، وليس أن يعرف المنطقي من حيث هو منطقي ذلك أولى من معرفة. نعم هنا شيء واحد هو أن المتعلم قد ينتفع بهذا انتفاعا من وجه وهو أنه يحصل له إحاطة ما بما هو من الشرائط في التعريفات واقتدار على إيراد الأمثلة في التقسيمات. وإذا كانت الحدود قد يعرض فيها اختلاف باختلاف وقوع المحدودات في مقولات شتى كحال الشيء الذي من مقولة المضاف مثلا، فإنه يعرض له أن يحتاج في تحديده إلى أحوال لا يعرض لما يقع في مقولة الجوهر، وربما اختص بأنواع الكمية في التحديد خواص هي لها دون غيرها من الأنواع. وإذا كانت هذه الأشياء مفهومه على حالها كان تعلم ذلك أسهل بعد أن كانت الحاجة إلى أفراد هذا التعلم غير ما تبين في هذا المعنى. فإنه يمكن أن تعلم صناعة التحديد بكمالها من غير أن يحتاج إلى أفراد هذا الفن. وبالجملة فإن من وضعه فذلك لم يضعه على سبيل التعلم؛ بل على سبيل الوضع والتقليد. فإنه لا سبيل بالبيان المناسب للمنطق إلى أن يعلم ما يعلم فيه بالتحقيق من أنه بهذا العدد وهو العشرة، وأنه لا تمام لها وأنه لا تداخل فيها. وأن كل واحد منها جنسًا حقيقيًّا، وأن تسعة منها مخالفة للواحد في أنه جوهر، وهي أعراض، وما أشبه ذلك فإنها بيانات لا سبيل إلى معرفتها إلا المباحث متروكة في الكتاب الذي هو الأصل وكل ما يكون كذلك فأخذه على سبيل التقليد أولى، فإن ذلك يكفي في هذا الموضع والإقدام على الزوائد التي لا تعلق لها بالمباحث المنطقية غير معقول.
They enter into demonstration. Division also is among the ways leading to the acquisition of knowledge, just as it is for genera to species through differentiae, with order preserved in it so that no leap occurs from a degree to one not next to it. And it may also be by properties and accidents. So knowledge of the states of the five is beneficial in syllogisms, and its benefit in definitions and descriptions is more apparent; for definitions are from genera and differentiae, and descriptions from genera and properties. So preceding the knowledge of these states attaching to singular expressions before beginning to know compound expressions is either necessary or near-necessary. And singular expressions have other states, namely their indication of existing things, and there is no necessity to know those states for learning the art of logic — neither from the aspect of their indication of particular individuals, for that is of no benefit in any science at all, let alone logic; nor from the aspect of their indication of species, for the art of logic is separate from that and has been completed without it; nor from the aspect of their indication of the highest genera called categories. For the student of logic, after knowing singular expressions, names, and words, it is possible for him to proceed to learn propositions and their divisions, syllogisms and their varieties, definitions and their types, and demonstrative and non-demonstrative definitions, their genera and species — even if it does not occur to his mind that there are ten categories or more or less, and no appreciable defect arises from that. And that these are things that are necessarily described by genus-hood and other things by species-hood; rather, knowledge of these — either from the aspect of how they exist belongs to first philosophy, or from the aspect of having a concept of them belongs to a branch of natural science adjacent to first philosophy, and knowledge that they deserve words placed opposite them as primary or secondary placement belongs to the science of language. And it is not more appropriate for the logician qua logician to know that than to know others. Yes, there is one thing: that the student may benefit from this in one respect, namely that he obtains some comprehension of the conditions in definitions and the ability to bring examples in divisions. And if definitions sometimes differ according to the difference of the definiendum falling under various categories — like the case of a thing from the category of relation, for example, for it may need in its definition states that are not needed for what falls under the category of substance, and perhaps types of quantity have special characteristics in definition that belong to them and not to other types — and if these things are understood as they are, learning that becomes easier, since the need for particulars of this learning is not as shown in this meaning. For one can learn the whole art of definition without needing the particulars of this art. In sum, whoever sets it down, he did so not by way of learning but by way of imposition and imitation. For there is no way through the demonstration appropriate to logic to know what is known with certainty: that it is this number — ten — that they are complete, that there is no overlapping, that each one of them is a real genus, that nine of them are opposed to the one in that it is substance and the others are accidents, and so on. For these are demonstrations that cannot be known except through thorough investigation; and there is no way to thorough investigation except after reaching the level of first philosophy. So one must be certain that the purpose of this art is to believe that ten things are highest genera encompassing existents, by way of imitation and good opinion, without demonstrating that each one is a genus in reality or such and such. For that is undertaken by the science mentioned earlier, namely first philosophy. And whoever occupies himself with that in this place has occupied himself with what exceeds his apparent capacity, because these investigations are left to the original book. And everything that is like that, taking it by way of imitation is better, for that suffices in this place, and embarking on extras that have no connection with logical investigations is unreasonable.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about what knowledge is necessary or beneficial for the student of logic. The author begins by affirming that division (al-qisma) is a method to acquire knowledge, just as genera are divided into species by differentiae, preserving order to avoid leaps. It can also be done by properties and accidents. Then he states that knowledge of the states of the five universals is beneficial in syllogisms and even more so in definitions and descriptions, because definitions are composed of genus and differentia, and descriptions of genus and property. Therefore, learning these states of singular expressions before moving to compound expressions is either necessary or near-necessary.

However, singular expressions have other states—their reference to existing things—but the logician does not need to know those: not reference to particular individuals (that benefits no science), not reference to species (logic is separate from that), and not reference to the highest genera called categories. The student of logic can proceed to learn propositions, syllogisms, definitions, etc., even without knowing about the ten categories. Knowing the categories belongs to first philosophy (metaphysics), natural science, or philology, not to logic. The logician qua logician does not have priority in knowing them.

Nevertheless, there is one benefit: the student gains some comprehension of the conditions in definitions and ability to give examples in divisions. Definitions can vary depending on the category of the definiendum—e.g., a relative thing needs different conditions than a substance, and quantity has its own peculiarities. But still, the art of definition can be learned completely without delving into the categories. The author concludes that the categories are taken on imitation and good opinion (taqlid wa husn al-zann) in logic, not through demonstration. Demonstrating that they are ten, non-overlapping, that each is a real genus, etc., is the job of first philosophy. Attempting to prove that in logic is overstepping and unreasonable. Thus, the passage clarifies the boundary between logic and metaphysics.

المقولات
الفلسفة الأولى

The Ten Categories: Why They Are Not Required in Logic
Categories are unnecessary for logic

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the ten categories are not necessary for logic and identify which science they belong to, using examples from Islamic metaphysics.

Core Idea
Categories belong to metaphysics, not logic; the logician may take them on imitation.

After establishing the necessity of the five universals, the author argues that knowledge of the ten categories (substance and nine accidents) is not required for learning logic. He explains that reference to particular individuals, species, and highest genera belongs to other sciences. The logician can proceed perfectly well without them. This lesson unpacks this boundary-setting argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify what the logician does NOT need
The author lists three things singular expressions indicate that are unnecessary: indication of particular individuals (benefits no science), indication of species (logic is separate), and indication of highest genera (categories). The student of logic can learn all logical operations without categories.
This is a demarcation: logic deals with form, not the real existential content.
2
Find where categories belong
The passage assigns knowledge of categories to other sciences: how they exist to first philosophy (metaphysics), their concepts to natural science near metaphysics, and their linguistic expression to philology. The logician qua logician need not know them.
This shows the author's understanding of the division of Islamic sciences.
3
Recognize the one benefit of studying categories
Despite not being necessary, the author admits one benefit: it helps in understanding conditions in definitions and providing examples in divisions. But he emphasizes that the entire art of definition can be learned without categories.
This acknowledges a pedagogical aid but not a logical necessity.
4
Evaluate the author's critique of overemphasizing categories
The author criticizes those who present categories as essential to logic, calling it imitation (taqlīd) and imposing extras (zawā'id). He argues that demonstrating the categories as ten, real genera, etc., belongs to first philosophy, not logic.
This is a direct refutation of the view that categories are foundational to logic.

Source Text

تدرخل في البرهان. والقسمة أيضًا هي من الطرق الموصلة إلى اكتساب العلم كما هي للأجناس إلى الأنواع بالفصول محفوظا فيها الترتيب لأن لا يقع طفرة من درجة إلى غير التي يليها. وقد يكون أيضًا بالخواص والأعراض، فمعرفة أحوال الخمسة نافعة في القياسات ومنفعتها في الحدود والرسوم أظهر؛ إذ الحدود من الأجناس والفصول، والرسوم من الأجناس والخواص. فتقديم تعرف هذه الأحوال اللاحقة للألفاظ المفردة قبل الشروع في معرفة المركبات إما ضروري وإما كالضروري. وللألفاظ المفردة أحوال أخرى وهي دلالتها على الأمور الموجودة ولا ضرورة إلى معرفة تلك الأحوال أن يتعلم صناعة المنطق لا من جهة حال دلالتها على الأشخاص الجزئية، فإن ذلك مما لا ينتفع به في شيء من العلوم أصلا، فضلا عن المنطق. ولا من جهة حال دلالتها على الأنواع، فإن صناعة المنطق بمعزل عن ذلك. وقد تمت بدونه ولا من حال دلالتها على الأجناس العالية المسماة بالمقولات. فإن المتعلم للمنطق إذا انتقل بعد معرفة الألفاظ المفردة والأسماء والكلمات أمكنه أن ينتقل إلى تعلم القضايا وأقسامها والقياسات والتحديدات وأصنافها والحدود البرهانية وغير البرهانية أجناسها وأنواعها. وإن لم يخطر بباله أن ههنا مقولات عشر أو غير عشر ولا يعرض من أعمال ذلك خلل يعتد به ومن أنها هي الأمور التي توصف بالجنسية لا محالة والأمور الأخرى توصف بالنوعية؛ بل معرفة هذه إما من جهة كيفية الوجود فإلى الفلسفة الأولى، وإما من جهة تصور نفس لها فإلى حد من العلم الطبيعي يصاقب الفلسفة الأولى ومعرفة أنها تستحق ألفاظًا يوضع بإزائها وضعًا أولًا أو ثانيًا فإلى صناعة اللغة، وليس أن يعرف المنطقي من حيث هو منطقي ذلك أولى من معرفة. نعم هنا شيء واحد هو أن المتعلم قد ينتفع بهذا انتفاعا من وجه وهو أنه يحصل له إحاطة ما بما هو من الشرائط في التعريفات واقتدار على إيراد الأمثلة في التقسيمات. وإذا كانت الحدود قد يعرض فيها اختلاف باختلاف وقوع المحدودات في مقولات شتى كحال الشيء الذي من مقولة المضاف مثلا، فإنه يعرض له أن يحتاج في تحديده إلى أحوال لا يعرض لما يقع في مقولة الجوهر، وربما اختص بأنواع الكمية في التحديد خواص هي لها دون غيرها من الأنواع. وإذا كانت هذه الأشياء مفهومه على حالها كان تعلم ذلك أسهل بعد أن كانت الحاجة إلى أفراد هذا التعلم غير ما تبين في هذا المعنى. فإنه يمكن أن تعلم صناعة التحديد بكمالها من غير أن يحتاج إلى أفراد هذا الفن. وبالجملة فإن من وضعه فذلك لم يضعه على سبيل التعلم؛ بل على سبيل الوضع والتقليد. فإنه لا سبيل بالبيان المناسب للمنطق إلى أن يعلم ما يعلم فيه بالتحقيق من أنه بهذا العدد وهو العشرة، وأنه لا تمام لها وأنه لا تداخل فيها. وأن كل واحد منها جنسًا حقيقيًّا، وأن تسعة منها مخالفة للواحد في أنه جوهر، وهي أعراض، وما أشبه ذلك فإنها بيانات لا سبيل إلى معرفتها إلا بالاستقصاء؛ ولا سبيل إلى الاستقصاء إلا بعد الوصول إلى درجة الذي فلسفة أولى، فيجب أن يتحقق أن الغرض من هذا الفن هو أن يعتقد أن أمورًا عشرة هي أجناس عالية تحوي الموجودات اعتقادا على سبيل التقليد وحسن الظن من غير أن يبرهن أن كل واحد منها جنس بالحقيقة أو كذا وكذا. فإن ذلك مما يتكلفه العلم الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو الفلسفة الأولى، ومن يشتغل بذلك في هذا الموضع فقد اشتغل بما لا يفي وسعه ظاهرا لما أن هذه المباحث متروكة في الكتاب الذي هو الأصل وكل ما يكون كذلك فأخذه على سبيل التقليد أولى، فإن ذلك يكفي في هذا الموضع والإقدام على الزوائد التي لا تعلق لها بالمباحث المنطقية غير معقول.
They enter into demonstration. Division also is among the ways leading to the acquisition of knowledge, just as it is for genera to species through differentiae, with order preserved in it so that no leap occurs from a degree to one not next to it. And it may also be by properties and accidents. So knowledge of the states of the five is beneficial in syllogisms, and its benefit in definitions and descriptions is more apparent; for definitions are from genera and differentiae, and descriptions from genera and properties. So preceding the knowledge of these states attaching to singular expressions before beginning to know compound expressions is either necessary or near-necessary. And singular expressions have other states, namely their indication of existing things, and there is no necessity to know those states for learning the art of logic — neither from the aspect of their indication of particular individuals, for that is of no benefit in any science at all, let alone logic; nor from the aspect of their indication of species, for the art of logic is separate from that and has been completed without it; nor from the aspect of their indication of the highest genera called categories. For the student of logic, after knowing singular expressions, names, and words, it is possible for him to proceed to learn propositions and their divisions, syllogisms and their varieties, definitions and their types, and demonstrative and non-demonstrative definitions, their genera and species — even if it does not occur to his mind that there are ten categories or more or less, and no appreciable defect arises from that. And that these are things that are necessarily described by genus-hood and other things by species-hood; rather, knowledge of these — either from the aspect of how they exist belongs to first philosophy, or from the aspect of having a concept of them belongs to a branch of natural science adjacent to first philosophy, and knowledge that they deserve words placed opposite them as primary or secondary placement belongs to the science of language. And it is not more appropriate for the logician qua logician to know that than to know others. Yes, there is one thing: that the student may benefit from this in one respect, namely that he obtains some comprehension of the conditions in definitions and the ability to bring examples in divisions. And if definitions sometimes differ according to the difference of the definiendum falling under various categories — like the case of a thing from the category of relation, for example, for it may need in its definition states that are not needed for what falls under the category of substance, and perhaps types of quantity have special characteristics in definition that belong to them and not to other types — and if these things are understood as they are, learning that becomes easier, since the need for particulars of this learning is not as shown in this meaning. For one can learn the whole art of definition without needing the particulars of this art. In sum, whoever sets it down, he did so not by way of learning but by way of imposition and imitation. For there is no way through the demonstration appropriate to logic to know what is known with certainty: that it is this number — ten — that they are complete, that there is no overlapping, that each one of them is a real genus, that nine of them are opposed to the one in that it is substance and the others are accidents, and so on. For these are demonstrations that cannot be known except through thorough investigation; and there is no way to thorough investigation except after reaching the level of first philosophy. So one must be certain that the purpose of this art is to believe that ten things are highest genera encompassing existents, by way of imitation and good opinion, without demonstrating that each one is a genus in reality or such and such. For that is undertaken by the science mentioned earlier, namely first philosophy. And whoever occupies himself with that in this place has occupied himself with what exceeds his apparent capacity, because these investigations are left to the original book. And everything that is like that, taking it by way of imitation is better, for that suffices in this place, and embarking on extras that have no connection with logical investigations is unreasonable.
Source pages p. 174

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion about what knowledge is necessary or beneficial for the student of logic. The author begins by affirming that division (al-qisma) is a method to acquire knowledge, just as genera are divided into species by differentiae, preserving order to avoid leaps. It can also be done by properties and accidents. Then he states that knowledge of the states of the five universals is beneficial in syllogisms and even more so in definitions and descriptions, because definitions are composed of genus and differentia, and descriptions of genus and property. Therefore, learning these states of singular expressions before moving to compound expressions is either necessary or near-necessary.

However, singular expressions have other states—their reference to existing things—but the logician does not need to know those: not reference to particular individuals (that benefits no science), not reference to species (logic is separate from that), and not reference to the highest genera called categories. The student of logic can proceed to learn propositions, syllogisms, definitions, etc., even without knowing about the ten categories. Knowing the categories belongs to first philosophy (metaphysics), natural science, or philology, not to logic. The logician qua logician does not have priority in knowing them.

Nevertheless, there is one benefit: the student gains some comprehension of the conditions in definitions and ability to give examples in divisions. Definitions can vary depending on the category of the definiendum—e.g., a relative thing needs different conditions than a substance, and quantity has its own peculiarities. But still, the art of definition can be learned completely without delving into the categories. The author concludes that the categories are taken on imitation and good opinion (taqlid wa husn al-zann) in logic, not through demonstration. Demonstrating that they are ten, non-overlapping, that each is a real genus, etc., is the job of first philosophy. Attempting to prove that in logic is overstepping and unreasonable. Thus, the passage clarifies the boundary between logic and metaphysics.

Key Takeaway
The ten categories are metaphysical, not logical; the logician need only take them on imitation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Three Definitions of Accident
Author presents three definitions of accident and clarifies the expression 'existing in something'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three definitions of accident and distinguish accident from a whole existing in its parts.

Core Idea
Accident is not just 'in a subject' — it must not be a part and cannot subsist without it.

Accident (al-ʿaraḍ) is a fundamental category in Islamic logic and ontology. The author presents three definitions, each adding a condition to exclude mistaken cases. He also examines how the phrase 'existing in something' is used in different ways. Understanding these precise distinctions is crucial for avoiding errors in defining substances and accidents in kalam and falsafah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions
Read the three definitions in the Arabic. The first says accident is 'that which exists in a subject'. The second adds 'not as a part of it'. The third adds 'and whose subsistence is not valid without that in which it is'. Notice how each addition rules out a specific confusion.
The third definition is the most complete.
2
Understand the progressive refinements
The first definition could include parts of a substance (like a hand in a body) − so the second excludes parts. The second could still include forms that subsist independently − so the third requires dependence on the subject.
3
Analyze 'existing in something'
The author says this expression applies to many things in three ways: univocally (same meaning), equivocally (related meanings by priority/posteriority), and ambiguously (confused meaning). Consider whether accident fits univocally or equivocally.
The author does not specify which mode for accident here.
4
Distinguish accident from whole-in-parts
The whole exists in its parts, but not as an accident − the parts are constitutive. The author rejects an earlier view that the whole has a unifying form existing independently. Use the third definition to see why the whole is not an accident.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [في العرض]
أما العرض فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة: [أ] منها هو أنه الموجود في موضوع [ب] ومنها أنه الموجود في شيء لا كجزء منه، [ت] ومنها أنه الموجود في شيء لا كجزء منه ولا يصح قوامه من دون ما هو فيه. فقولنا الموجود في شيء يقع على أشياء كثيرة على بعضها بالتواطؤ وعلى بعضها بالتشكيك وعلى بعضها بالاشتباه وإنه أي الموجود في شيء يفرق بين حال العرض وبين حال ما يقال في شيء دون حال الكل في الأجزاء كما قيل من قبل، فإن ذلك باطل. وإن كان للكل صورة ما جامعة لا توجد قائمة بنفسها
Chapter on Accident: As for accident, it is defined by different definitions: [A] Among them is that it is that which exists in a subject. [B] Among them is that it is that which exists in something not as a part of it. [C] Among them is that it is that which exists in something not as a part of it and whose subsistence is not valid without that in which it is. So our saying 'existing in something' applies to many things — some of them by univocity, some by equivocity, and some by ambiguity. And it (i.e., 'existing in something') distinguishes between the state of an accident and the state of what is said to be 'in something' such as the state of the whole in its parts, as was said before, for that is false. Even if the whole has some unifying form that does not exist independently.
Source pages p. 182

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section on the concept of accident ('araḍ). The author lists three definitions, each more precise than the last. The first definition is simply 'that which exists in a subject'. The second adds the condition 'not as a part of it', to distinguish accident from a part of a substance. The third adds 'whose subsistence is not valid without that in which it is', emphasizing that an accident cannot exist independently; it requires a subject. The author then analyzes the expression 'existing in something' (al-mawjūd fī shay'), noting that it can be applied in three ways: by univocity (same meaning), by equivocity (different meanings related by priority and posteriority), and by ambiguity (equivocation). He contrasts accident with the case of a whole in its parts: a whole exists in its parts but not as a accident, because the parts are constitutive of the whole. Earlier claims that a whole has a unifying form that does not exist independently are rejected as false. The passage is a careful refinement of what it means for something to 'exist in' another, a crucial distinction for Aristotelian ontology.

Clarifying the Third Proposition: Substance vs. Accident
Author clarifies the third proposition on substance and accident.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two interpretations of the third proposition on substance and accident and correctly determine when something qualifies as a substance in another.

Core Idea
Not being an accident does not automatically make something a substance in that thing.

The author examines a key proposition about substance and accident, showing that it is only uncontroversial under one interpretation. He warns against conflating logical dependence with ontological categories and introduces a crucial distinction between 'accident' (ʿaraḍ) and 'accidental' (ʿaraḍī). This clarification is essential for precise reasoning in Islamic ontology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition being clarified
The third proposition states: 'Everything that is in something and is not an accident in it is a substance in it.' Locate this in the Arabic.
2
Distinguish the two interpretations
Interpretation A: what is in something and is not itself an accident while being in it is a substance. Interpretation B: anything in something, even if not as an accident, is a substance simply because it is in that thing. The author accepts A but denies B.
3
Analyse the author's denial of interpretation B
The author argues that not being an accident in a thing does not imply being a substance in that thing. Substance is defined by not being in a subject at all (ontological independence), not by mere relation.
4
Grasp the distinction between 'accident' and 'accidental'
A thing in a subject is called an 'accident' because it depends on a subject for existence, and 'accidental' because it is a non-constitutive attribute relative to that subject. This clarifies that being an accident is a matter of ontological dependence, not just relation.
5
Apply the summary rule
If a thing is inherently independent of any subject, then that in which it inheres: if that in-here is itself dependent on a subject, it is an accident; otherwise it is a substance existing in another.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يلزم منه إلا وأن العرض لا يكون في المركب وأما الثالثة وهى أن كل ما كان في شيء، وليس عرضًا فيه فهو جوهر فيه، فإنها من جملة ما لا نزاع فيه إذا كان المراد منه أن كل شيء هو في شيء، وليس في نفسه عرضًا وهو أيضًا فيه، فهو جوهر. فأما إذا كان المراد منه أن كل شيء هو في شيء لا على أنه فيه نحو العرض في الموضوع بحسب أن يكون بالقياس إلى كونه فيه جوهرًا، فذلك في حيز المنع . فإنه لا يلزم من عدم كون الشيء عرضًا في الشيء الذي هو فيه فهو جوهر فيه؛ بل ما لم يكن عرضًا في نفسه فهو جوهر في نفسه إذا لم يكن الجوهر ما ليس في موضوع هو ذلك المركب أو غيره. ثم الشيء إذا أضيف إلى شيء هو فيه كالشيء في الموضوع فهو عرض وعرضي أيضًا؛ إما أنه عرض فلكونه مفتقرًا في نفسه إلى موضوع، وإما أنه عرضي فلكونه أمرًا له بالقياس إلى هذا الموضوع وهو غير مقوم له بالقياس إليه. وبالجملة فالشيء إذا كان في نفسه غير مفتقر إلى موضوع ما البتة، فالذي هو فيه إن كان في نفسه مفتقرًا إلى موضوع يكون فيه أي شيء كان ذلك الموضوع فهو عرض، وإلا فهو جوهر يكون في الغير على ما عرف.
It follows from it that an accident does not occur in a composite. As for the third (proposition), namely that everything that is in something and is not an accident in it is a substance in it, it is among that about which there is no dispute if what is meant is that everything that is in something, and is not in itself an accident while also being in it, is a substance. But if what is meant is that everything that is in something not as an accident is in a subject such that relative to being in it it is a substance, then that is subject to denial. For it does not follow from a thing not being an accident in the thing in which it is that it is a substance in it; rather, what is not an accident in itself is a substance in itself, if the substance is not what is not in a subject—whether that composite or otherwise. Then, when a thing is related to something in which it is, like a thing in a subject, it is both an accident and accidental: an accident because of its being in itself in need of a subject, and accidental because of its being something relative to this subject while not constitutive relative to it. In summary, if a thing in itself is in no need at all of a subject, then that in which it is—if the latter is in itself in need of a subject in which it is, whatever that subject may be—is an accident; otherwise it is a substance occurring in another, as was known.
Source pages p. 192

Commentary
This passage is a definitional clarification of the third proposition in a prior discussion about the relationships between substance and accident. The author first restates the third proposition: 'Everything that is in something and is not an accident in it is a substance in it.' He notes that this is uncontroversial if interpreted to mean that something which is in another and is not itself an accident while being in that other is a substance. However, if it is taken to mean that anything in something else, even if not in the manner of an accident, is a substance in virtue of being in that thing, then it is denied. He explains that not being an accident in a thing does not automatically make it a substance in that thing; rather, what is not an accident in itself is a substance in itself, provided that 'substance' is defined as what is not in a subject—whether that composite or something else. The author then refines the terminology: when a thing is attributed to something in which it exists, like a thing in a subject, it can be called both 'accident' (&#x639;ara&#x1e0d;) and 'accidental' (&#x639;ara&#x1e0d;&#x12b;). It is an accident because it is inherently dependent on a subject; it is accidental because it is relative to this subject without being constitutive of it. In conclusion, if a thing is in itself independent of any subject, then that in which it inheres—if that in-which is itself dependent on a subject—is an accident; otherwise it is a substance that exists in another, as previously defined. This passage guards against conflating logical dependence with ontological categories.

جوهر

Checkpoint
According to the author, the third proposition is denied when interpreted as:
A) Everything that is in something and is not an accident in itself is a substance in it.
B) Everything that is in something, not as an accident, is a substance relative to that thing.
C) Everything that is in something and is a substance in itself is a substance in that thing.
D) Everything that is in something and is an accident in that thing is not a substance.

Key Takeaway
Not being an accident in a thing does not make something a substance in that thing; only if it is not an accident in itself (independent of a subject) does it qualify as a substance, and then the in-herent's own status determines whether it is an accident or a substance.

Distinguishing 'First' from 'More Deserving' in Substantiality
Author distinguishes 'first' from 'more deserving' and argues individual priority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'first' (al-awwal) and 'more deserving' (al-awlā) in the context of substantiality and identify the three aspects in which individuals are prior to universals.

Core Idea
Not everything more deserving of a thing is its first; priority is measured by existence, perfection, and naming.

The author clarifies a crucial logical distinction: being 'first' in essence vs being 'more deserving' of a term. Individuals are not first in the reality of substance (that belongs to quiddity), but they are more deserving of being called substance for three specific reasons. This lesson will walk you through that distinction and the arguments for individual priority.

Guided Path
1
Locate the distinction between al-awwal and al-awlā
Find where the author defines these two terms. What is the key difference? Note that 'first' means priority in reality itself, while 'more deserving' means having more of the thing's properties or being prior in existence.
The author states: 'Not everything that is more deserving of a thing is its first.'
2
Identify the three aspects of priority
The author gives three reasons individuals are more deserving of substantiality: existence, perfection, and naming. List them from the passage.
3
Analyze the argument for priority in existence
How does the author argue that particular substances are prior in existence? Focus on the dependence of universals on subjects versus the independence of individuals.
Universal substances necessarily have subjects; individuals do not need something else to be predicated of them.
4
Examine the objection about correlation
An objector says universal and particular are correlatives. How does the author respond? What distinction does he make about the meaning of 'universal' and 'particular'?
The author clarifies that 'universal' means predicated of many, not the correlative sense.

وإن كانت أولى بالجوهرية. وفرق بين الأول والأولى فليس كل ما هو أولى الشيء فهو أوله بل قد يكون أولى به إذا كان لواحق الشيء، وكمالاته يكون له أكثر مما لغيره أو أقدم له في الوجود بما لغيره والشخصيات ليست أولا في حقيقة الجوهرية؛ إذ تلك الحقيقة للماهية لكنها هي الأولى بالجوهرية لما أنها أول من جهة الوجود ومن جهة ما يكون باعتباره الجوهر جوهرا؛ وهو الحصول في الأعيان لا في موضوع ومن جهة الكمال والفضيلة أيضًا. ومن جهة السبق إلى التسمية أما من جهة الوجود فإن الجواهر الكلية من حيث هي كلية بالفعل فهي إما مقولة بالقياس إلى الجزئيات، أو باعتبار النسبة إليها أو بأن وجودها ذلك أن يكون مقولة بوجه ما على موضوعات؛ فلا بد لها من الموضوعات والشخص لا يحتاج في أن يكون شخصا أي غير مقول معناه قولا وجوديًا أو وهميّا على كثرته إلى أن يكون شيء آخر مقولا عليه وعلى غیره وإلا لكان من شرط كل شخص أن يكون معه غيره. ولما كان كل شخص غنيا في تقرر وجوده عن صاحبه فهو غني عن الكلي، فإن قلت إن الكلي كما أنه كلي بالقياس إلى الجزئي كذلك الجزئي جزئي بالقياس إلى الكلي، وكما جزئي، كذلك أن ماهية الجزئي من حيث هي لا تتعلق بالكلي بل من حيث هو جزئي، ماهية الكلي لا تتعلق من حيث هي بل تتعلق من حيث هو هو كلي. فنقول لا كلام في الكلي والجزئي من حيث هما متضايفان بل يعني بالكلي ما يكون مقولا على كثيرين وبالجزئي ما لا يكون كذلك بل واحد بالعدد كزيد وعمرو، وإنه لا يقال الكلي مقابلة المضاف فلا يتعلق وجوده إذن بطبيعة الكلي. ثم لقائل أن يقول كما أن الشخص بعينه لا يتعلق وجوده بأن يكون الكلي موجودًا، فالكلي أيضًا لا يتعلق بالشخص بعينه. لكنا نقول المعنى بالشخص هو الطبيعة الشخصية على الإطلاق لا هو الشخص بعينه، ولا يتعلق لتلك الطبيعة في الوجود بوجود الطبيعة الكلية من حيث هي كلية، وأما الطبيعة الكلية فهي متعلقة بشخص ما لا محالة. وبالجملة فالكلام في ماهية الجوهر من حيث هي كلية لا من حيث هي ماهية والمنطقي ينظر في الأشياء من حيث هي كلية . وأما التقدم باعتبار الجوهرية وهي أنها ماهية من شأنها إذا وجدت أن لا تحتاج إلى موضوع، والجواهر الأولى بهذه الصفة على خلاف الجواهر الكلية. وأما التقدم بالكمال والفضيلة فقد قال قوم: «إنها إذا كانت موضوعات وأصولا لغيرها كانت هي أفضل لكنه لا يتم إلا وأن يكون الأصل يجب أن يكون أفضل والموضوع كذلك، وفيه من الكلام بل ربما كان ذو الأصل الذي له الأصل وفضيلته أفضل من الأصل. وأما السبق إلى التسمية فلأن أول شيء عرف أنه موجود لا في موضوع فهو الأشخاص الجزئية، وبالحري أن يكون سابقة إذا كانت موضوعات لكلياتها على سبيل «على» والأعراض على سبيل «في»، فكان كل شيء وجوده إما أن يكون مقولا عليها أو موجودًا فيها. والجواهر الكلية وإن كانت ثانية فإن لها فيما بينها تفاوتا فالنوع منها أولى بالجوهرية من الجنس. ألا ترى أنك إذا سألت عن زيد «ما هو؟» فقلت «إنسان» كان أتم من جوابك أنه حيوان». وكما أن الشخص مقدم على النوع لما أنه موضوع الجنس وكذلك النوع من الجنس؛ فإن حال الجنس من حيث هو كلي من النوع الذي هو دونه كحال النوع من الشخص الذي دونه ولا يقال كيف يكون النوع كالشخص والنوع لا يكون نوعا إلا بالقياس إلى .الجنس. اللهم إلا أن يكون المعنى من النوع النوع السافل فإن المعنى بالنوع فيما نحن بصدده من الكلام هو طبيعة النوع من حيث هو كلي فقط، والنظر في طبيعة النوع من حيث هو كلي غير النظر في طبيعة النوع من حيث هو طبيعة النوع أو من حيث هو النوع.
And if they are more deserving of substantiality. And there is a difference between 'first' and 'more deserving': not everything that is more deserving of a thing is its first; rather it may be more deserving of it when it has more of the thing's concomitants and perfections than others, or it is prior in existence to others. Individual persons are not first in the reality of substantiality, for that reality belongs to the quiddity, but they are the most deserving of substantiality because they are first from the aspect of existence and from the aspect by which substance is considered substance, which is existing in reals not in a subject, and also from the aspect of perfection and virtue. And from the aspect of precedence in naming. As for the aspect of existence, universal substances insofar as they are actually universal are either predicated in relation to particulars, or by considering the relation to them, or their existence is such that they are predicated in some way of subjects; so they necessarily have subjects. But the person does not need, in order to be a person—i.e., not predicated, its meaning being existential or imaginary predication over its multiplicity—for there to be something else predicated of it and of others; otherwise it would be a condition of every person that another be with it. And since every person is independent in the determination of its existence from its companion, it is independent of the universal. If you say: 'Just as the universal is universal in relation to the particular, so the particular is particular in relation to the universal; and just as it is particular, so the quiddity of the particular as such is not attached to the universal but only as it is particular, and the quiddity of the universal as such is not attached but only as it is universal.' We say: There is no discussion about the universal and particular as they are correlatives; rather by 'universal' is meant what is predicated of many, and by 'particular' what is not so but is one in number like Zayd and 'Amr, and it is not said that the universal is in opposition to the relative; so its existence is not then attached to the nature of the universal. Then one may say: 'Just as the existence of the individual person as such is not attached to the universal's existence, so also the universal is not attached to that particular person.' But we say: By 'person' is meant the personal nature absolutely, not that particular person, and that nature's existence is not attached to the existence of the universal nature as universal; but the universal nature is necessarily attached to some person. In sum, the discussion is about the quiddity of substance as it is universal, not as it is quiddity; and the logician considers things as they are universal. As for precedence by substantiality—i.e., that its quiddity is such that when it exists it does not need a subject—the primary substances have this description contrary to universal substances. As for precedence by perfection and virtue, some people said: 'If they are subjects and principles for others, then they are better.' But it is not complete unless the principle must be better and the subject likewise; but there is discussion in this, for perhaps the possessor of the principle—which has the principle and its virtue—is better than the principle. As for precedence in naming, it is because the first thing known to exist not in a subject is individual persons, and it is fitting that they be prior since they are subjects for their universals in the mode of 'alā (upon) and for accidents in the mode of 'fī' (in). So every thing's existence is either predicated of them or existing in them. And universal substances, though secondary, have disparity among themselves: the species is more deserving of substantiality than the genus. Do you not see that if you ask about Zayd 'What is he?' and you say 'Human', it is more complete than your answer 'Animal'. And just as the person is prior to the species because it is the subject of the genus, so too the species is prior to the genus; for the state of the genus as universal in relation to the species that is below it is like the state of the species in relation to the person that is below it. And it is not said: 'How can the species be like the person, when the species is only a species in relation to the genus?' Except if by 'species' is meant the lowest species; for the meaning of 'species' in our present discussion is the nature of the species as it is universal only, and consideration of the nature of the species as it is universal is different from consideration of the nature of the species as it is the nature of the species or as it is the species.
Source pages p. 213

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the priority of individual substances (al-jawāhir al-shakhṣiyya) over universal substances (al-jawāhir al-kulliyya) in deserving the designation 'substance' (jawhar). The author begins by distinguishing between 'first' (al-awwal) and 'more deserving' (al-awlā). Not everything more deserving of a thing is its first; something can be more deserving because it has more of the thing's properties or perfections, or is prior in existence. Individual persons (al-shakhṣiyyāt) are not 'first' in the reality of substantiality (ḥaqīqat al-jawhariyya), since that reality is the quiddity (al-māhiyya) itself, but they are 'more deserving' of substantiality for three reasons: existence, perfection, and naming. In terms of existence, universal substances as actual universals depend on subjects (mawḍū‘āt) because they are predicated of or related to particulars. A person, however, does not need something else to be predicated of it to be a person; it is independent. The author addresses an objection about the correlation (taḍāyuf) between universal and particular: the universal is defined as what is predicated of many, while the particular is one in number. The universal's existence is necessarily attached to some person, but not to a specific person. The discussion is about the quiddity of substance as universal, which is the logician's perspective. Next, the author discusses precedence by substantiality proper: primary substances (al-jawāhir al-ūlā) are those whose quiddity does not need a subject, unlike universal substances. Precedence by perfection is debated: some argue that what is a subject and principle for others is better, but the author notes that the possessor of a principle might be better than the principle itself. Precedence by naming: the first things known to exist not in a subject are individuals, so they are deserving of the name 'substance' first. Finally, within universal substances, species (al-naw‘) is more deserving than genus (al-jins). For example, answering 'human' for Zayd is more complete than 'animal'. The species is prior to the genus just as the individual is prior to the species. The author clarifies that 'species' here means the nature of the species as universal, not as a specific class.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, individual persons are not 'first' in the reality of substantiality because:
A) They are not substances.
B) The reality of substantiality belongs to the quiddity.
C) They are predicated of many.
D) They are temporal.

Key Takeaway
Individual substances are more deserving of the name 'substance' than universals because they are prior in existence, perfection, and naming, even though the quiddity of substance is conceptually first.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the following claim: 'The species 'human' is prior in definition to individual humans.' Using the author's distinction, explain whether 'human' is 'first' or 'more deserving' of substantiality compared to Zayd.
?
Consider a Fiqh example: the universal category 'intoxicant' vs individual drinks like khamr. Which is more deserving of being called 'prohibited'? Apply the three aspects of priority (existence, perfection, naming) to argue your case.

intermediate
6 min
Priority of Species Over Genus in Substantiality
Author argues species is more deserving of substantiality than genus, with example of Zayd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why species is more deserving of substantiality than genus, using the example of defining Zayd as 'human' versus 'animal'.

Core Idea
Species is more deserving of substantiality than genus because it is closer to the individual and completes the definition.

The author extends the priority argument from individuals to universals: within universal substances, species are more deserving of being called substance than genus. The example of asking 'What is Zayd?' illustrates that answering 'human' (species) is more complete than 'animal' (genus). This lesson explores that reasoning and clarifies the meaning of 'species' in this context.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim about species and genus
Find where the author states that species is more deserving of substantiality than genus. What is the basis for this claim?
The author says: 'But the universal substances, though secondary, have disparity among themselves: the species is more deserving of substantiality than the genus.'
2
Examine the example of Zayd
Why is answering 'human' more complete than 'animal' when asked 'What is Zayd?'? How does this illustrate the priority of species?
Answering 'human' gives the species, which is more specific and captures the essence more fully than the genus.
3
Understand the analogy
The author compares the relation of species to genus with that of individual to species. How does this analogy work? What does it mean that species is like the individual?
Just as the individual is subject for the species, the species is subject for the genus.
4
Clarify the meaning of 'species'
The author notes a possible confusion: 'species' here means the nature as universal, not as a specific class. Why is this distinction important?
The consideration of species as universal is different from considering it as the specific nature or as the species itself.

And if they are more deserving of substantiality. Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the priority of individual substances (al-jawāhir al-shakhṣiyya) over universal substances (al-jawāhir al-kulliyya) in deserving the designation 'substance' (jawhar). The author begins by distinguishing between 'first' (al-awwal) and 'more deserving' (al-awlā). Not everything more deserving of a thing is its first; something can be more deserving because it has more of the thing's properties or perfections, or is prior in existence. Individual persons (al-shakhṣiyyāt) are not 'first' in the reality of substantiality (ḥaqīqat al-jawhariyya), since that reality is the quiddity (al-māhiyya) itself, but they are 'more deserving' of substantiality for three reasons: existence, perfection, and naming. In terms of existence, universal substances as actual universals depend on subjects (mawḍū‘āt) because they are predicated of or related to particulars. A person, however, does not need something else to be predicated of it to be a person; it is independent. The author addresses an objection about the correlation (taḍāyuf) between universal and particular: the universal is defined as what is predicated of many, while the particular is one in number. The universal's existence is necessarily attached to some person, but not to a specific person. The discussion is about the quiddity of substance as universal, which is the logician's perspective. Next, the author discusses precedence by substantiality proper: primary substances (al-jawāhir al-ūlā) are those whose quiddity does not need a subject, unlike universal substances. Precedence by perfection is debated: some argue that what is a subject and principle for others is better, but the author notes that the possessor of a principle might be better than the principle itself. Precedence by naming: the first things known to exist not in a subject are individuals, so they are deserving of the name 'substance' first. Finally, within universal substances, species (al-naw‘) is more deserving than genus (al-jins). For example, answering 'human' for Zayd is more complete than 'animal'. The species is prior to the genus just as the individual is prior to the species. The author clarifies that 'species' here means the nature of the species as universal, not as a specific class.

Critique of the Common Definition of Shape
Author critiques the common definition of shape and distinguishes shape from magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the category error in the common definition of shape and distinguish between shape as quality and magnitude as quantity.

Core Idea
Defining shape by boundaries confounds quality with quantity.

In Islamic logic, precise definitions are crucial. The common definition of shape as 'what is enclosed by boundaries' is widely used, but the author here exposes a subtle category error. By the end of this lesson, you will see why shape belongs to the category of quality, not quantity, and how this affects geometric reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition under critique
Locate the phrase 'rasm al-mashhur fi al-shakl' (the famous definition of shape) in the Arabic. The author is targeting the notion that shape is 'that which is surrounded by boundaries'.
2
Find the category mismatch
Notice that the thing surrounded by boundaries is called 'al-mahdud' (the bounded), which the author equates with magnitude (miqdar). Magnitude belongs to quantity (kammiyya), while shape belongs to quality (kayfiyya). The definition thus misapplies a quantitative property to a quality.
This is a fundamental distinction in Aristotelian categories used in Islamic logic.
3
Analyze the author's alternative
The author states that shape is not the bounded entity itself, but rather the configuration (hay'a) resulting from the existence of the boundary and the bounded together. This configuration is a quality.
The alternative is not a full formal definition but a conceptual shift.
4
Examine the illustrative contrast
The author uses the pair 'murabba' (square) vs 'tarbi' (squareness). The square (magnitude) can be bounded, but squareness (quality) is not bounded. This clarifies the distinction.
Similar to 'white' vs 'whiteness' in English.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن الرسم المشهور في الشكل يشبه أن يكون ذلك الرسم غير متحقق للشكل الذي هو الكيفية بل هو رسم للذي يستعمله المهندسون الذين يقولون إنه مساو لشكل آخر وغير مساو، وهو مقدار مشكل. فإن الشيء الذي يحيط به الحدود بالذات هو المحدود، والمحدود بالذات هو المقدار، والمقدار بالذات كم، والشكل كيف. فليس إذن ما يحيط به الحدود بشكل هو الشكل الذي من باب الكيف لكن الهيئة الحاصلة من وجود الحد والمحدود على نسبة ما هو الشكل. ألا ترى أن المربع غير التربيع؟ وقد يقال [المربع]، ويُعنى به التربيع كما يقال أبيض ويُعنى به البياض. ثم التربيع شكل من باب الكيف ولا يقال إنه ما أحاط به حد، أو حدود ولا يحيط به الحد ولا الحدود أيضًا
And the second [point] is that the famous definition (rasm) of shape appears to be not a true definition of shape as it is a quality, but rather a definition of that which geometers use, who say that it is equal to another shape and not equal, and it is a complicated magnitude. For the thing that is surrounded by boundaries in itself is the bounded, and the bounded in itself is magnitude, and magnitude in itself is quantity, while shape is quality. Therefore, what is surrounded by boundaries is not the shape that belongs to the category of quality, but rather the configuration (hay'a) resulting from the existence of the boundary and the bounded in a certain proportion is the shape. Do you not see that the square (murabba') is not squareness (tarbi')? And one may say 'square' meaning squareness, just as one says 'white' meaning whiteness. Then squareness is a shape from the category of quality, and it is not said to be what is surrounded by a boundary or boundaries, nor is it surrounded by the boundary or boundaries either.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the standard definition of shape (shakl). The author argues that the famous definition used by geometers—'what is surrounded by boundaries'—actually defines magnitude (miqdar), not shape as a quality. He explains that the thing enclosed by boundaries is the magnitude, which is quantity, whereas shape is a quality. The true shape is the configuration (hay'a) that results from the boundary and the bounded together. To illustrate, he contrasts a square (murabba'), which can refer to the magnitude, with squareness (tarbi'), which is the shape-quality. Just as 'white' can mean whiteness, 'square' can mean squareness, but properly squareness is not defined by being surrounded by boundaries. The author's critique follows the logical precision of distinguishing between quantity and quality, using geometric examples from the Islamic tradition.

Key Terms
شكل (shakl): shape as quality
رسم (rasm): descriptive definition
مقدار (miqdar): magnitude (quantity)
تربيع (tarbi'): squareness (quality)
مربع (murabba'): square (magnitude or quality by metonymy)
