Refining the Definition of al-Mudaf: Avoiding Circularity
Refinement of al-mudaf definition and resolution of circularity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical flaw in a definition that fails to exclude foreign elements, apply the remedy of specifying existence, and evaluate whether a definition is genuinely circular.

Core Idea
A proper category definition must exclude non-instances; using the term itself is not automatically circular.

In this passage, the author addresses a classic problem in defining categories: the initial definition of al-mudaf (the relative) is too broad, including things from other categories like substance. He offers a corrective and then defends against the charge of circularity. This mirrors similar challenges in Islamic jurisprudence when defining key terms like 'qiyas' or 'riba'. Understanding how to fix a flawed definition while avoiding logical pitfalls is essential for rigorous reasoning in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and initial definition
The term is al-mudaf (the relative). The first definition (al-rasm al-awwal) described it as something with a relation, but this includes items from substance and other categories. Note why this is problematic: a category definition must be coextensive with the category.
2
Extract the flaw
The flaw is that the definition does not prevent non-relative things (like a head, which can be someone's head) from being considered al-mudaf. The author says the definition is not specific to the category.
This is a violation of the rule that a proper definition (hadd or rasm) must exclude all non-instances.
3
Understand the remedy
The well-known remedy (al-tadarak al-mashhur) is that relative things are those whose very existence is relative—not just having a relation, but being essentially relational. The author insists that this is distinct from the first definition.
The remedy adds the condition that existence itself is relative (al-wujud laha mudaf).
4
Address the circularity objection
Some object that using 'relative' in the definition of 'relative' leads to circularity. The author refutes this by analogy with al-mumkin (the possible): initially defined as 'not impossible', specialists later redefined it as 'neither necessary nor impossible'. The term is used in both definitions without circularity because the meaning shifts from general to specific.
This shows that a term can appear in its own definition if the definiendum is a specific sense and the definiens uses a general sense.
5
Apply the refined definition
The refined definition: al-mudaf is that whose quiddity and existence is being relative (mudaf), with no other existence. This excludes things like 'head' which are only accidentally relative, and includes only items like 'half' and 'double' that are essentially relational.
The author uses the example of 'half' and 'double': if half exists, double is implied, but double is not independently realized—mirroring the relational existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النسبة. وكذلك أمور أخرى من مقولات أخرى. فالرسم الأول لا يمنع من أن يكون هنا أمور هي من باب الجوهر وغيره داخلة في المضاف فليس هذا الرسم إذن رسم المقولة وإلا لا تشترك في رسم واحد أمور من مقولات شتى وإن لم يكن رسمًا لها بل هو اسم معنى يعمها ؛ فيجب إذن تدارك هذا الخلل. والتدارك المشهور هو أن الأمور التي من المضاف هي التي الوجود لها مضافة؛ وهذا تدارك صحيح لكن بعض الناس يظن أن هذا بعينه هو الرسم الأول؛ وليس كذلك وإلا لكان بالأول عنه عني على الخصوص إذا كان الأول يدل على المعنى الذي يدل عليه دلالة واضحة لا يدل عليه الثاني. وإن دل دلالة بذلك الوضوح وبعضهم يظن أنه يقضي إلى الدور لما أن المضاف جزء لما يعرف به وليس كذلك أيضًا. فإن من الأشياء ما يكون جنسه أشهر عند الجمهور فيكون الاسم بحسب الوضع الأول موضوعًا لجنسه أو لما هو كالجنس. ثم إن الخواص يعرفون معنى نوعيا تحته أو معنى هو كالنوعي فينقلون اسم الجنس إليه كما في الممكن فإنه اسم لما ليس بممتنع بحسب الوضع الأول. فالخواص يُنقلونه إلى ما ليس بواجب الوجود، ولا يمتنع أيضًا فخصوه باسم الممكن من حيث هو غير ضروري؛ وهذا هو نقل اسم ما هو كالجنس إلى ما كالنوع. وعلى هذا في المضاف فإنه كان مقولا في الوضع الأول عند جمهور الفلاسفة على المعنى المذكور؛ وهو أنه ما يقال ماهيته على الصفة المذكورة من غير اعتبار أن له وجودًا غير ذلك أو ليس له وجود غير ذلك حتى إذا كان الشيء من الجوهر أو من غيره؛ ثم لحقه نسبة كان من حيث هو كذلك مقول الماهية بالقياس إلى غيره. فكان من المضاف فله ماهية مخصوصة من جملة ما لا يقال بالقياس، أو ماهية مقولة بالقياس إلى الغير كالأخوة مثلا؛ فكان المضاف يقع على معنيين جميعًا. وإن لم يكن لهما جنسًا فليس كل ما يحمل بالمعنى على مقولتين أو على شيئين من مقولتين أو على شيئين من مقولة واحدة فهو جنس المقولتين. ولو كان كذلك لكان معنى المضاف المأخوذ في التعريف هو هذا المعنى العام. ومعنى المضاف المعرف هو هذا المعنى الخاص؛ فكما أن الممكن الحقيقي يعرف بأنه هو الذي يمكن أن يكون، ويمكن أن لا يكون؛ ولا دور فيه فكذلك المضاف الحقيقي يعرف بأنه هو الذي ماهيته ووجوده أنه مضاف يعني أنه الذي وجوده وماهيته هو أنه مقول الماهية بالقياس ولا وجود له غيره ؛ والمعنى من الجنس والنوع هنا هو العام والخاص لا الجنس الحقيقي ولا النوع كذلك. فإذا كان رسم المضاف الذي هو المقولة هو هذا الرسم فالرأس لا يكون مضافًا بالمعنى الذي للمقولة. وكذلك العلم والضعف فإنه لا يكون كما يعرض له نسبة. ولقائل أن يقول إن المضاف الذي هو المقولة فهو أيضًا شيء ذو إضافة لأنه شيء مقول ماهيته بالقياس إلى غيره، وإذا كان كذلك فقد شارك الذي هو المقولة غير المقولة فلا يكون بينهما فرق . وأما الشيئية فهي أمر لا ينفك عنه المضاف الذي هو المقولة لكن الفرق بين فإن الشيئية التي تحمل على المقولة هي التي يخصصها الوجود الذي للمضاف من حيث هو مضاف. وأما الشيئية التي تحمل على الغير فهي التي يخصصها وجود آخر كما مرَّ في هذا الباب من الجوهر وغيره من قبل. وأما شيئية الإضافة فهي الإضافة بالحقيقة. فلو قلنا إن المضاف الذي هو المقولة هو الشيء الذي ليس له وجود إلا الوجود الذي هو به مضاف فلسنا نعني به الوجود العام بل نحو أمر الوجود مخصصًا للعام ليس هو التخصيص بكونه إضافة فقط بل قد يخصص تخصيصا في أنه إضافة. ثم إذا كان المضاف لا وجود له إلا أنه مضاف فيلزم أن يكون إذا جعل أحد طرفيه كان الآخر محصلا بسببه حتى إذا قلت نصف مطلقًا كان بإزائه ضعف من غير تحصيل.
The relation. And likewise other things from other categories. So the first descriptive definition does not prevent that there are things from the category of substance and others that fall under the relative. So this definition is not the definition of the category, otherwise things from various categories would not share a single definition, even if it is not a definition for them, but rather it is a name for a meaning that encompasses them. So it is necessary to remedy this flaw. The well-known remedy is that the things that are from the relative are those whose existence is relative. This is a correct remedy, but some people think that this is exactly the first definition. It is not so, otherwise the first would have referred to it specifically, if the first indicates the meaning that the second indicates but the second does not indicate it. And if it indicates with that clarity, some people think that it leads to circularity because the relative is part of what it is defined by, but it is not so either. For some things, their genus is more famous among the common people, so the name in its first application is used for its genus or for something like a genus. Then the specialists know a specific meaning under it or a meaning like a specific meaning, and they transfer the name of the genus to it, as in the 'possible': for it is a name for what is not impossible according to the first application. So the specialists transfer it to what is not necessary of existence and not impossible either, and they particularize it with the name 'possible' from the aspect of being non-necessary. This is the transfer of the name of what is like a genus to what is like a species. And similarly for the relative: it was predicated in the first application among the majority of philosophers on the mentioned meaning, which is that it is that whose quiddity is said in the described manner without considering whether it has another existence or does not have another existence, so that if a thing is from substance or from other, then a relation attaches to it, it is from that aspect said to have quiddity in relation to another. So it is from the relative, and it has a specific quiddity among things not said in relation, or a quiddity said in relation to another, like brotherhood for example. So the relative applied to both meanings. And if it does not have a genus for them, then not everything that is predicated by a meaning on two categories or on two things from two categories or on two things from one category is a genus of the two categories. If it were so, then the meaning of 'relative' taken in the definition would be this general meaning, while the meaning of 'relative' being defined is this specific meaning. So just as the real possible is defined as that which can be and can not be, and there is no circularity in it, so too the real relative is defined as that whose quiddity and existence is being relative, meaning that its existence and quiddity is that it is predicated quidditatively in relation [to another] and it has no existence other than that. And the meaning of genus and species here is general and specific, not the real genus nor the real species. So if the descriptive definition of the relative that is the category is this definition, then 'head' is not relative in the sense of the category. Likewise, 'knowledge' and 'weakness' are not such that a relation attaches to them. And an objector may say that the relative that is the category is also something possessing relation, because it is something whose quiddity is predicated in relation to another, and if so, it shares [that property] with what is not the category, so there is no difference between them. As for thingness, it is something inseparable from the relative that is the category, but the difference is that the thingness that is predicated on the category is that which the existence of the relative particularizes from the aspect that it is relative. And the thingness that is predicated on the other is that which another existence particularizes, as has passed in this chapter concerning substance and others before. As for the thingness of relation, it is relation in reality. So if we say that the relative that is the category is the thing that has no existence except the existence by which it is relative, we do not mean by it the general existence, but rather a kind of existence particularizing the general. The particularization is not by being relation only, but it may particularize as being relation. Then if the relative has no existence except that it is relative, then it follows that when one of its two ends is posited, the other is realized by its cause, so that if you say 'half' absolutely, on its opposite side is 'double' without realization.
Source pages p. 260

Commentary
This passage refines the definition of the category of the relative (al-mudaf). The author begins by identifying a flaw in an earlier definition: it does not prevent things from other categories, such as substance, from falling under the relative. Therefore, it is not a proper definition (rasm) for the category, but rather a name for a general meaning that includes items from multiple categories. To fix this, the author presents the well-known remedy: relative things are those whose existence is relative. However, some mistakenly think this is identical to the first definition. The author clarifies that the first definition was not specific enough; the corrected definition adds the condition that existence itself is relative. The discussion then addresses a potential charge of circularity (dawr): since the term 'relative' is used in the definition of the relative category, some fear a vicious circle. The author argues this is not circular, using the example of 'possible' (al-mumkin). Initially, 'possible' was defined as 'not impossible', but specialists later transferred the term to mean 'neither necessary nor impossible'. Similarly, the relative was first applied to any thing that can be described in relation, but the refined definition specifies that its quiddity and existence are only being relative. The author then distinguishes between general and specific meanings, noting that here 'genus' and 'species' are used loosely. The refined definition ensures that items like 'head' (which is not essentially relative) are not included in the category. An objection is raised: the relative category and non-relative things both share the property of being 'something' (shay'iyya). The response is that the thingness of the relative is particularized by its relative existence, while other things have a different existence. Finally, the author explains that if the relative has no existence except as relative, then positing one endpoint necessarily entails the other—just as 'half' implies 'double' without the latter being independently realized. This passage is characteristic of a hashiya (super-commentary) tradition, where the author critically refines prior definitions to avoid logical pitfalls.

Key Terms
المضاف
الرسم
الدور
الممكن
الشيئية

D) It is based on a false analogy with 'possible'.

Key Takeaway
A proper definition of a category must exclude all non-instances, and using the definiendum term in the definition can be non-circular if the meanings are general and specific respectively.

Critical Refinement of the Definition of Kalam
Hashi critiques and refines the definition of kalam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the shortcomings in the definition of kalam as 'what indicates a meaning' and explain the refinements offered by the hashia.

Core Idea
Defining kalam requires more than indication; it demands attribution, independence, and completeness.

In this lesson, we examine a hashia on the definition of kalam (speech) in logic. The base definition 'what indicates a meaning' is critiqued as insufficient. The commentator raises several objections: the genus must be definite, the definition must exclude non-kalam like conditional clauses, and the role of particles is ambiguous. Through a dialectical exchange, the commentator refines the definition to require proper attribution and independent meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base definition being critiqued
Locate the phrase in the Arabic that states the definition of kalam as 'a word indicating a meaning'. The objector challenges this definition.
The base definition is implicit; the hashia responds to it.
2
Extract the objections raised
List the four main objections: (1) the genus is indefinite, (2) any two words with attribution would count (e.g., dancing), (3) word count is ambiguous (e.g., 'Zayd is standing' contains seven words if inflections are counted), (4) conditional sentences like 'if Zayd is in the house' have attribution but are not kalam.
Objection 4 is especially important: it shows that attribution alone is insufficient.
3
Analyze the commentator's responses
For each objection, find the commentator's rebuttal: 'includes' means includes words, not morphemes; kalam must have at least two words but also completeness; the addition of 'nothing' (bila shay') is needed to exclude incomplete utterances; particles indicate meanings only by relation, not independently.
The responses refine the definition step by step.
4
Synthesize the refined definition
Combine the commentator's conditions: kalam is a meaningful utterance consisting of at least two words with attribution (isnad) and independent meaning, excluding conditional clauses and incomplete constructions.
This refined definition avoids the objections raised.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقائل أن يقول ما ذكره ليس بحد الكلام لوضع أنه لفظ ما يدل على شيء غير معين، والجنس أن يكون شيئًا معينًا فيقولون جنسا. ثم لو كان زيد قائم مشتملا على وقعة صح أن يقال الرقص شيء يتضمن كلمتين بالإسناد ؛ ومعلوم أنه ليس كلام (ج) أنه كثيرا من الكلام يتضمن حملاً كثيرًا ما . وأيضًا قيل زيد قائم متضمن سبع كلمات» : «زيد» كلمة، و«الرفع» كلمة، و«التنوين كلمة : وكذلك الإيقاع ثلاث كلمات؛ وفيه ضمير آخر فيكون سبع كلمات فالحاكم = بأن الكلام ما يتضمن كلمتين بط عواقر؛ فذلك عشاري عندي إلا يتضمن كلمتين بالإسناد لأن عبد الرحمان جملة إسنادية تتضمن كلمتين مع زمانه مع ليس الكلام في إن كان زيد في الدار يتضمن كلمتين بالإسناد مع أنه ليس بكلام ما يليق أن يجار عن الأول والثاني أن المراد من قولنا يتضمن لفظ يتضمن، وعن الثالث المراد أن لا يتضمن أقل من كلمتين. أما من جهة الأكبر فلا يجري، وعن الرابع قدر بعد قوله بلا شيء فكلام وإلا لا يحسن السكوت عليه. وكذلك الجواب عنه والجوابات من السكوت لا بد وأن يكون لبعض المعرف وإلا على بعض المعرف إما بالمطابقة أو بالتضمن أو بالالتزام؛ ولفظ ما لا يدل على اللفظ بأحد الدلالات الثلاث؛ ولذا الكلام قال بعضهم الضمير الرفع في نفسه يوجه إليه فعلى أنه ما دل على معنى باعتباره به نفسه، والنظر إليه نفسه لا باعتبار أمر خارج عنه. ولذلك قيل لا الحرف ما دل على معنى غيره أي باعتبار متعلقة لا باعتبار ما لا لنفسه، ويمكن أن يقال تقديره ما دل على معنى مستقل بنفسه دون ذلك المتعلق.
And an objector might say: what he mentioned is not a definition of kalam because it assumes that a word indicates something indefinite, while the genus must be something definite. Then if 'Zayd is standing' includes a position, it would be correct to say 'dancing is a thing that includes two words with attribution', and it is known that this is not kalam. (C) that much of kalam includes a predicate often. Also it was said: 'Zayd is standing' includes seven words: 'Zayd' is a word, 'the nominative' is a word, 'the nunation' is a word, and likewise the predicate is three words, and in it is another implicit pronoun, so it becomes seven words. So the judge = that kalam is what includes two words with an inner consequence? So that is decimal? In my opinion, except that it includes two words with attribution, because 'Abd al-Rahman' is an attributive sentence including two words with its time, but not kalam. 'If Zayd is in the house' includes two words with attribution, yet it is not kalam. What is appropriate to respond about the first and second is that what is meant by 'includes' is 'a word includes'? And about the third: what is meant is that it includes no less than two words. As for the greater, it does not apply. And about the fourth: one should add after his statement 'nothing' so it becomes kalam, otherwise it is not proper to pause upon it. Likewise the answer to it and the answers from pausing must be for some definer, or else upon some definer either by conformity, by inclusion, or by commitment. And the expression 'what' does not indicate a word by any of the three indications. Therefore kalam, some said that the nominative pronoun refers to itself, so it is what indicates a meaning by itself, and considering itself not by considering an external matter. Therefore it was said: 'No, the particle indicates a meaning other than itself', that is, by considering its relation, not by considering what is not for itself. And it can be said its meaning is 'what indicates a meaning independent in itself without that relation.'
Source pages p. 267

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement (hashiya) on the definition of 'kalam' (speech) in logic. The objector raises several issues. First, the definition 'a word indicating something indefinite' is problematic because the genus must be definite. Second, if any utterance with two words and attribution were kalam, then 'dancing includes two words with attribution' would be kalam, which is not. Third, the example 'Zayd is standing' contains more than two words (if particles and inflections are counted). The author of the hashya then offers a response: what is meant is that kalam includes at least two words, but with proper attribution and completeness. He also discusses the nature of particles (huruf) and the conditions for a meaningful utterance. The commentary follows the objection-response structure, explaining each point as it appears in the text.

الحد
الإسناد
الدلالة

A) The genus is indefinite.

C) Conditional clauses would be excluded incorrectly.
D) Particles indicate meanings only by relation.

Key Takeaway
The hashia refines the definition of kalam by requiring attribution, independence, and exclusion of incomplete clauses.

Classification of Being and Definition of Motion
Author classifies being and defines motion as gradual actualization.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify beings into actual/potential/mixed categories and define motion as gradual actualization, distinguishing it from instantaneous generation.

Core Idea
Motion is gradual actualization; pure potential being is impossible.

In this passage, the author sets the stage for understanding change by first classifying all beings into three categories: fully actual, fully potential, and mixed. He then defines motion as the gradual transition from potentiality to actuality, contrasting it with instantaneous generation. This conceptual framework is foundational for physics and metaphysics in the Islamic tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of being
The passage states that a thing is either actual in every respect, potential in every respect, or actual in one respect and potential in another. Write these three categories down.
The second category (purely potential in every respect) is argued to be impossible.
2
Analyze why purely potential being is impossible
The author argues that a being that is purely potential in every respect would be potential in its very existence, which is contradictory. Understand the reasoning: existence itself cannot be merely potential without some actuality.
This eliminates the possibility of a completely potential being.
3
Distinguish the two types of actualization
The passage contrasts instantaneous actualization (called al-kawn) with gradual actualization (called al-haraka). Note that al-kawn includes generation and corruption that happen in an instant, while al-haraka is motion proper.
Gradualness (al-tadrij) is the key differentia.
4
Extract the definition of motion
Motion is defined as 'the gradual exit from potentiality to actuality.' Write this definition verbatim and identify its genus (exit from potentiality to actuality) and differentia (gradual).
This is a complete definition (hadd haqiqi).
5
Apply the definition to examples
Consider the growth of a tree from a seed (gradual, hence motion) versus the creation of the world at the beginning of time (instantaneous, hence generation). Classify each according to the definition.
This application cements the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أولا أن الشيء إما أن يكون موجودًا بالفعل من كل وجه، وإما أن يكون موجودًا بالقوة من كل وجه، وإما أن يكون بالفعل من وجه وبالقوة من وجه، ولا مجال للثاني منها وإلا لكان في وجوده بالقوة كذلك؛ وذلك محال لاستحالة أن يكون وجوده بالقوة ولا بالقوة فبقي منها الأول أو الثالث فيكون بالفعل من كل الوجوه أو بعضها. وما يكون بالقوة فذلك إما أن يكون خروجه إلى الفعل دفعة وهو المسمى بالكون، أو لا دفعة وهو الحركة؛ فالحركة إذن هو الخروج من القوة إلى الفعل على التدريج.
Know first that a thing is either actual in every respect, or potential in every respect, or actual in one respect and potential in another. The second of these is impossible, for otherwise it would be potential in its [very] existence, which is absurd because it is impossible for it to exist in potentiality without being potential [in some respect]. So only the first or third remains, thus it is actual in all respects or some. What is potential is either brought to actuality instantaneously, called generation (kawn), or not instantaneously, which is motion (haraka). So motion is the gradual exit from potentiality to actuality.
Source pages p. 270

Commentary
This passage defines motion by first classifying beings into three categories: fully actual, fully potential, and mixed. The author argues that a thing cannot be purely potential in every respect. Then he distinguishes two ways of transitioning from potentiality to actuality: instantaneous (generation/كون) and gradual (motion/الحركة). The definition of motion as 'gradual actualization' is standard in Aristotelian physics. The key is the contrast with instantaneous change. In fiqh terms, instantaneous generation is like the creation of the world, while motion is like the gradual ripening of a fruit.

Key Takeaway
Motion is defined as the gradual transition from potentiality to actuality, distinct from instantaneous change (generation).

Alternative Definition and Six Correlates of Motion
Author gives another definition of motion and enumerates its six essential correlates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six essential correlates of motion and evaluate the alternative definition using the criterion of self-evidence.

Core Idea
Motion is defined by change, but its full analysis requires six correlates.

The author offers a second definition of motion—mere 'change' (tabaddul) from one state to another—but warns that it works only if 'change' is self-evident. He then lists six necessary components that every instance of motion must have: the moving object, the mover, the starting point, the endpoint, the category (like place or quality), and time. Understanding these correlates helps us analyze motion in Islamic physics and kalam arguments about the physical world.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternative definition
Find the sentence that gives the definition of motion as 'change of a thing from one state to another' (تبدل الشيء من حالة إلى حالة). Notice the author says this definition is 'safer from criticism' only if 'change' is self-evident.
Self-evident means immediately known without proof.
2
Evaluate the condition of self-evidence
Consider: is 'change' really self-evident? The author implies that if it is not, the same difficulties arise as with other definitions. This is a meta-logical point: definitions must be based on concepts that are clearer than the definiendum.
This echoes the earlier discussion on the impossibility of a fully correct definition of motion.
3
List the six correlates
From the sentence beginning 'ثم الحركة مما يتعلق بالغير...' extract the six items: (1) المتحرك (the moving object), (2) المحرك (the mover), (3) ما منه (that from which), (4) ما إليه (that to which), (5) المقصود حصوله (the intended end – e.g., place or quality), and (6) الزمان (time).
The author arranges them as: that which has motion, that from which, that to which, that in which, and time. The 'that in which' is the category.
4
Apply to a real example
Take the motion of a stone falling from a hand towards the ground. Identify each correlate: (1) the stone, (2) gravity (or the force causing motion), (3) the hand, (4) the ground, (5) change in place (ayn), (6) the time elapsed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال «إنها عبارة عن تبدل الشيء من حالة إلى حالة»، وهذا هو الأسلم عن الطعن إذا كان التبدل من الأمور البديهية. فأما إذا لم يكن، ففيه من الكلام ما في الغير. وبالجملة فالحركة مما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة لا مجال للصحة في واحد منها؛ غير أن التعرض لبيان ذلك من جملة ما هو مفروغ عنه فيما نَحنُ فيه . ثم الحركة مما يتعلق بالغير، وذلك الغير فيها هو ما له الحركة وما منه وما إليه وما فيه والزمان إما الأول فهو المتحرك، وإما الثاني فهو المحرك، وإما الثالث فذلك ينبسط من حدها لأنها أول كمال يحصل لشيء له الكمال الثاني؛ فالأول ما منه الحركة، والثاني ما إليه، وذلك هو [ والثالث] الرابع منها. وأما الخامس فهو المقصود حصوله فيها من الأين أو الكيف أو غير ذلك، وأما السادس وهو الزمان؛ فإنه مما يتعلق به الحركة لما أنه يطابقها وهو مقدار الحركة.
Some have said: 'It is the expression for the change of a thing from one state to another.' This is safer from criticism if 'change' is among self-evident matters. If not, then the same difficulty applies. In sum, motion is known through various definitions, none of which are fully valid; but engaging in explaining that is beyond our present scope. Then motion relates to something else, which are: that which has motion, that from which, that to which, that in which, and time. As for the first, it is the moving object; the second is the mover; the third is derived from its definition because it is the first perfection that occurs to something having second perfection; so the first is that from which motion proceeds, the second is that to which it proceeds, and that is the fourth of them. The fifth is the intended [end] occurring in it, such as place (ayn), quality (kayf), or something else; and the sixth is time, which is related to motion because it corresponds to it and is the measure of motion.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage first presents a simpler definition of motion as mere 'change', but notes it works only if change is self-evident. The author then dismisses further discussion as beyond the present work. Next, he enumerates the six correlates of motion: (1) the subject that moves (المتحرك), (2) the agent that causes motion (المحرك), (3) the starting point (ما منه), (4) the endpoint (ما إليه), (5) the category in which the motion occurs (e.g., place, quality), and (6) time. The enumeration is standard in Aristotelian physics and helps analyze motion in terms of its essential components. For example, the motion of a stone falling from a hand involves: the stone (subject), gravity (mover), the hand (starting point), the ground (endpoint), change in place (category), and the duration (time).

Motion can be defined simply as change, but a complete analysis requires six correlates: subject, mover, starting point, endpoint, category, and time.

Definition of Motion and Its Six Related Aspects
Author discusses definitions of motion and enumerates its six related aspects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six relational aspects of motion as enumerated by the author and evaluate the author's stance on definitions of motion.

Core Idea
Motion defies perfect definition, but its six relational aspects can be enumerated.

This passage addresses the definition of motion, a topic debated in falsafah and kalam. The author notes that while various definitions exist, none are fully sound. He then shifts to discussing six aspects related to motion: the moved, the mover, the starting point, the endpoint, the path, and time. Understanding these relational aspects is crucial for analyzing discussions of motion in Islamic physics and metaphysics.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's stance on definitions of motion
The author states that no definition of motion is sound, but the definition 'change of a thing from one state to another' is safest if 'change' is self-evident. If not, it has the same problems as others.
2
List the six aspects of motion
From the Arabic passage, extract the six: the moved (المتحرك), the mover (المحرك), that from which (ما منه), that to which (ما إليه), that in which (ما فيه), and time (الزمان). Note the text is partially corrupted.
The third aspect (that in which) is unclear due to OCR corruption.
3
Understand the brief explanations for each aspect
The author explains that the mover causes motion, the moved undergoes it, the starting point and endpoint relate to perfection (first and second perfection), the path is the medium, and time corresponds to motion as its measure.
4
Relate these aspects to kalam arguments
In kalam, motion is key to the huduth (temporal origination) argument. The six aspects help structure proofs: the mover is the creator, the moved is the world, time is created, etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قال «إنها عبارة عن تبدل الشيء من حالة إلى حالة»، وهذا هو الأسلم عن الطعن إذا كان التبدل من الأمور البديهية. فأما إذا لم يكن، ففيه من الكلام ما في الغير. وبالجملة فالحركة مما يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة لا مجال للصحة في واحد منها؛ غير أن التعرض لبيان ذلك من جملة ما هو مفروغ عنه فيما نَحنُ فيه . ثم الحركة مما يتعلق بالغير، وذلك الغير فيها هو ما له الحركة وما منه وما إليه وما فيه والزمان إما الأول فهو المتحرك، وإما الثاني فهو المحرك، وإما الثالث فذلك ينبسط من حدها لأنها أول كمال يحصل لشيء له الكمال الثاني؛ فالأول ما منه الحركة، والثاني ما إليه، وذلك هو [ والثالث] الرابع منها. وأما الخامس فهو المقصود حصوله فيها من الأين أو الكيف أو غير ذلك، وأما السادس وهو الزمان؛ فإنه مما يتعلق به الحركة لما أنه يطابقها وهو مقدار الحركة.
And among them is one who said: 'It (motion) is an expression for the change of a thing from one state to another.' This is safest from criticism if the change is among self-evident matters. But if not, then it entails the same talk as the others. In sum, motion is known by various definitions, none of which can be sound; but to expound that is among what we have finished with in our subject. Then motion is connected to something other; that other in it is that which has motion, that from which, that to which, that in which, and time. As for the first, it is the moved; as for the second, it is the mover; as for the third, it spreads from its limit because it is the first perfection that occurs to a thing that has the second perfection. So the first is that from which motion is, the second is that to which it is, and that is [and the third] the fourth of them. As for the fifth, it is what is intended to be obtained in it from 'where' or quality or other than that. As for the sixth, which is time, it is that which motion is connected to because it corresponds to it, and it is the measure of motion.
Source pages p. 271

Commentary
This passage defines motion and its relational aspects. The author begins by citing a definition: motion is the change of a thing from one state to another. He notes this definition is safest from criticism if 'change' is self-evident. If not, it invites the same problems as other definitions. He concludes that no definition of motion is sound, but that is not his concern here. He then lists six things related to motion: the thing moved (mutaharrik), the mover (muharrik), that from which motion begins (mā minhu), that to which it tends (mā ilayhi), the path or medium (mā fīhi), and time (zamān). He explains each briefly. For example, the point of arrival is the second perfection. The path is the first perfection. Time is the measure of motion. The enumeration is partially corrupted in the text.

Correlatives and Contraries: Opposition between Existences
Opening of the chapter: definition of opposites and division of opposition between two existences into correlatives and contraries.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between correlatives and contraries and identify them in examples.

Core Idea
Opposition between existences is either relational (correlatives) or non-relational (contraries).

This passage introduces the general definition of opposites and then focuses on the two types that occur between two existences: correlatives, which require a relation, and contraries, which do not. Understanding this distinction is fundamental to mastering the four-fold classification of opposition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of opposites
The author defines two opposites as things that cannot coexist in one subject at one time. Write down this definition.
2
Find the first division
Opposition is either between two existences or between existence and non-existence. The first two types fall under the first branch.
3
Distinguish correlatives from contraries
Correlatives (e.g., owner and owned) are each understood in relation to the other; contraries (e.g., black and white) are not. Check the examples in the passage.
4
Test with your own example
Think of an Islamic science example: father and son are correlatives; black and white are contraries. Verify that they meet the conditions.

فصل: في المتقابلات. أما المتقابلان فهما اللذان يجتمعان في محل واحد في زمان واحد معا، وإنه على أربعة أقسام؛ وذلك لأن التقابل بين الشيئين وهو امتناع الاجتماع بينهما في محل واحد في زمان واحد لا يخلو من أن يكون بين الوجودين؛ وإنه على قسمين: فإن كل واحد منهما من حيث هو لا يخلو من أن يكون معقولاً بالقياس إلى الغير وهما المتضايفان كالمالك والمملوك مثلا؛ وهذا من جملة ما سبق ذكره في المقولات، أو لا يكون وهما المتضادان كالسواد والبياض كذلك. أو بين العدمين؛ وذلك لا يمكن فإن من المحال أن يكون العدم متميزا عن العدم، فضلا عن أن يكون مقابلا له ومنافيًا إياه فيكون بين المختلفين لا محالة. وذلك على قسمين أيضًا: فإن كل واحد منهما لا يخلو من أن يكون مجرَّدًا عن تحقق الموضوع المستعد لقبوله بحسب الجنس أو النوع أو الشخص وهما المتضايفان : وهذا من جملة ما يجيء من بعد، أو لا يكون بل يعتبر ذلك في الكل؛ وهما الملكة والعدم الحقيقيتان كالحركة والسكون لما كان السكون عبارة عن عدم الحركة فيما من شأنه أن يتحرك؛ إذ المشهوران فإنهما باشتراط الموضوع القابل بل بحسب الوقت كما في القدرة على الشيء عند المشية وارتفاعها عن المادة المتهيئة للقبول. ولما كان التقابل منقسما إلى هذه الأقسام كان بالضرورة أعم من السلب والإيجاب، وكذلك السلب والإيجاب من السلب والإيجاب في المتناقضين وغيره. ثم التقابل بين السلب والإيجاب إما أن يكون بسيطًا كتقابل الفرس واللا فرس، وإما أن يكون مركبا كما في قولنا زيد فرس، زيد ليس بفرس»: والأول مما لا صدق فيه ولا كذب بخلاف الثاني؛ فإن فيه صدق وكذب. ويشتركان في أنه ليس فيهما إشارة إلى الوجود الخارجي فإن ذلك من قسم الملكة والعدم بل الاعتبار فيهما اعتبار أحكام عقلية فإنه إذا كانت اللا فرسية شيئًا له وجود بوجه كان السماء فيه سلوب موجودة بالفعل غير متناهية لا مرة واحدة بل مرارًا غير متناهية. فإن كل جملة تعرض سلب مستأنف؛ وذلك محال فيكون
Chapter on Opposites. The two opposites are those that meet in one subject at one time together, and they are of four divisions. That is because the opposition between two things, which is the impossibility of their co-existence in one subject at one time, is either between two existences; and it is of two divisions: for each of them, in itself, is either conceived in relation to the other—these are the correlatives, like owner and owned for example; this is part of what was previously mentioned in the categories—or not—and these are the contraries, like black and white similarly. Or between two non-existences; that is impossible, for it is impossible that non-existence be distinguished from non-existence, let alone be opposite and contrary to it. So it must be between two different things. And that is also of two divisions: for each of them, in itself, is either abstracted from the realization of the subject receptive to it according to genus, species, or individual—and these are the correlatives? (this is to come later)—or not, but that is considered in all; these are true privation and possession, like motion and rest, since rest is the absence of motion in that which is by nature to move. For the two well-known ones are conditional upon the receptive subject, even according to time, as in the power to do something at will and its removal from matter prepared to receive. And since opposition is divided into these divisions, it is necessarily more general than negation and affirmation, and likewise negation and affirmation are from negation and affirmation in contradictories and other than them. Then the opposition between negation and affirmation is either simple, like the opposition between 'horse' and 'not horse', or compound, as in our statement 'Zayd is a horse', 'Zayd is not a horse'. The first is neither true nor false, unlike the second, for it has truth and falsehood. They share in that they do not refer to external existence—that belongs to the division of privation and possession—rather the consideration in them is the consideration of intellectual judgments. For if 'not-horse' were something with existence in some respect, then the sky would have infinite actual negations, not once but infinitely many times. For every proposition presents a new negation; that is impossible, so...
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of opposition (taqabul). It begins by defining two opposites as things that cannot coexist in one subject at one time. Then it divides opposition into four kinds based on whether the two things are existences or non-existences. First, between two existences: either correlatives (mutadayifan) like father and son (each understood in relation to the other), or contraries (mutadaddan) like black and white (no relation needed). Second, between two non-existences is impossible because non-existence cannot be distinguished. Third, between existence and non-existence: either privation and possession (malaka wa 'adam) like sight and blindness (requires a suitable subject that can receive the possession), or negation and affirmation (salb wa ijab) like 'man' and 'not-man' (no condition of a subject). The passage then explains that negation and affirmation can be simple (e.g., 'horse' vs. 'not-horse') or compound (e.g., 'Zayd is a horse' vs. 'Zayd is not a horse'); the simple has no truth value, the compound does. Both do not refer to external existence but are intellectual judgments. It concludes with an argument against treating 'not-horse' as something existent, which would lead to infinite negations.

متقابل
متضايفان
متضادان

D) Man and not-man

Key Takeaway
Opposition between two existences is either correlative (relation-based) or contrary (non-relational).

Privation/ Possession and Negation/ Affirmation: Opposition Involving Non-Existence
Continuation of the classification: opposition between existence and non-existence, including privation/possession and negation/affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between privation/possession and negation/affirmation, and between simple and compound negation.

Core Idea
Opposition between existence and non-existence requires either a suitable subject (privation) or no condition (negation).

After discussing opposition between two existences, the author turns to opposition between existence and non-existence. He distinguishes two types: privation/possession, which depends on a subject capable of receiving the quality, and negation/affirmation, which is a purely intellectual judgment without subject condition. He then further divides negation/affirmation into simple (terms) and compound (propositions).

Guided Path
1
Understand the impossibility of opposite non-existences
The author argues that two non-existences cannot be opposites because non-existence cannot be distinguished. So opposition must involve at least one existence.
2
Identify the two types under existence/non-existence
When one opposite is existence and the other non-existence, either the non-existence is located in a subject that can naturally possess the existence (privation/possession) or it is not (negation/affirmation).
3
Differentiate simple and compound negation/affirmation
Simple negation is between terms like 'horse' and 'not-horse' (no truth value). Compound negation is between propositions like 'Zayd is a horse' and 'Zayd is not a horse' (truth value).
4
Reflect on the argument against treating negation as existent
The author argues that if 'not-horse' were an existent entity, then the sky would have infinite negations, which is impossible. This shows that negation is a mental judgment.

Essential vs. Accidental Opposition in Privation and Possession
Author distinguishes essential from accidental opposition in privation/possession.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential opposition from accidental opposition in the category of privation and possession, and identify whether a given opposition includes a subject.

Core Idea
Essential opposition excludes the subject; accidental opposition includes it.

In Islamic logic, opposition (taqabul) is not uniform. Some oppositions are essential, arising directly between the terms themselves, while others are accidental, involving a subject that bears the privation. This passage by the author clarifies this distinction, focusing on the pair of privation (al-‘adam) and possession (al-malaka), and corrects a common error of treating privation as a genus. Understanding this helps you parse definitions and classifications in logic and other Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two kinds of opposition
Read the final sentence: 'But the first and essential opposition is that which does not include the subject; for if the subject is included, then it is opposition by secondary intention, accidentally.' This gives you the core criterion.
Essential opposition: 'horse' vs. 'non-horse'. Accidental opposition: 'blind man' vs. 'sighted man' (includes subject 'man').
2
Understand why privation and possession are not correlatives
The author argues that if A (e.g., blindness) and B (sight) were correlatives, each would be defined by reference to the other, like father and son. But here, blindness is defined by sight, yet sight is not defined by blindness. So they are not correlatives.
This shows that the opposition is accidental: the subject (e.g., eye) is implicitly present.
3
Critique the mistaken view that privation is a genus
Some thought that 'blindness' belongs to the genus 'privation'. The author refutes this: privation (as a genus) would be an essential predicate, but blindness is not an essential predicate of sight. Privation is not 'measured against a mental form' like possession is.
The author emphasizes that privation is understood 'accidentally' (bi al-‘arad), relative to the subject.
4
Apply the distinction to examples
Consider: 'horseness' vs. 'non-horseness' (essential, no subject), versus 'horse' vs. 'non-horse' (accidental, subject 'animal' implied). Similarly, 'blindness' vs. 'sight' (essential? Actually no subject), but 'blind' vs. 'sighted' (accidental, subject 'person').
Use this to classify other opposites you encounter in logic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المتقابلات الأول بل أمور تلحق المتقابلات فيعرض لها أن يكون مقابله. وعلى هذا في السلب والإيجاب فإن ما يوجب أو يُسلب نفسه ليس بقول بل هو محمول في القول كقولك جالس وليس بجالس. ثم العدم والملكة لا يكون أحدهما مقولاً بالنسبة إلى الآخر؛ أما الملكة فليست مفتقرة في تصورها إلى العدم البتة، وأما العدم كالعمى فإنه وإن كان لا يتصور إلا بتصور الملكة فإنه ليس مقول الماهية بالقياس إلى الملكة. وقد ظن بعض الناس أن العمى ينسب إلى البصر من طريق جنسه أو ما هو كجنسه وهو العدم. فإن العدم معقول بالعرض وبسبب ما هو عدمه: وهذا خطأ فإن العدم الذي هو جنس العمى ليس مقول الماهية بالقياس إلى شيء ولا بالقياس إلى الملكة. فإن العدم ليس إنما هو عدم لأجل أنه مقيس إلى صورة موضوعة في الذهن بإزائها يقال له عدم كما يكون العمى عميا لأن الملكة ملكة. وكما يكون الأب أبا لأن الابن ابن فينعكس من الجانبين لما أن القول بالقياس عبارة عن حال الشيء من جهة أن شيئًا آخر موجود بإزائه، ومأخوذ أيضًا من حيث هو كذلك. وليس حال الملكة عند العدم كذلك بل ينسب إليها العدم بإزاء زوالها وفقدانها فقدان شيء آخر كيف اتفق؛ وبهذا يظهر أن العدم والملكة ليسا بمتضايفين. وبالجملة فكما أن بين الفرسية واللا فرسية والسواد والبياض والأبوة والبنوة والعمى والبصر تقابلا فكذلك بين الفرس واللا فرس والأب والابن والأبيض والأسود والأعمى والبصير لكن التقابل الأول وبالذات هو ما ليس فيه الموضوع؛ فإنه إذا أخذ فيه الموضوع كان تقابلا بالقصد الثاني، وبالعرض لا بالذات.
The primary opposites are matters that attach to opposites, so that they come to have their opposite. And on this basis in negation and affirmation, that which affirms or negates itself is not a statement, but it is predicated in the statement, like your saying 'sitting' and 'not sitting'. Then privation and possession: one of them is not a predicate in relation to the other; as for possession, it is not in its conception dependent on privation at all; as for privation like blindness, although it is not conceived except by conceiving possession, it is not an essential predicate in relation to possession. And some people have thought that blindness is attributed to sight via its genus or what is like its genus, which is privation. For privation is understood accidentally and because of that of which it is a privation: and this is a mistake, for privation which is the genus of blindness is not an essential predicate in relation to anything nor in relation to possession. For privation is not such that it is a privation because it is measured against a form placed in the mind opposite which it is called privation, as blindness is blind because possession is a possession. And as the father is a father because the son is a son, so it reflects from both sides, because the statement 'by measurement' expresses the state of a thing in respect that another thing exists opposite it, taken also from that aspect. And the state of possession with respect to privation is not like that; rather, privation is attributed to it in respect of its removal and loss, a loss of something else in whatever way. And by this it becomes clear that privation and possession are not correlatives. And in summary, just as there is an opposition between horseness and non-horseness, and between blackness and whiteness, and between fatherhood and sonship, and between blindness and sight, so too between horse and non-horse, between father and son, between white and black, between blind and sighted. But the first and essential opposition is that which does not include the subject; for if the subject is included, then it is opposition by secondary intention, accidentally and not essentially.
Source pages p. 285

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and critiquing classification of types of opposition, focusing on privation (al-‘adam) and possession (al-malaka). The author begins by stating that the primary opposites (al-mutaqabilat al-awwal) are not themselves opposites but rather matters that attach to opposites. Then he applies this to negation and affirmation: the act of affirming or negating itself is not a statement (qawl) but a predicate within a statement (like 'sitting' and 'not sitting'). Next, he examines privation and possession, asserting that they are not predicates relative to each other in the sense of essential predication (maqul al-mahiyya). For example, 'blindness' is understood only through 'sight', but blindness is not an essential predicate of sight. He critiques a mistaken view that privation is the genus of blindness, arguing that privation is not an essential predicate because it is not measured against a mental form as possession is (e.g., blindness is called blindness because sight is a possession, not because privation is a genus). He contrasts this with correlation (tadāyuf), where terms like 'father' and 'son' imply each other mutually. The key distinction is that the first/essential opposition (taqabul awwal bi al-dhat) does not involve the subject (mawdu‘); if the subject is included, the opposition becomes accidental (bi al-‘arad) and by secondary intention (bi al-qasd al-thani). The passage helps clarify that privation and possession are not correlatives (mutadayifayn).

Key Terms
تقابل
عدم وملكة
مقول الماهية
بالقصد الثاني
متضايفين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is 'adam' not an essential predicate relative to 'malaka'?
A) Because 'adam' is only a mental concept and has no real existence.
B) Because 'adam' is not measured by a standard (qiyas) placed in the mind as possession is, so it is not correlative.
C) Because 'adam' is a genus of blindness and thus an essential predicate.
D) Because possession depends on privation for its conception.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction is that essential opposition does not include a subject, while accidental opposition includes a subject, making privation and possession an example of accidental opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Contrariety of Good and Evil: When Evil Opposes Evil
Definition and examples of contrariety in moral qualities

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the logical conditions under which one evil is directly contrary to another evil, using the Aristotelian mean of virtue as a framework.

Core Idea
Virtues are means between two vices; therefore, in rare cases, one vice is directly contrary to another vice.

This passage explores the nature of contrariety (tadad) as applied to good and evil. While in most cases good and evil are absolute contraries, the author highlights a special case where evil can be contrary to another evil—this occurs in the realm of moral virtues, where a virtue is a mean (wasat) between two extremes of excess and deficiency. Understanding this logical nuance helps us analyse moral concepts more precisely.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish two types of contrariety
The author begins by stating a common view: absolute evil is contrary to absolute good, and each particular evil is contrary to a particular good (e.g., sickness vs. health). Then he introduces a second type: evil can be contrary to another evil. Identify both types in the passage.
This is the key logical distinction.
2
Identify the domain where evil opposes evil
The passage says this occurs 'because the middle dispositions (malakat) between excess and deficiency... are virtues, and the excesses and deficiencies are vices.' For example, cowardice (deficiency) and recklessness (excess) are vices, with courage as the mean. They are contrary to each other because each is an extreme.
The mean is good and opposed to both extremes.
3
Test the condition for evil vs. evil
The author notes this only happens in 'few matters'. Check if a given pair of vices fits the pattern: they must be extremes of the same virtue spectrum. For example, greed and wastefulness are extremes of generosity. Are they contrary to each other? If the virtue (generosity) is the mean, then yes. If there is no such middle (e.g., knowledge vs. ignorance), then the two evils are not mutually contrary.
This step applies the author's logic to new examples.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: فيما يتعلق بالتضاد من المباحث وما يتعلق بالتضاد من المباحث فذلك أهم بالنسبة إلى الغير؛ إذ فيه من الاختلافات بين الأهم كما قيل في الخير والشر إن الشر على الإطلاق ضد الخير على الإطلاق، وكل واحد من الشر لكل واحد من الخير كذلك كالمرض للصحة والجور للعدل فنحو ذلك . وأما مضادة الشر للشر فقد يكون؛ وذلك لأن الملكات المتوسطة بين الإفراط والتفريط في الأمور المتعلقة بالشهوة والغضب، وبالتدبير الجزئي في اقتناء الخير هي الفضائل والإفراطات والتفريطات هي الرذائل، فإن الجبن والتهور والخمود والفجور مثلا رذائل ؛ والوسائط تضاد الطرفين، وهذا إنما يوجد في قليل من الأمور. وأما في أكثر الأشياء فإن الخير يضاد الشر مطلقاً ولا يوجد للشر شر يضاده؛ فمن ذلك ما فيه الإفراط كله رديء كالجهل ، ومن ذلك أيضًا ما فيه الإفراط كله خير كالعلم. وقد قيل في قولهم وهذا إنما يوجد في قليل من الأمور أن المراد منه هو أن بعض الوسائط في الإفراطات والتفريطات ليس بخير مثل القتل فإن الوسط فيه رديء كله والطرف وهو اللا قتل خير كله؛ وليس المراد كذلك بل المراد أن من الشرور ما يوجد له خير يضاده وشر أيضًا يضاده. وذلك لأن هناك طبيعة موضوعة للإفراط والتفريط من أول حدود الإفراط إلى آخر حدود التفريط ذاهبًا باتصال واحد فهناك يوجد متوسط وطرفان في الطبع. ويكون المتوسط أيضًا هو خير يضاد الطرفين اللذين يضاد كل واحد منهما الخير؛ وهذا في قليل من الأمور؛ وليس الحكم في كل الأمور هكذا فإن العلم خير والجهل شر؛ وليس هناك توسط هو خير فطرفان هما شران . وكذلك الحال في كثير من
Section: On what pertains to contrariety from the investigations. And what pertains to contrariety from the investigations is more important relative to others; for in it there are differences among the important, as it was said concerning good and evil: that evil absolutely is contrary to good absolutely, and each evil to each good likewise, like sickness to health and injustice to justice, and so forth. As for the opposition of evil to evil, it may occur; because the middle dispositions (malakat) between excess and deficiency in matters related to appetite and anger, and in particular management in acquiring good, are virtues; and the excesses and deficiencies are vices. For cowardice, recklessness, insensibility, and debauchery are vices; and the means are opposed to the two extremes, and this only occurs in few matters. As for most things, good is absolutely contrary to evil, and there is no evil that is contrary to another evil; of that, some have all excess as bad, like ignorance; and some have all excess as good, like knowledge. And it has been said concerning their statement 'and this only occurs in few matters' that what is meant is that some means in excesses and deficiencies are not good, like killing, for the middle in it is all bad, and the extreme, which is not killing, is all good. But that is not meant; rather, what is meant is that among evils there is that which has a good contrary to it and also an evil contrary to it. That is because there is a nature set for excess and deficiency from the first limit of excess to the last limit of deficiency, proceeding with a single continuity; so there exists a middle and two extremes in nature. And the middle is also a good that is contrary to the two extremes, each of which is contrary to the good; and this is in few matters. And the ruling is not like this in all matters, for knowledge is good and ignorance is evil; and there is no middle that is good, so both extremes are evils. And likewise in many...
Source pages p. 286

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exploration of the nature of contrariety (tadad) specifically applied to good and evil. The author begins by stating that this investigation is important. He cites a common view: absolute evil is the contrary of absolute good, and each particular evil is contrary to a particular good (e.g., sickness vs. health). Then he introduces a special case: evil can be contrary to another evil. This happens in the realm of moral virtues, where virtues are means (wasat) between two extremes of excess (ifrat) and deficiency (tafrit). For example, cowardice (jubn) and recklessness (tahawwur) are two vices, with courage as the mean. The mean is opposed to both extremes, so one extreme (evil) can be contrary to the other extreme (evil). However, this only occurs 'in few matters'. In most cases, good is only contrary to evil (e.g., knowledge vs. ignorance). The author then clarifies a misinterpretation: the phrase 'in few matters' does not mean that some means are not good (like killing, where the mean is all bad and the extreme is good). Rather, it means that there are some evils that have both a good contrary and an evil contrary, due to a continuous nature that admits of a middle and two extremes. This is rare. He gives the example of knowledge (all good) vs. ignorance (all evil), where there is no middle that is good, so both extremes are evils. The passage is cut off, but the argument seems complete in essence.

إفراط

وسط
فضيلة

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in which case does an evil have another evil as its contrary?
A) In all cases, because evil is always opposed to evil.
B) In most cases, since most things have both good and evil opposites.
C) In a few cases involving moral virtues and vices, where a virtue is a mean between two vices.
D) Never; good is always the contrary of evil.

Key Takeaway
In the domain of moral virtues, a good (virtue) is a mean between two evils (vices), making those two evils directly contrary to each other, but this pattern is rare and not universal.

Why Virtue Does Not Essentially Oppose Vice
Author explains that good does not essentially oppose each evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the true essential opposites in the example of courage, cowardice, and recklessness, and explain why virtue does not essentially oppose vice.

Core Idea
Virtue is not the essential opposite of either vice; the two vices oppose each other.

In this passage, the author makes a precise logical point about moral opposites. He argues that a virtuous quality like courage does not directly oppose a vice like cowardice in its essence. Instead, the true opposition is between the two vices (cowardice and recklessness), with courage occupying a middle ground. Understanding this distinction prevents a common misinterpretation of virtue theory.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
Locate where the author states that good (virtue) does not essentially oppose each of the two evils. This is the thesis of the passage.
It appears at the beginning: 'الخير ليس يضاد كل واحد من الشرين بالذات'.
2
Extract the example
Find the example of courage, cowardice, and recklessness. Notice how they are related: courage is the virtue, cowardice and recklessness are the two corresponding vices.
3
Determine the true opposites
The author says the essential opposite of one thing is one. Determine which two of the three are essentially opposed according to the author.
The answer is cowardice and recklessness, not courage and cowardice.
4
Understand the first perspective
The author presents the first way of looking at these qualities: considering their natures without reference to subjects. In this view, courage is intermediate, and the extremes (the two vices) are the true opposites that succeed each other on a single subject.
This is the 'النظر في طبائعها ومعانيها غير مضافة إلى موضوعاتها'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد هذا فنقول ينبغي أن تعلم أن الخير ليس يضاد كل واحد من الشرين بالذات؛ إذ الشجاعة ليست تضاد الجبن من حيث هو جبن بل من حيث تشارك الجبن والتهور في كون كل واحد منهما رذيلة والشجاعة فضيلة؛ فإذن الضد بالذات للواحد واحد وإنما يحصل هذا بالنظر إلى هذه الملكات؛ وذلك على وجهين : أحدهما هو النظر في طبائعها ومعانيها غير مضافة إلى موضوعاتها من حيث إنها تفيد حالا لا يلزم لأجلها محمدة أو مذمة وهو أن ينظر في جملة الطبيعة التي بين الجبن والتهور مارا على الشجاعة من حيث هي ملكات يصدر عنها أفعالا ما؛ فحينئذ لا تكون الشجاعة مضادة لأحد الطرفين بل تكون أمرًا متوسطاً؛ فيكون الطرفان هما المتعاقبان على موضوع واحد وبينهما غاية البعد فيكونان هما الضدين فقط. والثاني هو النظر فيها من حيث الأحوال التي تحصل لموضوعاتها؛ وذلك باعتبار أنها أمور تناسب مصلحة نفس الإنسان أو نوعه أو لإنسانيته. وهذا باعتبار أمر يعرض للكيفيات من جهة إضافات لها؛ وليس في ذواتها كما أن كون الشيء حارا أو باردًا غير كونه موافقا وكون الجسم في طبيعته بحال غير كونه دواء نافعا أو شيئًا قابلا . وإذا اتضح ما قلناه فقد علمتَ أن الكيفية التي يقال لها جبن والتي يقال لها شجاعة لا يضادان في ذاتيهما بل من عارض سمي به أحدهما جبنا والآخر شجاعة ولا يضاد ذلك من حيث طبيعتها بل طبيعتها وسط.
After this we say: you should know that good does not oppose each of the two evils essentially; courage does not oppose cowardice insofar as it is cowardice, but insofar as cowardice and recklessness share that each is a vice and courage is a virtue. Thus the essential opposite of one thing is one, and this occurs only by considering these dispositions. [This is] in two ways: one is considering their natures and meanings without addition to their subjects, insofar as they yield a state for which no praise or blame is necessary, which is to consider the totality of the nature between cowardice and recklessness passing over courage insofar as they are dispositions from which actions proceed; then courage is not opposed to either extreme but is intermediate; thus the two extremes are the ones that succeed each other on one subject, and between them is the utmost distance, so they are the two opposites only. The second is considering them with respect to the states that occur to their subjects, considering that they are things pertaining to the benefit of the human soul or his species or his humanity. This is by considering something that accrues to qualities from the aspect of relations to them; it is not in their essences, just as something being hot or cold is not its being suitable, and the body in its nature being in a state is not its being a useful medicine or a receptive thing. And when what we said is clear, you know that the quality called cowardice and that called courage are not opposed in their essences, but rather from an accident by which one is called cowardice and the other courage, and they are not opposed in terms of their nature, but their nature is intermediate.
Source pages p. 287

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of opposition between moral qualities. The author begins by stating that goodness (virtue) does not essentially oppose each of the two evils (vices). The example given is courage (shaja'a), which does not oppose cowardice (jubn) in itself, but only insofar as cowardice and recklessness (tahawwur) are both vices and courage is a virtue. Thus, the true essential opposition is between the two vices, not between virtue and vice. The author then presents two perspectives (wajhayn) for examining these qualities. The first perspective looks at their innate natures without regard to subjects: courage appears as a mean between two extremes, and the extremes (cowardice and recklessness) are the true opposites that alternate on a single subject. The second perspective considers the effects on the subject: these qualities are evaluated based on benefit or harm to the human soul, species, or humanity. Here, the opposition is accidental, not essential. The author concludes that cowardice and courage are not opposites in their essences; the opposition arises from an accidental attribute (benefit/harm) that labels one as vice and the other as virtue, whereas their natural essence is intermediate.

خير
شر
شجاعة
جبن
تهور

D) Goodness

Key Takeaway
The true essential opposition exists between the two vices (cowardice and recklessness), not between virtue and vice.

Two Ways to Analyze Moral Qualities
Author describes two ways to examine moral qualities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the two perspectives (by nature and by effect) for analyzing moral qualities and apply them to an example from Islamic ethics.

Core Idea
Moral opposition arises accidentally from benefit or harm, not from the intrinsic nature of the qualities.

The author now expands the analysis by presenting two complementary ways of looking at moral qualities. The first considers their intrinsic natures, independent of subjects, revealing the mean structure. The second considers the effects on subjects, where qualities gain moral evaluation due to their benefit or harm. This distinction is vital for understanding how a quality can be neutral in itself yet become praiseworthy or blameworthy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two perspectives
Find where the author introduces 'وجهين' (two ways) and enumerate them. The first is considering natures without subjects, the second is considering states that occur to subjects.
The first is described in the previous part; the second begins at 'والثاني'.
2
Understand the first perspective (nature)
Recall from the previous lesson that by nature, courage is intermediate and the extremes (cowardice and recklessness) are the true opposites. Note that this perspective ignores benefit or harm.
3
Understand the second perspective (effect)
Read the description of the second perspective: qualities are considered with respect to the benefit or harm they bring to the human soul, species, or humanity. This is an accidental attribution.
The author gives an analogy: hot/cold vs. suitable/unsuitable.
4
Conclusion: accidental opposition
The author concludes that cowardice and courage are not opposed in their essences; the opposition arises from an accident (the attribution of benefit/harm) that labels one as vice and the other as virtue.
The natural essences of both are actually intermediate.

Epistemological Foundations of Definitions
Author describes perception, psychic trace, and the invention of language and writing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Islamic epistemological model of perception and the roles of utterance and writing in communicating definitions.

Core Idea
Definitions rest on mental representations that are communicated via utterance and preserved via writing.

Before diving into the technical rules of definition, the author lays the epistemological groundwork. He explains how we perceive external objects, form mental representations (psychic traces), and then communicate those representations through articulated sounds and eventually writing. This foundation is essential because definitions are ultimately about precisely conveying these psychic traces.

Guided Path
1
Trace the perception process
Identify the three stages: sensory faculty imprints external images, then passes to imagination, then imprints in the soul (intellect) in a different manner. This final imprint is intellectual perception.
The author emphasizes that the soul's imprint is not identical to the sensory image but is a 'matching example'.
2
Understand the psychic trace
The psychic trace (al-athar al-nafsānī) is the mental existence of an object, corresponding to its external existence. It is the true object of perception and the basis for statements like 'this exists'.
The psychic trace is the bridge between external reality and human knowledge.
3
Recognize the need for utterance
Humans, being social, needed to communicate these psychic traces. They invented gesture and then utterance (lafẓ), using articulated sounds to indicate the traces.
Utterance is the primary tool, but it has limitations.
4
Identify the role of writing
Utterance alone cannot convey matters about the absent or future. So humans invented writing (kitāba) as a secondary signifier that indicates utterance through its parts and composition.
Writing is derived from philosophical laws and serves where utterance falls short.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أولا بأن للإنسان قوة حسية ترتسم فيها صور الأشياء الخارجية وسالك عنها إلى الخيال فيرتسم فيه ارتساما كذلك إلى أن يرتسم في النفس لا على نحو ما ارتسم في الحس: وهذا هو الإدراك بالعقل على ما عرف بتفاصيل المدركات في العلوم. فللأمور إذن وجود في الأعيان ووجود في الأذهان، وما الموجود في الأذهان وهو المسمى بالأثر النفساني، فذلك مثال مطابق لما هو الموجود في الأعيان ودال عليه. والمدرَك بالحقيقة هو الأثر لما في النفس، حتى إذا قلت هذا موجود أو معدوم كان معناه أن الأثر الحاصل في الذهن له وجود في الخارج أو ليس له وجود فيه. ولما كانت الطبيعة الإنسانية محتاجة إلى المحاورة لاضطرارها إلى المجاورة مثلا انبعثت إلى اختراع شيء يتوصل به إلى ذلك؛ وذلك قد يكون بالإشارة، وقد يكون بالعبارة، فمالت إلى استعمال الصوت وتقطيعه الحروف بآلات هي من عند الخالق الحكيم تبارك وتعالى ليركب منها ما يدل على ما في النفس من الآثار وهو اللفظ. فالألفاظ حكاية عن تلك الآثار ، وإنها لا تكون وافية في كل ما يحتاج إليه من الأمور؛ إذ منها ما يكون في للحال الحاضر مرة وللغائب أخرى. ومنها ما يكون في الاستقبال كذلك فيحتاج إلى اختراع شيء آخر غير ذلك من اللفظ واستنباط ذلك من القوانين الحكمية ليعلم به ما لا يمكن إعلامه باللفظ وذلك الكتابة، فاخترعت أشكالها تهيئ به الهيئة. وإنها أي الكتابة تدل على اللفظ أو يتجزأ بأجزائها وتركيبها أجزاء اللفظ وتركيبه؛ وهذا هو السهولة لا هو. (*) النسخة : وأما الجملة الثانية فإنها في المركبات. (* النسخة والنوع الأول منها فذلك في التعريفات.
Know first that man has a sensory faculty in which images of external things are imprinted, and they pass from it to the imagination and are imprinted there in the same way, until they are imprinted in the soul not in the manner they were imprinted in sense: this is intellectual perception as known through the details of perceptibles in the sciences. So things have existence in external entities and existence in minds, and what exists in minds, called the psychic trace, is a matching example of and indication for what exists in external entities. What is truly perceived is the trace in the soul, so that when you say 'this exists' or 'does not exist', its meaning is that the trace obtained in the mind has existence in the outside or does not have existence there. Since human nature is in need of conversation due to its necessity for association, for example, it was impelled to invent something by which to achieve that; that may be by gesture or by expression; so it inclined to use sound and its division into letters with organs that are from the wise Creator, Blessed and Exalted, to compose from them what indicates the traces in the soul, and that is utterance. So utterances are narrations of those traces, and they are not sufficient for all that is needed among matters; for some of them are for the present time once and for the absent another time, and some are for the future as well. So there is need to invent another thing besides utterance and to derive it from philosophical laws to make known what cannot be made known by utterance, and that is writing. So its forms were invented to prepare the state. And it (writing) indicates utterance, either by its parts and composition corresponding to the parts of utterance and its composition; and this is the ease, not it. (*) The copy: 'As for the second section, it is on composites.' (*) The copy: 'And the first type of that is on definitions.'
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage introduces the section on definitions by laying epistemological and linguistic foundations. The author first describes the process of perception: sensory images are imprinted, then pass to imagination, then to the soul (intellect) in a different manner. This results in a psychic trace (athar nafsani) that corresponds to external objects. Knowledge involves this trace. Humans, being social, need to communicate, so they invent gestures and then articulated sounds (utterances, lafz) to indicate the psychic traces. Utterances are not sufficient for all needs (present, absent, future), so writing (kitaba) is invented as a secondary signifier. Writing indicates utterances through its composition corresponding to the parts of utterance. The footnotes indicate that this is the beginning of the second section on composites and the first type on definitions.
