Definition of the Compound Expression with Four Conditions
Author defines al-lafz al-murakkab with four conditions and answers an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the four conditions in the definition of a compound expression (al-lafz al-murakkab) and apply them to distinguish compound from individual expressions.

Core Idea
Four precise conditions define a compound expression, excluding single words, accidental signs, and parts that only indicate before composition.

In this passage, the author unpacks the technical definition of 'compound expression' (al-lafz al-murakkab) by listing four conditions, each of which excludes a specific counterexample. Understanding these conditions is crucial for correctly identifying logical propositions (qadaya) in subsequent lessons, as well as for analyzing legal statements in usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definiendum
Find the term being defined: al-lafz al-murakkab (also called al-qawl). The author states: 'It is the expression part of which indicates by itself something when it is a part of it, by conventional indication.'
The definition is given at the start of the passage.
2
Extract the four conditions
List the four conditions that modify the definition: (1) it must be an expression (lafz), (2) its parts indicate something, (3) the indication is by itself (not by accident), (4) the indication occurs when the part is actually part of the compound.
The author numbers them with [b], [t], [th] after the initial condition of lafz.
3
Understand the exclusion purpose of each condition
Condition 2 excludes individual words (mufrad) where no part indicates anything. Condition 3 excludes accidental indication (e.g., letters that only signify by being joined). Condition 4 excludes proper nouns like 'Abdullah' where the part 'abd' indicated 'slave' before composition but not after.
The author explicitly states the exclusion for each condition.
4
Analyze the objection and response
An objector says a part cannot indicate 'when it is part of it' because before composition it is not yet a part. The author clarifies that 'part' here means the entity that will become part, not the part in actuality. This prevents infinite regress.
The response uses the example of 'abd' in 'Abdullah' to illustrate the intended meaning.
5
Note the final condition of conventional indication
The phrase 'by conventional indication' (dalala wad'iyya) excludes rational indication (dalala aqliyya), where a part naturally indicates it is less than the whole. This ensures the definition applies only to conventional language.
This is part of the definition itself, not a separate condition, but it is explicitly justified.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في اللفظ المركب ] وأما اللفظ المركب وهو الذي يعبر عنه بالقول فهو اللفظ الذي يدل جزء منه بنفسه على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه دلالة وضعية [1] إما قيد اللفظ فقد مرت إفادته فيما مر [ب] وإما أن أجزاء منه تدل على شيء ما فإنه لإخراج الألفاظ المفردة؛ إذ المفرد من الألفاظ ما لا يدل جزء منه على شيء أصلا، [ت] وإما أنه يدل بنفسه فذلك لإخراج ما يدل بعرضية انضمام الغير إليه. فإن المفرد من الألفاظ وإن لم تكن أجزاؤه دالة بنفسها فقد كانت دالة بعرضية انضمام الغير إليها، [ث] وإما أنه يدل على شيء ما حين هو جزؤه فذلك لإخراج ما يكون جزؤه دالا قبل التركيب لا بعده مثل قولنا عبد الله إذا كان علمًا.

ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه قبل التركيب لا يكون جزءا؛ والكلام في الجزء. لكنا نقول المراد من الجزء هو الذي يوصف بصفة كونه جزءًا نحو العبد في قولنا عبد الله مثلا لا هو مع اتصافه بصفة كونه جزءًا . فإن من المحال أن يكون هو ا بهذه الصفة جزءًا ؛ وإنه يفضي إلى التسلسل. وإن منع وقال: بل المراد من الجزء فيما نحن فيه هو العبد في قولنا / عبد الله لا العبد من حيث هو العبد. فنقول المراد بالجزء وإن كان هذا فلا يفهم منه هو إلا بقولنا حين هو جزؤه. وإن قيل بل يفهم منه من غير أن يراد عليه ذلك : فنقول هب أنه كذلك لكنه لا يفهم منه صريحًا لا يوهم أن يكون المراد هذا أو ذلك. ولو كان كذلك فذلك اللفظ لا يكون زائدا خاليًا عن الفائدة. وأما أنه يدل دلالة وضعية فذلك لإخراج ما يدل على الشيء دلالة عقلية؛ إذ الجزء أي جزء كان في المفرد من الألفاظ أو في المركب منها فإنه يدل على أنه دون الكل دلالة عقلية.
Chapter: On the compound expression. As for the compound expression, which is also called 'al-qawl', it is the expression part of which indicates by itself something when it is a part of it, by conventional indication. [1] The condition 'al-lafz' has already been mentioned previously [b]. [2] The condition 'that parts of it indicate something' serves to exclude individual expressions; for the individual expression is that of which no part indicates anything at all. [3] The condition 'it indicates by itself' serves to exclude what indicates by the accident of something else joining it. For the individual expression, even if its parts do not indicate by themselves, they do indicate by the accident of something else joining them. [4] The condition 'it indicates something when it is part of it' serves to exclude that whose part indicates before composition and not after, like our saying 'Abdullah' when it is a proper noun.

Then there is an objector who says that before composition it is not a part; and we are speaking about the part. But we say that by 'part' is meant that which is described with the attribute of being a part, like 'abd' in our saying 'Abdullah' for example, not that it is described with the attribute of being a part. For it is impossible for it to be a part with this attribute; and that leads to infinite regress. And if he rejects and says: Rather, by 'part' in this discussion is meant 'abd' in our saying 'Abdullah', not 'abd' inasmuch as it is 'abd'? We say: By 'part' even if it is this, it is not understood except by our saying 'when it is part of it'. And if it is said that it is understood without indicating that? We say: Grant that it is so, but it is not explicitly understood, and it does not prevent ambiguity that the intention is this or that. And if it were otherwise, that expression would not be redundant and devoid of benefit. As for 'it indicates by conventional indication', that is to exclude what indicates something by rational indication; for the part, any part whether in an individual expression or a compound one, indicates that it is less than the whole by rational indication.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage defines the compound expression (اللفظ المركب) in a detailed manner with four conditions. The definition: 'the expression part of which indicates by itself something when it is a part of it, by conventional indication.' Each condition is explained:
1. It must be an expression (لفظ) — already established.
2. Its parts must indicate something — this excludes individual (مفرد) words where no part indicates anything.
3. The indication must be by itself (بنفسه) — excludes accidental indication, e.g., letters that indicate only because they are combined into a word.
4. The condition 'when it is part of it' (حين هو جزؤه) — excludes proper nouns like 'Abdullah' where the part 'abd' before composition meant 'slave', but when combined as a proper name, it no longer indicates that meaning.

The author then addresses an objection: if the part is not yet a part before composition, how can we say 'when it is part of it'? The response clarifies that 'part' here refers to the entity that is described as being part (the word 'abd' in the compound), not that it is actually part at that moment. The objection leads to a discussion about infinite regress and ambiguity. Finally, the condition 'conventional indication' (دلالة وضعية) excludes rational indication (دلالة عقلية), which is when a part naturally signifies that it is less than the whole. This definition precisely captures the notion of a compound expression in logic.

Key Terms
لفظ مركب
قول
دلالة وضعية
دلالة عقلية

Checkpoint

A) To exclude individual expressions.

Key Takeaway
The compound expression is precisely defined by four conditions that exclude single words, accidental indications, and parts that indicate only before composition.

The Binary Division of Expressions: Individual vs Compound
Author critiques alternative definition and asserts binary division of expressions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any signifying expression as individual or compound based on the part-indication criterion, and justify why no third category exists.

Core Idea
Part-indication is binary: every expression is either individual or compound.

In Islamic logic, expressions that convey meaning are strictly divided into two categories: individual (mufrad) and compound (murakkab). This lesson examines the author's rejection of an alternative definition and his rational argument that the dichotomy is exhaustive. Understanding this binary division is essential for precise definition and argumentation in the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternative definition being critiqued
Locate where the author presents the alternative definition: 'the expression part of which indicates part of its meaning'. This is contrasted with the definition of the individual expression.
The alternative definition mirrors the definition of individual by negation.
2
Understand the author's grounds for rejection
The author states that the part of an individual expression 'does not indicate anything at all' (لا يدل على شيء ما البتة). Thus, defining the compound as having parts that indicate part of its meaning would be inconsistent with the nature of individual expressions.
The key premise: the part of an individual indicates nothing, so the binary property is exclusive.
3
Grasp the binary division based on part-indication
The author asserts that every signifying expression (لفظ دال) is either individual or compound. This is based on the property: the part of the expression either indicates something (compound) or does not indicate (individual).
This is a rational division (قسمة عقلية) based on a single binary property.
4
Justify the impossibility of a third category
Some might suggest a third category (neither individual nor compound). The author argues that since the two are opposites (the absence of one implies the other), a third is impossible. The final sentence reinforces: the part of an expression either indicates or does not—no middle ground.
This is a logical truth: a binary property cannot yield three categories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد قيل في تعريفه: إنه هو اللفظ الذي يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء»، معناه لما أن تعريفه على حسب تعريف المفرد. فمن عرف المفرد بأنه من الألفاظ ما لا يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء معناه فقد لزمه في تعريف المركب أن يقول يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء»، معناه غير أن الحق هو الأول لما مر من قبل أن جزء المفرد لا يدل على شيء ما البتة. والمركب في مقابلة المفرد؛ إذ اللفظ الدال لا يخلو من أن يكون مفردًا أو مركبا. ولا يقال كيف هو ؛ وإنه ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام قسمة عقلية لما أنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مفردًا أو مركبًا أو لا مفردًا ولا مركبا. فإنه إذا كان أحدهما أعني المفرد أو المركب في معنى عدم صاحبه حتى إذا لم يكن هذا فهو ذلك، وبالعكس؛ فلا يمكن أن يكون لا هذا ولا ذلك، وكيف يمكن ؟ والجزء من اللفظ لا يخلو من أن يدل على شيء ما أو لا يدل.
And it has been said in its definition: It is the expression part of which indicates part of its meaning, because its definition is in accordance with the definition of the individual. So whoever defines the individual as an expression of which no part indicates any part of its meaning, then he is obliged in defining the compound to say: 'part of it indicates part of its meaning'. However, the truth is the first definition, because it has passed that the part of the individual does not indicate anything at all. And the compound is opposite to the individual; for the signifying expression is either individual or compound. And it is not said how is that? And it is divided into three divisions by rational division: because it is either individual, or compound, or neither individual nor compound. For if one of them, i.e., the individual or the compound, is such that the absence of one implies the other, and vice versa, then it is impossible that it be neither this nor that. And how can it be possible? For the part of the expression either indicates something or does not indicate.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage first presents an alternative definition of the compound expression: some define it as an expression where a part indicates a part of its meaning (جزء من أجزاء معناه). This definition is based on contrasting it with the definition of the individual expression: if the individual is defined as that of which no part indicates any part of its meaning, then the compound would be defined by the opposite. The author rejects this alternative, asserting that the first definition (given in the previous lesson) is correct, because the part of an individual expression does not indicate anything at all (لا يدل على شيء ما البتة). Then the author establishes a binary division: every signifying expression (لفظ دال) is either individual (مفرد) or compound (مركب). Some might object by suggesting a third possibility (neither individual nor compound), but the author refutes this with a rational division (قسمة عقلية): since the two categories are mutually exclusive and exhaustive (the absence of one implies the other), a third is impossible. The final sentence reinforces the principle: a part of an expression either indicates something or does not indicate, which corresponds to compound and individual respectively, leaving no room for a middle category. This argument ensures a clear logical dichotomy.

Definition of Compound and the Objection about Parts
Author defines compound and refutes an objection regarding the part before composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the definition of a compound expression and defend it against the objection that the part before composition is not a part.

Core Idea
A part in a compound is defined by its function after composition, not by its isolated existence.

In this passage, the author defines the compound expression (المُرَكَّب) as one whose part indicates part of its meaning. He immediately anticipates an objection: if the part is considered before it enters the compound, it is not yet a part. Through a careful dialectical exchange, he clarifies that the 'part' in the definition refers to the element as it functions within the compound, not as an isolated word. This lesson unpacks the definition and the author's response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find where the author defines the compound (المُرَكَّب). The definition appears after 'وقد قيل في تعريفه'.
The definition is: 'اللفظ الذي يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء معناه' – an expression whose part indicates part of its meaning.
2
Understand the objection
The objector says that before composition, the part is not a part. This challenges the definition because if the part is defined by its being a part, then it must already be a part before composition to serve as a constituent.
The objection is: 'قبل التركيب لا يكون جزءا' – before composition it is not a part.
3
Analyze the author's initial response
The author distinguishes between the part in itself and the part as qualified by being a part. The part meant is 'الذي يوصف بصفة كونه جزءًا' – that which is described with the attribute of being a part.
He gives the example of 'عبد' in 'عبد الله'. The word 'عبد' itself is not a part; it is a part only when considered as a constituent of the compound.
4
Examine the further dialectic
If the objector insists that the part is simply the word 'عبد' without the relational attribute, the author responds that such a part is only understood as a part when we add 'when it is its part'. Otherwise, the understanding is not explicit and leads to redundancy.
This shows the precision required in logical definitions.
5
Compare the definitions of simple and compound
The author notes that the definition of compound is parallel to that of simple. The simple is 'ما لا يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء معناه' (whose part does not indicate part of its meaning). The compound is the opposite.
This contrast helps clarify the concept of compound.

ثم لقائل أن يقول إنه قبل التركيب لا يكون جزءا؛ والكلام في الجزء. لكنا نقول المراد من الجزء هو الذي يوصف بصفة كونه جزءًا نحو العبد في قولنا عبد الله مثلا لا هو مع اتصافه بصفة كونه جزءًا . فإن من المحال أن يكون هو بهذه الصفة جزءًا ؛ وإنه يفضي إلى التسلسل. وإن منع وقال: بل المراد من الجزء فيما نحن فيه هو العبد في قولنا عبد الله لا العبد من حيث هو العبد. فنقول المراد بالجزء وإن كان هذا فلا يفهم منه هو إلا بقولنا حين هو جزؤه. وإن قيل بل يفهم منه من غير أن يراد عليه ذلك : فنقول هب أنه كذلك لكنه لا يفهم منه صريحًا لا يوهم أن يكون المراد هذا أو ذلك. ولو كان كذلك فذلك اللفظ لا يكون زائدا خاليًا عن الفائدة. وأما أنه يدل دلالة وضعية فذلك لإخراج ما يدل على الشيء دلالة عقلية؛ إذ الجزء أي جزء كان في المفرد من الألفاظ أو في المركب منها فإنه يدل على أنه دون الكل دلالة عقلية. وقد قيل في تعريفه: إنه هو اللفظ الذي يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء»، معناه لما أن تعريفه على حسب تعريف المفرد. فمن عرف المفرد بأنه من الألفاظ ما لا يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء معناه فقد لزمه في تعريف المركب أن يقول يدل جزء منه على جزء من أجزاء»، معناه غير أن الحق هو الأول لما مر من قبل أن جزء المفرد لا يدل على شيء ما البتة. والمركب في مقابلة المفرد؛ إذ اللفظ الدال لا يخلو من أن يكون مفردًا أو مركبا. ولا يقال كيف هو ؛ وإنه ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام قسمة عقلية لما أنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مفردًا أو مركبا أو لا مفردًا ولا مركبا. فإنه إذا كان أحدهما أعني المفرد أو المركب في معنى عدم صاحبه حتى إذا لم يكن هذا فهو ذلك، وبالعكس؛ فلا يمكن أن يكون لا هذا ولا ذلك، وكيف يمكن ؟ والجزء من اللفظ لا يخلو من أن يدل على شيء ما أو لا يدل.
Then an objector may say that before composition it is not a part; and the discussion is about the part. But we say: what is meant by 'part' is that which is described with the attribute of being a part, like 'abd in our saying 'Abdullah', for example, not it along with its being described with the attribute of being a part. For it is impossible that it be a part while having this attribute; and that leads to infinite regress. And if he objects and says: rather what is meant by 'part' in our subject is 'abd in our saying 'Abdullah', not 'abd insofar as it is 'abd. Then we say: even if what is meant by 'part' is this, it is not understood from it except by our saying 'when it is its part'. And if it is said: rather it is understood from it without that intention, then we say: suppose that is so, but it is not understood explicitly without implying that the intended meaning is this or that. And if that were the case, then that word would be redundant and devoid of benefit. As for its indicating by conventional indication, that is to exclude what indicates a thing by rational indication; for a part, any part, whether in a simple expression or a compound one, rationally indicates that it is less than the whole. And it has been said in its definition: it is the expression whose part indicates part of its meaning – meaning that its definition is in accordance with the definition of the simple. For whoever defined the simple as 'that expression whose part does not indicate part of its meaning' must necessarily say in defining the compound: 'its part indicates part of its meaning' – except that the truth is the first, because it has passed before that the part of a simple indicates absolutely nothing. And the compound is opposite the simple; for the indicating expression is either simple or compound. And do not say: how is that? And it is divided into three divisions by rational division, because it cannot be other than simple, compound, or neither simple nor compound. For if one of them, I mean the simple or the compound, is in the sense of the absence of the other such that if it is not this then it is that, and vice versa, then it is impossible that it be neither this nor that. And how could it be? And the part of an expression cannot but either indicate something or not indicate.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and dialectical explanation of the term 'compound' (مركب) as used in logic. The author begins by addressing an objection: a part of a compound, considered before composition, is not actually a part; the discussion concerns the part after composition. The author clarifies that 'part' here means the element qualified as being a part within the compound, like 'abd in 'Abdullah', not the word 'abd considered in itself. If the objector insists that the part is simply the word 'abd without its relational attribute, the author responds that such a part is only understood as a part when we say 'when it is its part' – i.e., its part-hood is not self-evident from the mere word. If one argues that it is understood without that, the author grants it but notes that the understanding is not explicit and could lead to ambiguity, making the definition redundant. The author then distinguishes between conventional (وضعية) and rational (عقلية) indication: a part, whether in a simple or compound, rationally indicates that it is less than the whole. The definition of compound is then given: an expression whose part indicates part of its meaning. This definition parallels the definition of simple (whose part does not indicate part of its meaning). The author notes that the correct view is the first (the simple's part indicates nothing) because the part of a simple, like 'ب' in 'باء', has no independent meaning. Finally, the author argues that compound and simple are mutually exclusive and exhaustive; there is no third category, because any expression is either one or the other. The discussion uses examples from Arabic grammar and logic to clarify the concept of parts and composition.

Key Terms
جزء
مركب
مفرد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the definition of a compound (مركب) expression?
A) An expression whose part indicates the whole meaning.
B) An expression whose part indicates part of its meaning.
C) An expression that consists of two or more words.
D) An expression that is not simple.

Key Takeaway
The compound is defined by the part indicating part of its meaning, and that part must be considered after composition to avoid logical infinity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition of 'صلاة' (prayer) as a compound of intention, takbir, recitation, etc. Explain how each part indicates part of the meaning.
?

Division of Expression into Simple and Compound
Author divides expressions into simple and compound and argues against a third category.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why every expression is either simple or compound and refute the possibility of a third category.

Core Idea
Simple and compound are mutually exclusive and exhaustive; there is no third kind of expression.

After defining the compound, the author turns to the division of expressions. He argues that any indicating expression must be either simple or compound. He then considers the rational division into three categories—simple, compound, or neither—and shows that the third is impossible. This lesson examines the reasoning behind the binary classification.

Guided Path
1
Recall the two definitions
Simple: its part indicates nothing. Compound: its part indicates part of its meaning. These are opposites.
The author says: 'المركب في مقابلة المفرد' – the compound is opposite the simple.
2
Understand the rational division
The author considers a division into three: simple, compound, or neither. He asserts that the third is impossible because simple and compound are complementary in the sense that if one is absent, the other is present.
The phrase: 'وإنه ينقسم إلى ثلاثة أقسام قسمة عقلية' refers to a rational division into three.
3
Analyze the argument for no third category
If an expression is not simple, then its part must indicate something, making it compound. Conversely, if it is not compound, its part indicates nothing, making it simple. Therefore, there is no middle ground.
The author says: 'فإنه إذا كان أحدهما ... في معنى عدم صاحبه ... فلا يمكن أن يكون لا هذا ولا ذلك'.
4
Apply the classification to any expression
Take any Arabic word or phrase. Determine whether any part of it (like a letter or a word) indicates something independently. For example, the letter 'ب' in 'بسم' does not indicate part of the meaning, so it is simple. The word 'عبد' in 'عبد الله' indicates part of the meaning, so 'عبد الله' is compound.
This exercise reinforces the binary division.

Compound Expressions and Convention
Author explains convention in compounds and introduces classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why compound expressions indicate by convention while the composition itself is not subject to convention, using examples from Arabic.

Core Idea
Compounds indicate by convention like single words, but composition order is language-specific.

Before diving into types, the author clarifies a foundational point: compound expressions, just like single words, rely on convention (tawāṭuʾ) for meaning. Yet the very act of composing words in a specific order is not itself conventional, since word order varies across languages. This lesson unpacks that subtle distinction, which prepares you for the classifications that follow.

Guided Path
1
Recall convention for single expressions
The author states that compound expressions have the same ruling as single ones regarding convention. Review what convention means: meaning assigned by agreement, not by nature.
Convention was explained earlier in the source.
2
Identify the author's objection
The author anticipates a question: how can compounds be conventional if their composition is not? He argues that the generated meaning from combined parts is conventional, but the form of composition itself is not.
3
Analyze cross-linguistic variation
The author gives examples: the genitive (idafa) can be delayed in one language and advanced in another, and subject-predicate order also varies. This proves composition form is not fixed by convention.
Think of Arabic vs. English: in Arabic, the genitive follows the head noun; in English, it often precedes.
4
Connect to the need for classification
Because compounds have both conventional meaning and variable structure, we need a systematic classification based on wording and meaning. This sets the stage for the rest of the passage.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المركب من الألفاظ حكمه حكم المفرد منها في أنه لا يدل من حيث هو هو إلا بالتواطؤ ؛ وقد مرّ معنى التواطؤ من قبل. ولا يقال كيف هو والتأليف بين الألفاظ المفردة على هيئة مخصوصة لا يكون بالتواطؤ بل المعنى الذي يتولد من المعاني المفردة يقتضي ذلك بعينه فإن التغير في المفرد الذي منه غيره مما يقتضي التغير في الغير بالتواطؤ أيضًا. فأما نفس التركيب فلا يمكن أن يكون بالتواطؤ لاستحالة التغير فيه وإن كانت هيئة التركيب تغيرت بحسب لغة لغةً؛ فإن المضاف إليه مثلا يؤخر في لغة ويقدم في لغة. وكذلك الموضوع والمحمول فلا ضرورة لترتيبها في الطبع. وما قيل في الفرق بين المركب والمؤلف فقد مرَّ في بيان المفرد. ولما كانت المركبات محتاجًا إليها لدلالتها على ما هو في النفس من المعاني. وإنها متركبة من المفردات لا محالة فكانت مختلفة تارة بحسب اللفظ لاختلاف الألفاظ المفردة تارة بحسب المعنى لاختلاف المعاني الموجودة في النفس : أما بحسب اللفظ فإن منها ما يكون تاما؛ أي تام الدلالة فهو الذي يدل كل جزء منه بانفراده على معنى مستقل بنفسه كقولك كاتب «زيد و زيد كاتب مثلا، ومنها ما يكون ناقصا؛ / ناقص الدلالة وهو الذي لا يدل جزء منه بانفراده كما يدل في التام مثل قولك زيد كان إذا كان في شك أن يقول كان مريضًا لا أنه كان في ذاته فإن كان مما يتم دلالته حينئذ، وأما الحصر فيهما فظاهر. وأما بحسب المعنى، فإن منها ما يكون تقييديا، وهو الذي يتركب على طريق الحدود والرسوم بأن يكون بعض أجزائه مقيدًا بالبعض، فيصلح أن يورد بين أجزائه لفظة «الذي»، كقولنا الحيوان الناطق». فإنه يصلح أن يقال فيه الحيوان الذي هو الناطق»، وإنه أي التقييدي في قوة لفظ مفرد يعني يمكن أن يقوم مقامه مفرد يفهم منه ما يفهم من ذلك، نحو قولنا الإنسان فإنه يقوم مقام قولنا حيوان ناطق. ومنها ما يكون خبريا وهو الذي يكون قابلا للصدق والكذب بالفعل أي بالتصريح كقولنا العالم حادث مثلا. ومنها ما لا يكون خبريَّا؛ بل في قوة الخبري، وذلك هو المحرف عنه في الحقيقة ويسمى تنبيها نحو التمني والترجي والقسم والنداء، فإنك إذا قلت ليتك تأتيني، أستفيد من هذا أنك مُريد لإتيانه. ومنها ما لا يكون خبريا ولا في قوة الخبري كذلك، وذلك على قسمين : مثل الاستفهام والاستعلام ومثل الأمر والنهي ونحو ذلك. وأما بيان الحصر في هذه الأقسام فذلك أن الغرض من القول هو الدلالة على ما في النفس، والدلالة إما أن تراد لذاتها، وإما أن لا تراد لذاتها بل لشيء آخر يتوقع من الغير : والتي لذاتها فهي إما للتصور وهو الأول من الأقسام، وإما للتصديق وذلك إما على وجه وهو الثاني منها، وإما بالتحريف وهو الثالث. والتي تراد لشيء آخر فإما أن يكون ذلك الشيء دلالة أيضًا وهو الرابع، أو فعلا وهو الخامس. ويمكن أن يعبر هذا بعبارة أخرى كما يقال تركيب الألفاظ واستعمالا لتعريف المعاني المدلول عليها بحسب لغة لغة: وذلك إما لتصور ماهية الشيء كما هي أو لتمييزه عن الغير وهو الأول، أو لا للتصور فقط بل للحكم على ذلك الشيء إما صريحًا وهو الثاني، أو لا صريحًا بل محرفًا عن التصريح وهو الثالث، أو لا للتصور ولا للحكم كذلك لا صريحًا ومحرفًا بل لطلب الشيء عن الغير وهو الرابع والمطلوب فيه إما نفس الماهية وهو الاستفهام أو الفعل الذي يصدر عنه. وذلك إن كان بطريق الاستعلاء فهو . الأمر والنهي بهذا فظهر الفرق بين قولنا ما الزوج وبين قولنا أفهمني ماهية الزوج؛ إذ الأول طلب الماهية والثاني طلب الفعل وهو إفهام تلك الماهية. وأما الفرق بين العلو والاستعلاء، فظاهر لما أن الاستعلاء قد يوجد / من
Then the compound of expressions has the same ruling as the single one in that it does not indicate, in itself, except by convention; and the meaning of convention has already passed. And it cannot be said how is that, for the composition of single expressions in a specific form is not by convention, rather the meaning that is generated from the single meanings necessitates that very thing. For the change in the single from which another comes necessitates change in the other by convention as well. As for the composition itself, it cannot be by convention because of the impossibility of change in it, even if the form of composition changes according to language; for the genitive, for example, is delayed in one language and brought forward in another, and likewise the subject and predicate; there is no necessity for their order in nature. And what was said about the difference between the compound and the composite has already passed in the explanation of the single. And since compounds are needed for their indication of the meanings in the soul, and they are necessarily composed from single expressions, they differ sometimes in terms of wording due to the difference of single expressions, sometimes in terms of meaning due to the difference of meanings present in the soul. As for in terms of wording, some of them are complete, i.e., complete in indication, which is that each part of it individually indicates an independent meaning by itself, like your saying 'Zaid is writing' and 'Zaid is writing' for example, and some of them are incomplete, i.e., incomplete in indication, which is that a part of it does not indicate individually as it indicates in the complete, like your saying 'Zaid was' when in doubt you say 'he was sick' not that he was in himself; then if it becomes complete in its indication at that time. And as for the exclusivity of the two, it is apparent. And as for in terms of meaning, some of them are restrictive, which is composed in the manner of definitions and descriptions, such that some of its parts are restricted by others, so that the word 'which' can be inserted between its parts, like our saying 'the rational animal'; for it is permissible to say in it 'the animal which is rational', and it, i.e., the restrictive, is in the power of a single expression, meaning that a single expression can take its place that conveys what is understood from it, like our saying 'man', for it takes the place of our saying 'rational animal'. And some of them are declarative, which is susceptible to truth and falsity actually, i.e., explicitly, like our saying 'the world is originated' for example. And some of them are not declarative but rather in the power of the declarative, and that is what is actually shifted and called suggestive, like wishing, hoping, oath, and call; for if you say 'would that you come to me', I understand from this that you desire his coming. And some of them are not declarative nor in the power of the declarative like that, and these are of two types: like interrogation and inquiry, and like command and prohibition and the like. And as for the explanation of the exclusivity in these sections, that is because the purpose of speech is indication of what is in the soul, and indication is either intended for itself or not intended for itself but for something else expected from the other. That which is for itself is either for concept, which is the first of the sections, or for assent, and that is either in one way, which is the second of them, or by shifting, which is the third. And that which is intended for something else is either that that thing is also an indication, which is the fourth, or an action, which is the fifth. And this can be expressed in another phrase as is said: The composition of expressions and their use is to define the meanings indicated according to language: that is either to conceive the quiddity of a thing as it is or to distinguish it from others, which is the first; or not only for concept but for judgment on that thing, either explicitly, which is the second, or not explicitly but shifted from explicitness, which is the third; or not for concept nor for judgment like that, neither explicit nor shifted, but to seek the thing from the other, which is the fourth, and what is sought in it is either the quiddity itself, which is interrogation, or the action that emanates from him. And that if it is in a manner of superiority, then it is command and prohibition. With this the difference appears between our saying 'what is the spouse?' and our saying 'make me understand the quiddity of the spouse', for the first is seeking the quiddity and the second is seeking the action, which is making understand that quiddity. And as for the difference between superiority and assuming superiority, it is apparent, because assuming superiority may exist from ...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of compound expressions (al-murakkab min al-alfāẓ) by the author of Mantiq Kabir. The author begins by reiterating that compounds, like single expressions, indicate by convention (bi-al-tawāṭuʾ), but clarifies that the composition itself is not by convention despite changes in form across languages. Then he divides compounds in two ways: by wording (lafẓ) and by meaning (maʿnā). By wording, there are two types: complete (tāmm), where each part independently indicates a complete meaning (like 'Zaid is writing'), and incomplete (nāqiṣ), where parts do not so indicate (like 'Zaid was' alone). By meaning, there are five types: (1) restrictive (taqyīdī), composed like definitions (e.g., 'rational animal'), which can be replaced by a single word like 'man'; (2) declarative (khabarī), which is true or false (e.g., 'the world is originated'); (3) what is in the power of declarative but not actually declarative, called suggestive (tanbīhī), like wishes and oaths that imply a meaning; (4) and (5) what is neither declarative nor in its power, divided into seeking quiddity (like interrogation) and seeking action (like command and prohibition). The author then justifies this fivefold division by analyzing the purpose of speech: indication intended for itself (for concept or assent, with shifted assent as a third) or for something else (another indication or an action). He restates this in terms of composition and use: either for conceiving quiddity (type 1), judging explicitly (type 2), judging figuratively (type 3), or seeking quiddity (type 4a) or action (type 4b/5). The passage ends with a distinction between 'highness' (ʿuluw) and 'assuming superiority' (istiʿlāʾ), relevant to commands. Throughout, the author uses examples from Arabic language (e.g., 'Zaid is writing', 'rational animal') and an Islamic theology example ('the world is originated') — the latter is a classic mutakallimūn example. The commentary should note that the five meaning-based categories correspond roughly to: definitions, propositions, emotional speech, questions, and imperatives.

Key Terms
المركب
التواطؤ

Key Takeaway
Compounds share conventional indication with single expressions, but the syntactic form of composition is not conventional, as evidenced by cross-linguistic variation.

Classification by Wording: Complete vs. Incomplete
Author classifies compounds by wording into complete and incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify complete and incomplete compound expressions by the criterion of whether each part independently indicates a complete meaning.

Core Idea
A complete compound gives a full meaning with each part independently signifying.

The author first divides compounds by wording (lafẓ) into two types: complete (tāmm) and incomplete (nāqiṣ). This is a simple but essential distinction that hinges on whether each part of the compound individually conveys an independent meaning. Let's unpack this with clear examples from the text.

Guided Path
1
Define complete compound (tāmm)
A complete compound is one where every part individually indicates an independent meaning. The author gives the example 'Zaid is writing' (زيد كاتب or زيد كاتب). Each word has its own meaning and together they form a complete statement.
Note that the example can be either noun-adjective or subject-predicate; both are complete.
2
Define incomplete compound (nāqiṣ)
An incomplete compound is one where the parts do not individually indicate a complete meaning without additional context. The example is 'Zaid was' (زيد كان) alone, which feels incomplete because we expect a complement like 'sick'.
The author says you would only say 'Zaid was' if you are in doubt and about to add 'he was sick'.
3
Apply the criterion to new examples
Test other phrases: 'the book' (الكتاب) is a single word, not a compound. 'The book is new' (الكتاب جديد) is a complete compound because each part contributes. 'If Zaid' (إن زيد) is incomplete without a following clause.
4
Understand the exclusivity of the two types
The author says 'the exclusivity of the two is apparent' meaning there are no other wording-based types. Every compound is either complete or incomplete.
This is a binary division.

Then the compound of expressions has the same ruling as the single one in that it does not indicate, in itself, except by convention; and the meaning of convention has already passed. And it cannot be said how is that, for the composition of single expressions in a specific form is not by convention, rather the meaning that is generated from the single meanings necessitates that very thing. For the change in the single from which another comes necessitates change in the other by convention as well. As for the composition itself, it cannot be by convention because of the impossibility of change in it, even if the form of composition changes according to language; for the genitive, for example, is delayed in one language and brought forward in another, and likewise the subject and predicate; there is no necessity for their order in nature. And what was said about the difference between the compound and the composite has already passed in the explanation of the single. And since compounds are needed for their indication of the meanings in the soul, and they are necessarily composed from single expressions, they differ sometimes in terms of wording due to the difference of single expressions, sometimes in terms of meaning due to the difference of meanings present in the soul. Classification by Meaning: Restrictive and Declarative
Author explains the first two meaning-based categories: restrictive and declarative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish restrictive (taqyīdī) and declarative (khabarī) compounds by their function and structural properties.

Core Idea
Restrictive compounds (like definitions) are replaceable by a single word; declarative compounds are truth-apt.

Moving from wording to meaning, the author identifies five types of compound expressions. The first two — restrictive (taqyīdī) and declarative (khabarī) — correspond to two fundamental purposes of speech: conception and assent. Restrictive compounds function like definitions, while declarative ones express statements that can be true or false.

Guided Path
1
Understand restrictive compounds (taqyīdī)
A restrictive compound is one where one part qualifies another, like a definition. The author says it can include the word 'which' (الذي) between parts. Example: 'rational animal' (الحيوان الناطق) can be rephrased as 'the animal which is rational'.
This type is also called 'definitional' because it is used in definitions (hadd) and descriptions (rasm).
2
Recognize the replaceability property
The key feature: a restrictive compound can be replaced by a single word that conveys the same meaning. 'Rational animal' is replaceable by 'man' (إنسان). This shows its conceptual unity.
Not every restrictive compound has a single-word equivalent in every language, but the author asserts it is in its power.
3
Understand declarative compounds (khabarī)
A declarative compound is one that is actually true or false. Example: 'the world is originated' (العالم حادث). This is a proposition that can be affirmed or denied.
The author emphasizes 'by explicit statement' to distinguish from suggestive compounds (next lesson).
4
Compare the two types
Restrictive compounds aim to define or describe (conceptualisation), while declarative compounds aim to assert a truth (assent). They serve different purposes in logic and discourse.

ومنها ما لا يكون خبريا ولا في قوة الخبري كذلك، وذلك على قسمين : مثل الاستفهام والاستعلام ومثل الأمر والنهي ونحو ذلك. وأما بيان الحصر في هذه الأقسام فذلك أن الغرض من القول هو الدلالة على ما في النفس، الثالث. والتي تراد لشيء آخر فإما أن يكون ذلك الشيء دلالة أيضًا وهو الرابع، أو فعلا وهو الخامس. ويمكن أن يعبر هذا بعبارة أخرى كما يقال تركيب الألفاظ واستعمالا لتعريف المعاني المدلول عليها بحسب لغة لغة: وذلك إما لتصور ماهية الشيء كما هي أو لتمييزه عن الغير وهو الأول، أو لا للتصور فقط بل للحكم على ذلك الشيء إما صريحًا وهو الثاني، أو لا صريحًا بل محرفًا عن التصريح وهو الثالث، أو لا للتصور ولا للحكم كذلك لا صريحًا ومحرفًا بل لطلب الشيء عن الغير وهو الرابع والمطلوب فيه إما نفس الماهية وهو الاستفهام أو الفعل الذي يصدر عنه. وذلك إن كان بطريق الاستعلاء فهو . الأمر والنهي بهذا فظهر الفرق بين قولنا ما الزوج وبين قولنا أفهمني ماهية الزوج؛ إذ الأول طلب الماهية والثاني طلب الفعل وهو إفهام تلك الماهية. وأما الفرق بين العلو والاستعلاء، فظاهر لما أن الاستعلاء قد يوجد / من
Then the compound of expressions has the same ruling as the single one in that it does not indicate, in itself, except by convention; and the meaning of convention has already passed. And it cannot be said how is that, for the composition of single expressions in a specific form is not by convention, rather the meaning that is generated from the single meanings necessitates that very thing. For the change in the single from which another comes necessitates change in the other by convention as well. As for the composition itself, it cannot be by convention because of the impossibility of change in it, even if the form of composition changes according to language; for the genitive, for example, is delayed in one language and brought forward in another, and likewise the subject and predicate; there is no necessity for their order in nature. And what was said about the difference between the compound and the composite has already passed in the explanation of the single. And since compounds are needed for their indication of the meanings in the soul, and they are necessarily composed from single expressions, they differ sometimes in terms of wording due to the difference of single expressions, sometimes in terms of meaning due to the difference of meanings present in the soul. As for in terms of wording, some of them are complete, i.e., complete in indication, which is that each part of it individually indicates an independent meaning by itself, like your saying 'Zaid is writing' and 'Zaid is writing' for example, and some of them are incomplete, i.e., incomplete in indication, which is that a part of it does not indicate individually as it indicates in the complete, like your saying 'Zaid was' when in doubt you say 'he was sick' not that he was in himself; then if it becomes complete in its indication at that time. And as for the exclusivity of the two, it is apparent. And as for in terms of meaning, some of them are restrictive, which is composed in the manner of definitions and descriptions, such that some of its parts are restricted by others, so that the word 'which' can be inserted between its parts, like our saying 'the rational animal'; for it is permissible to say in it 'the animal which is rational', and it, i.e., the restrictive, is in the power of a single expression, meaning that a single expression can take its place that conveys what is understood from it, like our saying 'man', for it takes the place of our saying 'rational animal'. And some of them are declarative, which is susceptible to truth and falsity actually, i.e., explicitly, like our saying 'the world is originated' for example. And some of them are not declarative but rather in the power of the declarative, and that is what is actually shifted and called suggestive, like wishing, hoping, oath, and call; for if you say 'would that you come to me', I understand from this that you desire his coming. And some of them are not declarative nor in the power of the declarative like that, and these are of two types: like interrogation and inquiry, and like command and prohibition and the like. And as for the explanation of the exclusivity in these sections, that is because the purpose of speech is indication of what is in the soul, and indication is either intended for itself or not intended for itself but for something else expected from the other. That which is for itself is either for concept, which is the first of the sections, or for assent, and that is either in one way, which is the second of them, or by shifting, which is the third. And that which is intended for something else is either that that thing is also an indication, which is the fourth, or an action, which is the fifth. And this can be expressed in another phrase as is said: The composition of expressions and their use is to define the meanings indicated according to language: that is either to conceive the quiddity of a thing as it is or to distinguish it from others, which is the first; or not only for concept but for judgment on that thing, either explicitly, which is the second, or not explicitly but shifted from explicitness, which is the third; or not for concept nor for judgment like that, neither explicit nor shifted, but to seek the thing from the other, which is the fourth, and what is sought in it is either the quiddity itself, which is interrogation, or the action that emanates from him. And that if it is in a manner of superiority, then it is command and prohibition. With this the difference appears between our saying 'what is the spouse?' and our saying 'make me understand the quiddity of the spouse', for the first is seeking the quiddity and the second is seeking the action, which is making understand that quiddity. And as for the difference between superiority and assuming superiority, it is apparent, because assuming superiority may exist from ...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of compound expressions (al-murakkab min al-alfāẓ) by the author of Mantiq Kabir. The author begins by reiterating that compounds, like single expressions, indicate by convention (bi-al-tawāṭuʾ), but clarifies that the composition itself is not by convention despite changes in form across languages. Then he divides compounds in two ways: by wording (lafẓ) and by meaning (maʿnā). By wording, there are two types: complete (tāmm), where each part independently indicates a complete meaning (like 'Zaid is writing'), and incomplete (nāqiṣ), where parts do not so indicate (like 'Zaid was' alone). By meaning, there are five types: (1) restrictive (taqyīdī), composed like definitions (e.g., 'rational animal'), which can be replaced by a single word like 'man'; (2) declarative (khabarī), which is true or false (e.g., 'the world is originated'); (3) what is in the power of declarative but not actually declarative, called suggestive (tanbīhī), like wishes and oaths that imply a meaning; (4) and (5) what is neither declarative nor in its power, divided into seeking quiddity (like interrogation) and seeking action (like command and prohibition). The author then justifies this fivefold division by analyzing the purpose of speech: indication intended for itself (for concept or assent, with shifted assent as a third) or for something else (another indication or an action). He restates this in terms of composition and use: either for conceiving quiddity (type 1), judging explicitly (type 2), judging figuratively (type 3), or seeking quiddity (type 4a) or action (type 4b/5). The passage ends with a distinction between 'highness' (ʿuluw) and 'assuming superiority' (istiʿlāʾ), relevant to commands. Throughout, the author uses examples from Arabic language (e.g., 'Zaid is writing', 'rational animal') and an Islamic theology example ('the world is originated') — the latter is a classic mutakallimūn example. The commentary should note that the five meaning-based categories correspond roughly to: definitions, propositions, emotional speech, questions, and imperatives.

الخبري

Key Takeaway
Restrictive compounds are definitional and replaceable by a single word; declarative compounds are truth-apt statements.

Classification by Meaning: Suggestive, Interrogative, and Imperative
Author explains the remaining meaning-based categories: suggestive, interrogative, and imperative.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to differentiate between suggestive (muḥarraf/tanbīhī), interrogative (istifhām), and imperative (amr/nahy) compounds based on their relationship to declarative statements and their purpose.

Core Idea
Not all compounds assert; some imply (suggestive), some ask (interrogative), some command (imperative).

After restrictive and declarative compounds, the author introduces three more meaning-based types: suggestive compounds that imply but don't assert, interrogative compounds that seek quiddity, and imperative compounds that seek action. These complete the picture of how language conveys meaning beyond simple statements.

Guided Path
1
Understand suggestive compounds (muḥarraf/tanbīhī)
These are compounds that are not explicitly declarative but are in the power of a declarative — they imply a meaning without declaring it directly. Examples: wishes (e.g., 'Would that you come'), oaths, calls.
The author says they are 'shifted' (muḥarraf) from explicit declaration.
2
Analyze interrogative compounds (istifhām)
Interrogative compounds seek the quiddity (māhiyya) of something. Example: 'What is the spouse?' (ما الزوج). They are not declarative nor suggestive; they ask for conceptual understanding.
The author distinguishes between seeking quiddity and seeking the action of making understood.
3
Analyze imperative compounds (amr/nahy)
Command and prohibition seek an action from the listener in a manner of superiority (istiʿlāʾ). Example: 'Pray!' (صل). They are not for concept or judgment but for action.
The author notes that commands are not intended for indication of meaning but to elicit action.
4
Note the distinction between seeking quiddity and seeking action
Interrogation asks 'what is X?' seeking the quiddity, while imperatives ask 'do X!' seeking an action. This difference is illustrated by comparing 'What is the spouse?' and 'Make me understand the spouse' — the latter is a request for action (istifhām as a verb).

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of compound expressions (al-murakkab min al-alfāẓ) by the author of Mantiq Kabir. The author begins by reiterating that compounds, like single expressions, indicate by convention (bi-al-tawāṭuʾ), but clarifies that the composition itself is not by convention despite changes in form across languages. Then he divides compounds in two ways: by wording (lafẓ) and by meaning (maʿnā). By wording, there are two types: complete (tāmm), where each part independently indicates a complete meaning (like 'Zaid is writing'), and incomplete (nāqiṣ), where parts do not so indicate (like 'Zaid was' alone). By meaning, there are five types: (1) restrictive (taqyīdī), composed like definitions (e.g., 'rational animal'), which can be replaced by a single word like 'man'; (2) declarative (khabarī), which is true or false (e.g., 'the world is originated'); (3) what is in the power of declarative but not actually declarative, called suggestive (tanbīhī), like wishes and oaths that imply a meaning; (4) and (5) what is neither declarative nor in its power, divided into seeking quiddity (like interrogation) and seeking action (like command and prohibition). The author then justifies this fivefold division by analyzing the purpose of speech: indication intended for itself (for concept or assent, with shifted assent as a third) or for something else (another indication or an action). He restates this in terms of composition and use: either for conceiving quiddity (type 1), judging explicitly (type 2), judging figuratively (type 3), or seeking quiddity (type 4a) or action (type 4b/5). The passage ends with a distinction between 'highness' (ʿuluw) and 'assuming superiority' (istiʿlāʾ), relevant to commands. Throughout, the author uses examples from Arabic language (e.g., 'Zaid is writing', 'rational animal') and an Islamic theology example ('the world is originated') — the latter is a classic mutakallimūn example. The commentary should note that the five meaning-based categories correspond roughly to: definitions, propositions, emotional speech, questions, and imperatives.

The Rationale for the Fivefold Division
Author provides the meta-justification for the fivefold division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that there are exactly five types of compound expressions by meaning, based on the purpose of indication.

Core Idea
The five types are exhaustive because they derive from the fundamental purposes of speech: conception, assent (direct or indirect), seeking knowledge, and seeking action.

The author provides a meta-justification for why there are five meaning-based categories. He argues that the purpose of speech is to indicate what is in the soul. Indication is either intended for itself or for something else. This logical division yields exactly five types. Understanding this rationale allows you to see the completeness of the classification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the overarching division: indication for itself vs. for another
The author says indication is either intended for itself or for something else expected from the hearer. This is the first binary split.
Indication for itself means the speaker wants to convey a meaning; for another means the speaker wants a response.
2
Subdivide indication for itself
Indication for itself is either for concept (taṣawwur) or for assent (taṣdīq). Assent can be explicit (declarative) or shifted (suggestive). This gives three categories: restrictive (concept), declarative (explicit assent), suggestive (shifted assent).
3
Subdivide indication for something else
Indication for another is either for another indication (seeking quiddity – interrogative) or for an action (seeking action – imperative). That gives two more categories.
Thus total: 3 (for itself) + 2 (for another) = 5.
4
Map to the named types
The author restates: (1) for conception of quiddity – restrictive; (2) explicit judgment – declarative; (3) shifted judgment – suggestive; (4) seeking quiddity from another – interrogative; (5) seeking action – imperative.
5
Check the example of 'What is the spouse?' vs 'Make me understand the spouse'
The author uses this to show the difference between seeking quiddity (interrogative) and seeking action (a request for explanation, which is imperative-like). This reinforces the classification.

ثم المركب من الألفاظ حكمه حكم المفرد منها في أنه لا يدل من حيث هو هو إلا بالتواطؤ ؛ وقد مرّ معنى التواطؤ من قبل. ولا يقال كيف هو والتأليف بين الألفاظ المفردة على هيئة مخصوصة لا يكون بالتواطؤ بل المعنى الذي يتولد من المعاني المفردة يقتضي ذلك بعينه فإن التغير في المفرد الذي منه غيره مما يقتضي التغير في الغير بالتواطؤ أيضًا. فأما نفس التركيب فلا يمكن أن يكون بالتواطؤ لاستحالة التغير فيه وإن كانت هيئة التركيب تغيرت بحسب لغة لغةً؛ فإن المضاف إليه مثلا يؤخر في لغة ويقدم في لغة. وكذلك الموضوع والمحمول فلا ضرورة لترتيبها في الطبع. وما قيل في الفرق بين المركب والمؤلف فقد مرَّ في بيان المفرد. ولما كانت المركبات محتاجًا إليها لدلالتها على ما هو في النفس من المعاني. وإنها متركبة من المفردات لا محالة فكانت مختلفة تارة بحسب اللفظ لاختلاف الألفاظ المفردة تارة بحسب المعنى لاختلاف المعاني الموجودة في النفس : أما بحسب اللفظ فإن منها ما يكون تاما؛ أي تام الدلالة فهو الذي يدل كل جزء منه بانفراده على معنى مستقل بنفسه كقولك كاتب «زيد و زيد كاتب مثلا، ومنها ما يكون ناقصا؛ / ناقص الدلالة وهو الذي لا يدل جزء منه بانفراده كما يدل في التام مثل قولك زيد كان إذا كان في شك أن يقول كان مريضًا لا أنه كان في ذاته فإن كان مما يتم دلالته حينئذ، وأما الحصر فيهما فظاهر. وأما بحسب المعنى، فإن منها ما يكون تقييديا، وهو الذي يتركب على طريق الحدود والرسوم بأن يكون بعض أجزائه مقيدًا بالبعض، فيصلح أن يورد بين أجزائه لفظة «الذي»، كقولنا الحيوان الناطق». فإنه يصلح أن يقال فيه الحيوان الذي هو الناطق»، وإنه أي التقييدي في قوة لفظ مفرد يعني يمكن أن يقوم مقامه مفرد يفهم منه ما يفهم من ذلك، نحو قولنا الإنسان فإنه يقوم مقام قولنا حيوان ناطق. ومنها ما يكون خبريا وهو الذي يكون قابلا للصدق والكذب بالفعل أي بالتصريح كقولنا العالم حادث مثلا. ومنها ما لا يكون خبريَّا؛ بل في قوة الخبري، وذلك هو المحرف عنه في الحقيقة ويسمى تنبيها نحو التمني والترجي والقسم والنداء، فإنك إذا قلت ليتك تأتيني، أستفيد من هذا أنك مُريد لإتيانه. ومنها ما لا يكون خبريا ولا في قوة الخبري كذلك، وذلك على قسمين : مثل الاستفهام والاستعلام ومثل الأمر والنهي ونحو ذلك. وأما بيان الحصر في هذه الأقسام فذلك أن الغرض من القول هو الدلالة على ما في النفس، والدلالة إما أن تراد لذاتها، وإما أن لا تراد لذاتها بل لشيء آخر يتوقع من الغير : والتي لذاتها فهي إما للتصور وهو الأول من الأقسام، وإما للتصديق وذلك إما على وجه وهو الثاني منها، وإما بالتحريف وهو الثالث. والتي تراد لشيء آخر فإما أن يكون ذلك الشيء دلالة أيضًا وهو الرابع، أو فعلا وهو الخامس. ويمكن أن يعبر هذا بعبارة أخرى كما يقال تركيب الألفاظ واستعمالا لتعريف المعاني المدلول عليها بحسب لغة لغة: وذلك إما لتصور ماهية الشيء كما هي أو لتمييزه عن الغير وهو الأول، أو لا للتصور فقط بل للحكم على ذلك الشيء إما صريحًا وهو الثاني، أو لا صريحًا بل محرفًا عن التصريح وهو الثالث، أو لا للتصور ولا للحكم كذلك لا صريحًا ومحرفًا بل لطلب الشيء عن الغير وهو الرابع والمطلوب فيه إما نفس الماهية وهو الاستفهام أو الفعل الذي يصدر عنه. وذلك إن كان بطريق الاستعلاء فهو . الأمر والنهي بهذا فظهر الفرق بين قولنا ما الزوج وبين قولنا أفهمني ماهية الزوج؛ إذ الأول طلب الماهية والثاني طلب الفعل وهو إفهام تلك الماهية. وأما الفرق بين العلو والاستعلاء، فظاهر لما أن الاستعلاء قد يوجد / من
Then the compound of expressions has the same ruling as the single one in that it does not indicate, in itself, except by convention; and the meaning of convention has already passed. And it cannot be said how is that, for the composition of single expressions in a specific form is not by convention, rather the meaning that is generated from the single meanings necessitates that very thing. For the change in the single from which another comes necessitates change in the other by convention as well. As for the composition itself, it cannot be by convention because of the impossibility of change in it, even if the form of composition changes according to language; for the genitive, for example, is delayed in one language and brought forward in another, and likewise the subject and predicate; there is no necessity for their order in nature. And what was said about the difference between the compound and the composite has already passed in the explanation of the single. And since compounds are needed for their indication of the meanings in the soul, and they are necessarily composed from single expressions, they differ sometimes in terms of wording due to the difference of single expressions, sometimes in terms of meaning due to the difference of meanings present in the soul. As for in terms of wording, some of them are complete, i.e., complete in indication, which is that each part of it individually indicates an independent meaning by itself, like your saying 'Zaid is writing' and 'Zaid is writing' for example, and some of them are incomplete, i.e., incomplete in indication, which is that a part of it does not indicate individually as it indicates in the complete, like your saying 'Zaid was' when in doubt you say 'he was sick' not that he was in himself; then if it becomes complete in its indication at that time. And as for the exclusivity of the two, it is apparent. And as for in terms of meaning, some of them are restrictive, which is composed in the manner of definitions and descriptions, such that some of its parts are restricted by others, so that the word 'which' can be inserted between its parts, like our saying 'the rational animal'; for it is permissible to say in it 'the animal which is rational', and it, i.e., the restrictive, is in the power of a single expression, meaning that a single expression can take its place that conveys what is understood from it, like our saying 'man', for it takes the place of our saying 'rational animal'. And some of them are declarative, which is susceptible to truth and falsity actually, i.e., explicitly, like our saying 'the world is originated' for example. And some of them are not declarative but rather in the power of the declarative, and that is what is actually shifted and called suggestive, like wishing, hoping, oath, and call; for if you say 'would that you come to me', I understand from this that you desire his coming. And some of them are not declarative nor in the power of the declarative like that, and these are of two types: like interrogation and inquiry, and like command and prohibition and the like. And as for the explanation of the exclusivity in these sections, that is because the purpose of speech is indication of what is in the soul, and indication is either intended for itself or not intended for itself but for something else expected from the other. That which is for itself is either for concept, which is the first of the sections, or for assent, and that is either in one way, which is the second of them, or by shifting, which is the third. And that which is intended for something else is either that that thing is also an indication, which is the fourth, or an action, which is the fifth. And this can be expressed in another phrase as is said: The composition of expressions and their use is to define the meanings indicated according to language: that is either to conceive the quiddity of a thing as it is or to distinguish it from others, which is the first; or not only for concept but for judgment on that thing, either explicitly, which is the second, or not explicitly but shifted from explicitness, which is the third; or not for concept nor for judgment like that, neither explicit nor shifted, but to seek the thing from the other, which is the fourth, and what is sought in it is either the quiddity itself, which is interrogation, or the action that emanates from him. And that if it is in a manner of superiority, then it is command and prohibition. With this the difference appears between our saying 'what is the spouse?' and our saying 'make me understand the quiddity of the spouse', for the first is seeking the quiddity and the second is seeking the action, which is making understand that quiddity. And as for the difference between superiority and assuming superiority, it is apparent, because assuming superiority may exist from ...
Source pages p. 297

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of compound expressions (al-murakkab min al-alfāẓ) by the author of Mantiq Kabir. The author begins by reiterating that compounds, like single expressions, indicate by convention (bi-al-tawāṭuʾ), but clarifies that the composition itself is not by convention despite changes in form across languages. Then he divides compounds in two ways: by wording (lafẓ) and by meaning (maʿnā). By wording, there are two types: complete (tāmm), where each part independently indicates a complete meaning (like 'Zaid is writing'), and incomplete (nāqiṣ), where parts do not so indicate (like 'Zaid was' alone). By meaning, there are five types: (1) restrictive (taqyīdī), composed like definitions (e.g., 'rational animal'), which can be replaced by a single word like 'man'; (2) declarative (khabarī), which is true or false (e.g., 'the world is originated'); (3) what is in the power of declarative but not actually declarative, called suggestive (tanbīhī), like wishes and oaths that imply a meaning; (4) and (5) what is neither declarative nor in its power, divided into seeking quiddity (like interrogation) and seeking action (like command and prohibition). The author then justifies this fivefold division by analyzing the purpose of speech: indication intended for itself (for concept or assent, with shifted assent as a third) or for something else (another indication or an action). He restates this in terms of composition and use: either for conceiving quiddity (type 1), judging explicitly (type 2), judging figuratively (type 3), or seeking quiddity (type 4a) or action (type 4b/5). The passage ends with a distinction between 'highness' (ʿuluw) and 'assuming superiority' (istiʿlāʾ), relevant to commands. Throughout, the author uses examples from Arabic language (e.g., 'Zaid is writing', 'rational animal') and an Islamic theology example ('the world is originated') — the latter is a classic mutakallimūn example. The commentary should note that the five meaning-based categories correspond roughly to: definitions, propositions, emotional speech, questions, and imperatives.

Key Terms
التصور
التصديق
الاستعلاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Three
B) Four
C) Five
D) Six

Key Takeaway
The five meaning-based types are exhaustive because they stem from the fundamental purposes of speech: concept, assent (explicit or implicit), seeking quiddity, and seeking action.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Restrictive and Declarative Compounds: Tools for Conception and Affirmation
Author classifies beneficial compounds and justifies early treatment of definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between restrictive (taqyeedi) and declarative (khabari) compounds and identify their respective roles in the acquisition of conceptions (tasawwurat) and affirmations (tasdiqat).

Core Idea
Restrictive compounds define essences; declarative compounds build proofs.

The author explains that not all compound utterances are equally beneficial in the sciences. Some serve to give us concepts (tasawwurat) through definitions and descriptions, while others serve to give us judgments (tasdiqat) through syllogisms. Understanding this distinction is crucial for accessing the theoretical sciences, as it clarifies the purpose of definitions like hadd and rasm versus demonstrative arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of compounds
Notice that the author divides beneficial compounds into two categories: restrictive (taqyeedi) and declarative (khabari). Restrictive compounds are phrases that qualify or restrict a concept, while declarative compounds are statements that can be true or false.
The terms 'taqyeedi' and 'khabari' are technical in Islamic logic.
2
Match each compound to its function
The author states that restrictive compounds are used to acquire conceptions (tasawwurat) through definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum). Declarative compounds are used to acquire affirmations (tasdiqat) through syllogisms (maqayis).
Conception is the mental grasp of an essence; affirmation is the judgment of truth or falsity.
3
Recognize the explicatory statements
The author calls the statements that define essences 'al-aqwal al-shariha' (explicatory statements). These are a subset of restrictive compounds. They can be a complete definition (hadd) or a description (rasm), or other forms.
Hadd gives the genus and differentia; rasm gives specific properties or accidents.
4
Understand the author’s pedagogical choice
Although earlier scholars postponed the discussion of hadd and rasm to the book on Demonstration (Burhan), the author presents them earlier because definitions are needed in many places before constructing proofs. This shows the practical importance of definitions.
This does not mean that definitions are more important than demonstration; rather, they are foundational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [في النافع من هذه التعريفات ] أما النافع في العلوم من هذه التركيبات التي مرَّ ذكرها، فهو التقييدي أولا لما أن اكتساب التصورات بالحدود والرسوم وما يجري مجراها من المركبات التقييدية والخبري ثانيًا لما أن اكتساب التصديقات بالمقاييس وما يجري مجراها؛ وإنها من المركبات الخبرية. فالأقوال الشارحة إذن، وهي التي بها يحصل تعريف الماهيات إنما هي من المركبات التقييدية، فما يكون من هذه المركبات فذلك من جملة ما يفيد تصور ماهية الشيء بسيطة كانت الماهية أو مركبة. وإنه بطريق لما أن التعريف قد يكون بالحد وقد يكون بالرسم وقد يكون بغيرهما كما ستعرفه من بعد.
ثم العادة وإن كانت جارية بين المتقدمين في تأخير الحد والرسم وكيفية الاقتناص بهما إلى كتاب البرهان؛ ففي التقديم مصالح؛ إذ التعريف محتاج إليه في كثير من المواضع، فتقدم إذن ما هو لا يتم منهما دون غيره؛ إذ ذاك يعرف من ذلك الكتاب.
Chapter: On the beneficial of these definitions. As for what is beneficial in the sciences from these compositions that have been mentioned, it is the restrictive (taqyeedi) first because the acquisition of conceptions (tasawwurat) occurs through definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum) and what is analogous to them from the restrictive compounds; and the declarative (khabari) second because the acquisition of affirmations (tasdiqat) occurs through syllogisms (maqayis) and what is analogous to them; and they are from the declarative compounds. So the explicatory statements (aqwal sharhah), which are those by which the definition of essences (mahiyyat) is obtained, are from the restrictive compounds. So whatever is from these compounds is among what benefits conception of the essence of a thing, whether the essence is simple or composite. And it is in a way because definition may be by hadd (essential definition) or by rasm (descriptive definition) or by other than them, as you will know later.
Then, even though the custom among the earlier scholars was to delay the discussion of hadd, rasm, and the method of capturing them to the book of Demonstration (Burhan), there are benefits in presenting them earlier; because definition is needed in many places, so present then what is not complete without other than it; for that is known from that book.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of compound utterances that are beneficial in the sciences. The author states that the restrictive (taqyeedi) compounds are beneficial for acquiring conceptions through hudud and rusum, while the declarative (khabari) compounds are beneficial for acquiring affirmations through syllogisms. He then emphasizes that the explicatory statements (aqwal sharhah) which define essences belong to the restrictive category. He notes that definition can be by hadd, rasm, or other methods. Finally, he addresses a customary practice: although earlier scholars typically postponed the discussion of hadd and rasm until the book on Demonstration, he chooses to present them earlier because definition is needed in many places. This highlights the pedagogical order of this work.

Key Takeaway
Restrictive compounds define essences; declarative compounds prove truths; both are essential but serve different cognitive functions.

Restrictive vs Declarative Compounds in Tasawwur and Tasdiq
Author discusses the benefit of restrictive and declarative compounds for tasawwur and tasdiq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish restrictive from declarative compounds and associate each with the correct epistemological function (tasawwur vs tasdiq).

Core Idea
Restrictive compounds give conception; declarative compounds give assent.

This passage from a classical logic textbook clarifies a foundational distinction: not all logical compounds serve the same purpose. Some are designed to produce a mental conception of a thing's essence, while others are designed to produce a judgment of truth. Understanding this classification is key to navigating the rest of Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of compounds
Find in the Arabic the terms for restrictive compounds (المركبات التقييدية) and declarative compounds (المركبات الخبرية). Note that they are the beneficial ones in the sciences.
These two categories cover all logical expressions that produce knowledge.
2
Associate each compound with its epistemological product
The text states that restrictive compounds yield conceptions (تصورات) and declarative compounds yield assents (تصديقات). Conception is a mental representation of an essence; assent is a judgment of truth or falsity.
Tasawwur is about understanding what something is; tasdiq is about affirming or denying a proposition.
3
Connect compounds to specific logical tools
Restrictive compounds include definitions (حدود) and descriptions (رسوم). Declarative compounds include syllogisms (مقاييس) and similar arguments.
The author mentions that definitions and descriptions are themselves restrictive compounds.
4
Analyze the author's decision to present hadd and rasm early
Despite earlier custom of postponing hadd and rasm to the Book of Demonstration, the author argues that because definition is needed frequently, it is beneficial to present them earlier.
This shows that logical tools are arranged according to practical need in the curriculum.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [في النافع من هذه التعريفات] أما النافع في العلوم من هذه التركيبات التي مرَّ ذكرها، فهو التقييدي أولا لما أن اكتساب التصورات بالحدود والرسوم وما يجري مجراها من المركبات التقييدية والخبري ثانيًا لما أن اكتساب التصديقات بالمقاييس وما يجري مجراها؛ وإنها من المركبات الخبرية. فالأقوال الشارحة إذن، وهي التي بها يحصل تعريف الماهيات إنما هي من المركبات التقييدية، فما يكون من هذه المركبات فذلك من جملة ما يفيد تصور ماهية الشيء بسيطة كانت الماهية أو مركبة. وإنه بطريق لما أن التعريف قد يكون بالحد وقد يكون بالرسم وقد يكون بغيرهما كما ستعرفه من بعد. ثم العادة وإن كانت جارية بين المتقدمين في تأخير الحد والرسم وكيفية الاقتناص بهما إلى كتاب البرهان؛ ففي التقديم مصالح؛ إذ التعريف محتاج إليه في كثير من المواضع، فتقدم إذن ما هو لا يتم منهما دون غيره؛ إذ ذاك يعرف من ذلك الكتاب.
Section: [On the beneficial among these definitions]. As for the beneficial in the sciences from these compositions that have been mentioned, it is the restrictive first because the acquisition of conceptions (tasawwurat) is through definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum) and what runs in their course from the restrictive compounds, and the declarative second because the acquisition of assents (tasdiqat) is through syllogisms (maqayis) and what runs in their course, and they are from the declarative compounds. So the explicatory statements, which are those by which the definition of quiddities is obtained, are from the restrictive compounds. So whatever is from these compounds is among that which benefits the conception of the quiddity of a thing, whether the quiddity is simple or composite. And it is by way of that definition may be by hadd or by rasm or by other than them as you will know later. Then the custom, although it was current among the earlier scholars to postpone hadd and rasm and the method of capturing them to the Book of Demonstration, yet there are benefits in presenting them, because definition is needed in many places. So we present what is not complete from them without the other, for that will be known from that book.
Source pages p. 299

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the types of logical compounds according to their utility in the sciences. The author begins by distinguishing two main types of compounds that are beneficial: restrictive compounds (al-murakkabat al-taqyidiyya) and declarative compounds (al-murakkabat al-khabariyya). Restrictive compounds are those that yield conceptions (tasawwurat), which are mental representations of quiddities. These include definitions (hudud) and descriptions (rusum) and similar expressions. Declarative compounds, on the other hand, yield assents (tasdiqat), which are judgments of truth or falsity, and these are obtained through syllogisms (maqayis) and similar arguments. The passage then explains that explicatory statements (al-aqwal al-shariha), which define quiddities, fall under restrictive compounds. They benefit the conception of a thing's quiddity, whether that quiddity is simple (like 'existence') or composite (like 'human'). The author notes that definition can be by hadd (essential definition), rasm (descriptive definition), or other methods, to be discussed later. Finally, the passage addresses a customary practice among earlier scholars: they delayed the discussion of hadd and rasm, and how to derive them, to the Book of Demonstration (Kitab al-Burhan). However, the author argues that there are benefits in presenting them earlier because definition is frequently needed. Therefore, he presents what is necessary, noting that the more detailed treatment will be found in that book. This passage thus establishes the epistemological priority of these definitions in the logical curriculum.

تصديق
مركب تقييدي
مركب خبري

B) To produce conceptions
C) To produce definitions
D) To produce syllogisms

Key Takeaway
Restrictive compounds like definitions yield conceptions; declarative compounds like syllogisms yield assents.
