Definitions of Hadd and Complete vs. Incomplete
Multiple definitions of hadd and the complete hadd example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three common definitions of hadd and distinguish a complete hadd from an incomplete one based on the presence of genus and differentia in correct order.

Core Idea
A complete definition pictures the quiddity using both genus and differentia in the right order.

The author opens by listing three definitions of hadd (definition): a statement indicative of quiddity, a statement defining the essence as it is, and a statement picturing quiddity in the soul. The last excludes single words because a statement must be composite. Then the author distinguishes complete hadd from incomplete: the complete one requires a proximate genus and a differentia, arranged with the genus first. Without both, a definition cannot fully capture the quiddity.

Guided Path
1
Extract the three definitions of hadd
Note the three formulations: (a) 'indicative of quiddity', (b) 'defining a totality as it is', (c) 'picturing quiddity in the soul'. Recognize that the third excludes single words because 'statement' implies composition.
2
Identify the condition for completeness
A complete hadd must picture the quiddity in the soul and be 'preceded by its differentia with its proximate genus' – meaning the genus must come before the differentia, and both must be present.
3
Apply the order rule to 'human'
The author gives the example: 'rational animal' (hayawan natiq) is a proper definition because genus precedes differentia; 'animal rational' (natiq hayawan) is not, even though it has both components.
4
Test other examples mentally
Try defining 'tree' as 'plant having a woody stem' – 'plant' is the genus, 'having a woody stem' the differentia, in correct order. If you reversed it, it would not be a proper hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحد فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة : [أ] منها أن يقال هو القول الدال على ماهية الشيء، [ب] ومنها أن يقال قول معرف جملة لشيء واحد هو المحدود تعريفا يعرف منه ذات ذلك الشيء كما هي، [ت] ومنها أن يقال قول مصور ماهية الشيء في النفس. فقولنا قول مصور مما يخرج اللفظ المفرد عن كونه حدا؛ إذ القول هو المركب كما مر . ثم الحد قد يكون كاملا وقد لا يكون بل يكون ناقصا، والكامل هو قول مصور ماهية الشيء في النفس مسبوقا فصله بجنسه القريب وإنه هو المعرف للماهية كما هي؛ فيكون معرفًا لها بجميع ما هو داخل فيها من الأجناس والفصول وهي المقومات على ما عرف. فالحد الكامل مركب من الجنس والفصل لا محالة والمحدود كذلك: فحد الحد الكامل إذن مركب من الجنس والفصل فالجنس هو القول؛ إذ القول هو الجنس القريب والباقي فصله، فقولنا قول مصور فصله بجنسه القريب مما يخرج غيره عن كونه حدا حتى إذا قلتَ في تعريف الإنسان حيوان ناطق فإنك تعرفه تعريفا حدياً بخلاف ما إذا قلت ناطق حيوان، وإن كان هذا مركبا من الجنس والفصل الذي مرَّ ذكرهما في ذلك . ثم الجنس والفصل من اللوازم في الحد الكامل. ولا جنس لما لا فصل له والجنس هو تمام ما به المشاركة، ولا فصل لما لا جنس له كذلك. والفصل هو تمام ما به الممايزة على ما عرف من قبل. فالذي لا جنس له ولا فصل فلا حد له إذن والحد هو التعريف بالمقومات وإنها من الأجناس والفصول كما مر .
As for hadd (definition), it is known by different definitions: [a] among them is that it is said: it is the statement indicative of the quiddity of a thing; [b] and among them is that it is said: a statement defining a totality for one thing which is the defined, a definition by which the essence of that thing is known as it is; [c] and among them is that it is said: a statement picturing the quiddity of a thing in the soul. So our saying 'a statement picturing' removes the single word from being a definition, because 'statement' is composite as has passed. Then hadd may be complete or it may be not, but rather incomplete. And the complete is: a statement picturing the quiddity of a thing in the soul, preceded by its differentia with its proximate genus, and it is that which defines the quiddity as it is. So it is a definer of it by all that is internal to it from genera and differentiae, which are the constituents as known. Thus the complete hadd is composed of genus and differentia necessarily, and the defined likewise. So the definition of the complete hadd is composed of genus and differentia. So the genus is the statement, because the statement is the proximate genus, and the rest is its differentia. So our saying 'a statement picturing its differentia with its proximate genus' removes other than it from being a definition, such that if you say in the definition of 'human': 'rational animal', you define it by a definitional definition, unlike if you say 'animal rational', even though this is composed of genus and differentia that have been mentioned in that. Then the genus and differentia are among the necessities in the complete hadd. There is no genus for that which has no differentia, and the genus is all that by which there is participation. And there is no differentia for that which has no genus likewise. And the differentia is all that by which there is differentiation, as known before. So that which has no genus and no differentia has no definition, and definition is the definition by constituents, and they are the genera and differentiae as has passed.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the concept of hadd (definition) in logic. The author begins by presenting three common definitions of hadd: (a) a statement indicative of the quiddity of a thing, (b) a statement that defines the essence of a thing as it is, and (c) a statement that pictures the quiddity of a thing in the soul. The author notes that the phrase 'statement picturing' excludes a single word from being a definition, because a statement must be composite. Then the discussion moves to the distinction between complete and incomplete hadd. A complete hadd is one that pictures the quiddity of a thing in the soul, preceded by its differentia (fasl) with its proximate genus (jins qarib). This means it includes both the genus and differentia as constituents (muqawwimat). The complete hadd is necessarily composed of genus and differentia, and so is the defined object. The author illustrates this by defining 'human' as 'rational animal' (hayawan natiq), which is a proper essential definition, as opposed to 'animal rational' (natiq hayawan), which, though containing the same components, is not in the correct order for a definition (since the genus should precede the differentia). The passage then explains that genus and differentia are necessary (lazim) for a complete hadd. Whatever has no differentia has no genus, and whatever has no genus has no differentia. The genus is that by which a thing participates with others, and the differentia is that by which it is distinguished. Therefore, anything without both genus and differentia cannot have a definition. The conclusion is that definition (hadd) is definition by constituents (muqawwimat), which are genera and differentiae. This passage is crucial for understanding the structure of essential definitions in Islamic logic.

Necessity of Genus and Differentia in Hadd
Necessity of genus and differentia; definition by constituents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why genus and differentia are necessary for any complete hadd and identify whether a given thing can have a definition based on whether it possesses both.

Core Idea
Without genus or differentia, no definition is possible.

After explaining the structure of a complete hadd, the author asserts that genus and differentia are necessary (lazim) for it. The genus is that by which a thing participates with others; the differentia is that by which it is distinguished. Whatever lacks one lacks the other, and what lacks both cannot be defined. This establishes the foundation that definition is always by means of the constituents (muqawwimat) which are genera and differentiae.

Guided Path
1
Understand the roles of genus and differentia
The genus is 'that by which participation occurs' – it places the thing in a broader category. The differentia is 'that by which differentiation occurs' – it distinguishes the thing from other members of that category.
2
Recognize the mutual dependence
The passage says: 'there is no genus for that which has no differentia, and no differentia for that which has no genus'. This means if a thing lacks a differentia, it cannot have a genus either, and vice versa.
3
Apply the rule to a hypothetical thing
Consider a simple concept like 'existence' (wujud) – does it have a genus and differentia? According to the rule, it would not, so it cannot be defined by a complete hadd.
4
Conclude that definition is by constituents
The author ends by stating that definition (hadd) is definition by the muqawwimat (constituents), which are genera and differentiae. So if a thing has no constituents (no genus/differentia), it has no definition.

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed exposition of the concept of hadd (definition) in logic. The author begins by presenting three common definitions of hadd: (a) a statement indicative of the quiddity of a thing, (b) a statement that defines the essence of a thing as it is, and (c) a statement that pictures the quiddity of a thing in the soul. The author notes that the phrase 'statement picturing' excludes a single word from being a definition, because a statement must be composite. Then the discussion moves to the distinction between complete and incomplete hadd. This means it includes both the genus and differentia as constituents (muqawwimat). The complete hadd is necessarily composed of genus and differentia, and so is the defined object. The author illustrates this by defining 'human' as 'rational animal' (hayawan natiq), which is a proper essential definition, as opposed to 'animal rational' (natiq hayawan), which, though containing the same components, is not in the correct order for a definition (since the genus should precede the differentia). The passage then explains that genus and differentia are necessary (lazim) for a complete hadd. Whatever has no differentia has no genus, and whatever has no genus has no differentia. The genus is that by which a thing participates with others, and the differentia is that by which it is distinguished. Therefore, anything without both genus and differentia cannot have a definition. The conclusion is that definition (hadd) is definition by constituents (muqawwimat), which are genera and differentiae. This passage is crucial for understanding the structure of essential definitions in Islamic logic.

Components of a Complete Definition (Hadd Kamil)
Author lists various definitions of hadd and explains the complete hadd composed of genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia from any given definition and determine whether it is complete or incomplete.

Core Idea
Complete definition = genus + differentia: the mirror of quiddity.

This passage introduces the concept of 'hadd' (definition) in Islamic logic. The author lists three definitions and then focuses on the structure of a complete definition, which must include both genus (jins) and differentia (fasl). Understanding this structure is essential for evaluating any definition in the Islamic sciences, from fiqh to kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions of hadd
Read the Arabic and find the three definitions listed. Note that they all share the requirement that a definition is a composite statement (qawl) indicating the quiddity.
The definitions are marked with [a], [b], [c].
2
Understand the requirement of composition
The author excludes single words from being definitions. A definition must be a composite statement, because it must combine genus and differentia to capture the essence.
The term 'qawl' here means a composite statement, not a single word.
3
Distinguish complete from incomplete hadd
A complete definition is one that forms the quiddity with its differentia preceded by its proximate genus. It reveals the essence fully. An incomplete definition lacks this structure.
The complete definition uses the proximate genus, not a distant one.
4
Analyze the example 'rational animal'
In the definition of human, 'animal' is the genus and 'rational' is the differentia. The order matters: 'rational animal' is a complete definition, while 'animal rational' is not because the differentia should follow the genus? Actually the text says 'rational animal' is correct, 'animal rational' is also composed but not the preferred order? The text says 'if you say rational animal you define it definitionally, unlike if you said animal rational'. So the order of presentation is important for completeness.
The genus must precede the differentia in the complete definition.
5
Extract the rule about genus and differentia
The passage states that nothing lacking a genus or differentia can have a definition. Genus accounts for participation, differentia for distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحد فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة : [أ] منها أن يقال هو القول الدال على ماهية الشيء، [ب] ومنها أن يقال قول معرف جملة لشيء واحد هو المحدود تعريفا يعرف منه ذات ذلك الشيء كما هي، [ت] ومنها أن يقال قول مصور ماهية الشيء في النفس. فقولنا قول مصور مما يخرج اللفظ المفرد عن كونه حدا؛ إذ القول هو المركب كما مر . ثم الحد قد يكون كاملا وقد لا يكون بل يكون ناقصا، والكامل هو قول مصور ماهية الشيء في النفس مسبوقا فصله بجنسه القريب وإنه هو المعرف للماهية كما هي؛ فيكون معرفًا لها بجميع ما هو داخل فيها من الأجناس والفصول وهي المقومات على ما عرف. فالحد الكامل مركب من الجنس والفصل لا محالة والمحدود كذلك: فحد الحد الكامل إذن مركب من الجنس والفصل فالجنس هو القول؛ إذ القول هو الجنس القريب والباقي فصله، فقولنا قا فصله بجنسه القريب مما يخرج غيره عن كونه حدا حتى إذا قلتَ في تعريف الإنسان حيوان ناطق فإنك تعرفه تعريفا حدياً بخلاف ما إذا قلت ناطق حيوان، وإن كان هذا مركبا من الجنس والفصل الذي مرَّ ذكرهما في ذلك . ثم الجنس والفصل من اللوازم في الحد الكامل. ولا جنس لما لا فصل له والجنس هو تمام ما به المشاركة، ولا فصل لما لا جنس له كذلك. والفصل هو تمام ما به الممايزة على ما عرف من قبل. فالذي لا جنس له ولا فصل فلا حد له إذن والحد هو التعريف بالمقومات وإنها من الأجناس والفصول كما مر .
As for the definition (hadd), it is known by various definitions: [a] one is that it is the statement that indicates the quiddity (mahiyya) of a thing; [b] another is that it is a statement that makes known a summary of one thing which is the definiendum, a definition by which the essence of that thing is known as it is; [c] another is that it is a statement that forms the quiddity of a thing in the soul. Our saying 'a statement that forms' excludes the single word from being a definition, since a statement is composite as previously mentioned. Then the definition may be complete or it may not be, rather it may be incomplete. The complete one is a statement that forms the quiddity of a thing in the soul, its differentia being preceded by its proximate genus, and it is what makes known the quiddity as it is; so it makes it known with all that is included in it of genera and differentiae, which are the constituents (muqawwimat) as known. So the complete definition is necessarily composed of genus and differentia, and the definiendum is likewise. Thus the definition of the complete definition is composed of genus and differentia. The genus is the statement, for the statement is the proximate genus and the rest is its differentia. So our statement 'it forms its differentia by its proximate genus' excludes others from being a definition, so that when you say in the definition of human 'rational animal', you define it by a definitional definition, unlike if you said 'animal rational', even though this is composed of genus and differentia mentioned earlier. Then genus and differentia are necessary in the complete definition. There is no genus for that which has no differentia, and the genus is that through which participation is entirely achieved, and there is no differentia for that which has no genus likewise. The differentia is that through which distinction is entirely achieved, as known before. So that which has no genus and no differentia has no definition at all. The definition is the definition by constituents, and they are the genera and differentiae as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'hadd' (definition) by listing three definitions: (a) a statement indicating the essence, (b) a statement making known the essence as it is, (c) a statement that forms the essence in the mind. It then explains that the word 'statement' excludes single words, as definitions must be composite. The author distinguishes complete (kamil) from incomplete definitions. A complete definition is composed of a proximate genus and a differentia, and it makes the essence known in full. For example, 'rational animal' for human is a complete definition, whereas 'animal rational' reverses order but still contains the components. The passage emphasizes that genus and differentia are necessary parts of a complete definition. Anything lacking either genus or differentia cannot have a definition. This is grounded in the theory of universals: the genus accounts for shared essence, the differentia for specific difference. The commentary uses the Islamic example of 'human' (insan) defined as 'rational animal' (hayawan natiq), which is standard in the tradition.

Key Takeaway
The complete definition is necessarily composed of genus and differentia; without either, there is no definition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of Salah in Hanafi fiqh: 'a set of specific actions and words beginning with takbeer and ending with tasleem.' Identify the genus and differentia. Is this a complete definition according to the criteria in this lesson?
?
Construct a complete definition of 'Sawm' (fasting) by specifying its genus (e.g., 'an act of worship') and differentia (e.g., 'abstaining from food, drink, and marital relations from dawn to sunset with intention'). Ensure the order: genus followed by differentia.

Verbal Indication in Definitions and the Genus-Differentia Requirement
Author discusses whether definitions indicate by mutabaqa or tadammun and critiques al-Sawi's view that hadd need not be composed of genus and differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mutabaqa from tadammun in definitions and evaluate al-Sawi's critique of the genus-differentia requirement for hadd.

Core Idea
A complete definition must indicate by conformity and be composed of genus and differentia.

This passage tackles a fundamental question: How do definitions indicate their meanings? Is it by conformity (mutabaqa) or inclusion (tadammun)? The author argues that for a complete definition (hadd), indication must be by mutabaqa because the goal is to know the thing as it is. He then engages with al-Sawi's view that definitions need not be composed of genus and differentia, and resolves the dispute by clarifying what counts as a definitional definition.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the two modes of indication
Identify that words indicate meanings either by mutabaqa (the word covers the entire meaning) or by tadammun (the word covers only a part). For example, 'human' by mutabaqa indicates the whole human, while by tadammun it indicates animal.
2
Understand why the author insists on mutabaqa for definitions
The author argues that the purpose of a definition is to know the thing as it is. This requires the defining expression to indicate the entire essence, which is only achieved by mutabaqa, not tadammun.
3
Analyze al-Sawi's objection
Al-Sawi claimed that a definition need only be composed of the thing's constituents, not necessarily genus and differentia. His example is 'white body': body and white are both constituents, but neither is a genus nor a differentia relative to the other.
4
Examine the author's refutation
The author rejects al-Sawi's example as a genuine hadd. For 'white body' to be defined definitionally, body must function as genus and white as differentia; otherwise it is not a strict definition. True genus is something that is composed with others, and differentia is unique.
5
Resolve the verbal dispute
The author concludes that the issue is verbal: the strict technical hadd (hadd haqiqi) necessarily comprises genus and differentia, while a broader sense of definition includes any statement indicating quiddity. The objection from the broader definition does not apply to the strict one.

وأما الألفاظ الدالة على الماهيات المركبة من الأجناس والفصول فقد قيل في دلالتها إنها بحسب أن يكون دلالة المطابقة أو دلالة التضمن، وفيه نظر فإنه يجب في التعريفات الحدية أن تكون دلالتها دلالة المطابقة أو المقصود هو معرفة الشيء كما هو ولا مجال لحصولها إلا وأن تكون الألفاظ من جملة ما تدل عليه دلالة المطابقة كلفظي الجنس والفصل على ما وضع هذا بإزائه، وذلك كذلك وإنهما من اللوازم في الحد كما مرَّ . وما ذهب إليه صاحب البصائر أن الحد لا بد وأن يكون مركبا من مقومات الشيء، فإن كانت المقومات أجناسًا وفصولاً كان الحد مركباً من الجنس والفصل. فأما إذا لم يكن كان مركباً من مجموعها كيف كانت كما في الجسم الأبيض إذا أخذ من حيث هو جسم أبيض فإن الجسم من جملة مقوماته، والأبيض كذلك، ولا يكون واحد منهما جنسًا وفصلا(۱)، فذلك على خلاف ما ذهب إليه الشيخ (۳). والحق فيه أنه بحث لفظي يتوقف على تحرير المبحث وهو الحد المركب من الجنس والفصل؛ إذ الكلام فيه مما يتركب من الجسم والأبيض مثلا إذا عرف تعريفًا حدياً كان من اللوازم أن يكون للجسم جنسا لذلك والأبيض فصلا له كذلك وإلا لا يكون تعريفه تعريفا حدياً. والشيء لا يتعين ا أن يكون جنسًا للشيء أو فصلا له إلا وأن يتركب ذلك الشيء من شيء يتركب منه غيره ؛ وذلك هو الجنس بالنسبة إليه. ومن شيء لا يتركب منه غيره وذلك هو الفصل بالنسبة إليه كذلك. ولو كان كذلك فلا يرد عليه قول من يقول إن قولكم هذا وهو أن يكون من اللوازم أن يكون الحد مركبا من الجنس و الفصل يناقض الحد المشهور وهو أن الحد هو القول الدال على الماهية. فإن القول الدال على ماهية الشيء هو ما يشتمل على مقومات ذلك الشيء سواء كانت تلك المقومات من الأجناس والفصول أو لم يكن.
As for the expressions that indicate quiddities composed of genera and differentiae, it has been said concerning their indication that it is according to whether it is indication by conformity (mutabaqa) or indication by inclusion (tadammun). But there is consideration in this, for in definitional definitions it is necessary that their indication be indication by conformity, because the intent is to know the thing as it is, and there is no scope for obtaining that unless the expressions are among those that indicate by conformity, like the words for genus and differentia according to what they are placed for, and that is so, and they are necessary in the definition as previously mentioned. What the author of al-Basa'ir (al-Sawi) held is that the definition must be composed of the constituents of the thing. So if the constituents are genera and differentiae, then the definition is composed of genus and differentia. But if they are not, then it is composed of their aggregate however they are, as in the white body when taken insofar as it is a white body, for the body is among its constituents, and so is white, and neither is a genus and a differentia. That is contrary to what the Shaykh (Ibn Sina) held. The truth is that it is a verbal dispute that depends on clarifying the inquiry, namely the definition composed of genus and differentia; for the discussion concerning something like body and white, if it is defined definitionally, it would be necessary that body be a genus for it and white a differentia for it likewise, otherwise its definition would not be a definitional definition. A thing is not determined to be a genus for something or a differentia for it unless that thing is composed of something from which something else is composed—that is the genus relative to it—and of something from which nothing else is composed—that is the differentia relative to it likewise. And if that is so, then the objection does not apply that says: 'Your statement that it is necessary that the definition be composed of genus and differentia contradicts the well-known definition, which is that the definition is the statement indicating the quiddity. For the statement indicating the quiddity of a thing is that which includes the constituents of that thing, whether those constituents are genera and differentiae or not.'
Source pages p. 300

Commentary
This passage begins by discussing how composite expressions indicate their meanings, specifically whether they do so by mutabaqa (where the word indicates the entire meaning for which it was coined) or by tadammun (where it indicates a part of that meaning). The author argues that in definitions, the indication must be by mutabaqa, because the goal is to know the thing as it is, and this requires the defining words to cover the entire essence. He then introduces the view of al-Sawi (the author of al-Basa'ir al-Nasiriyya) who claimed that a definition must be composed of the thing's constituents, but if those constituents are not genera and differentiae, then the definition can be composed of aggregates like 'white body'. Al-Sawi's example: 'white body' taken as a whole; body and white are both constituents, but neither is a genus nor a differentia. This contradicts Ibn Sina's position. The author resolves that this is a verbal dispute: if we are speaking of a definition in the strict sense (hadd), it must be composed of genus and differentia. Even in the case of 'white body', if we define it definitionally, body would have to function as genus and white as differentia, otherwise it is not a hadd. Furthermore, for something to be a genus relative to another, that thing must be composed of a component that enters into the composition of other things (genus), and a component that is not shared (differentia). The author then addresses a potential objection: that his requirement contradicts the well-known definition of hadd as 'a statement that indicates the quiddity', which includes all constituents regardless of being genus/differentia. He responds that this objection does not hold because the well-known definition is broad, and the strict hadd (complete definition) must have genus and differentia. The commentary uses the example of 'white body' (jism abyad) to illustrate the dispute, grounding it in the Islamic sciences where definitions are crucial for clarifying terms in fiqh and kalam.

The Essential Composition of Hadd: Genus and Differentia
Author discusses the composition of definition from genus and differentia, and critiques an objection from Sahib al-Basa'ir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a proper hadd definition (genus+differentia) from a mere collection of essential constituents and evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences accordingly.

Core Idea
Not every essential constituent makes a hadd; only genus and differentia qualify.

In this passage, the author clarifies a fundamental logical point: what truly constitutes a definition (hadd). He engages with Sahib al-Basa'ir's objection that a definition can be composed of any essential constituents, not necessarily genus and differentia. By analyzing the example of 'white body', the author shows that for a definition to be a proper hadd, one constituent must be a genus (broader category) and the other a differentia (unique distinction). This distinction prevents confusion between essential description and rigorous logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two views on definition composition
Find the author's initial statement that genus and differentia are necessary in definition, and then locate Sahib al-Basa'ir's contrasting claim that definition can be composed of any essential constituents.
Sahib al-Basa'ir's position is stated as: 'al-hadd la budda an yakuna murakkaban min muqawwimat al-shay'.'
2
Analyze the 'white body' example
Examine how the example of 'white body' (al-jism al-abyad) is used to illustrate the dispute. Note that both 'body' and 'white' are constituents, but neither is a genus or differentia in the strict sense.
The author argues that if 'white body' were a hadd definition, then body must be genus and white differentia, otherwise it is not a hadd.
3
Understand the author's resolution: a verbal dispute
See how the author reframes the disagreement as a verbal dispute (bahth lafzi) that depends on refining the subject: the definition in question must be specified as 'the definition composed of genus and differentia'.
The author states: 'al-haqq fihi annahu bahth lafzi yatawaqquf 'ala tahrir al-mabhath.'
4
Grasp the criterion for genus vs. differentia
Learn the criterion: something is a genus for a thing if that thing is composed of it and something else is also composed from it (i.e., it is a wider category). A differentia is that from which nothing else is composed (unique distinction).
This is the core logical condition that separates hadd from other definitions.
5
Respond to the potential contradiction with the classic definition
See how the author addresses the objection that his view contradicts the famous definition of hadd as 'the statement indicating the essence' by noting that the classic definition includes all constituents, not necessarily only genus and species.
The author says: 'al-qawl al-dall 'ala mahiyyat al-shay' huwa ma yashtamilu 'ala muqawwimat dhalika al-shay' sawa'an kanat tilka al-muqawwimat min al-ajnas wa al-fusul aw lam yakun.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما تدل عليه دلالة المطابقة كلفظي الجنس والفصل على ما وضع هذا بإزائه، وذلك كذلك وإنهما من اللوازم في الحد كما مرَّ . وما ذهب إليه صاحب البصائر أن الحد لا بد وأن يكون مركبا من مقومات الشيء، فإن كانت المقومات أجناسًا وفصولاً كان الحد مركباً من الجنس والفصل. فأما إذا لم يكن كان مركباً من مجموعها كيف كانت كما في الجسم الأبيض إذا أخذ من حيث هو جسم أبيض فإن الجسم من جملة مقوماته، والأبيض كذلك، ولا يكون واحد منهما جنسًا وفصلا(۱)، فذلك على خلاف ما ذهب إليه الشيخ (۳). والحق فيه أنه بحث لفظي يتوقف على تحرير المبحث وهو الحد المركب من الجنس والفصل؛ إذ الكلام فيه مما يتركب من الجسم والأبيض مثلا إذا عرف تعريفًا حدياً كان من اللوازم أن يكون للجسم جنسا لذلك والأبيض فصلا له كذلك وإلا لا يكون تعريفه تعريفا حدياً. والشيء لا يتعين أن يكون جنسًا للشيء أو فصلا له إلا وأن يتركب ذلك الشيء من شيء يتركب منه غيره ; وذلك هو الجنس بالنسبة إليه. ومن شيء لا يتركب منه غيره وذلك هو الفصل بالنسبة إليه كذلك. ولو كان كذلك فلا يرد عليه قول من يقول إن قولكم هذا وهو أن يكون من اللوازم أن يكون الحد مركبا من الجنس و الفصل يناقض الحد المشهور وهو أن الحد هو القول الدال على الماهية. فإن القول الدال على ماهية الشيء هو ما يشتمل على مقومات ذلك الشيء سواء كانت تلك المقومات من الأجناس والفصول أو لم يكن.
What is indicated by mutabaqa correspondence like the terms genus and species to what they are placed for, and that is so, and they are among the necessary things in definition as mentioned. And what Sahib al-Basa'ir held is that definition must be composed of the constituents of the thing; if the constituents are genera and species, then the definition is composed of genus and species. But if not, it is composed of their sum however they are, as in white body when taken insofar as it is a white body: the body is among its constituents, and white likewise, and neither is one of them genus and species. That is contrary to what the Shaykh held. The truth is that it is a verbal inquiry dependent on refining the subject: the definition composed of genus and species. For the discussion there concerns what is composed of body and whiteness, for example, if it is defined by a hadd definition, it would be necessary that body be a genus for it and whiteness a species for it, otherwise its definition would not be a hadd definition. And a thing is only determined to be a genus for something or a species for it if that something is composed of something from which something else is composed – that is the genus for it – and from something from which nothing else is composed – that is the species for it likewise. If that is so, then there is no objection from one who says, 'This statement of yours, that it is necessary for the definition to be composed of genus and species, contradicts the well-known definition that definition is the statement indicating the essence.' For the statement indicating the essence of a thing is what includes the constituents of that thing, whether those constituents are genera and species or not.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is a definitional discussion with critical engagement. The author begins by stating that the terms 'genus' (jins) and 'species' (fasl) indicate by mutabaqa correspondence what they are conventionally placed for, and that they are necessary in definition as previously established. He then presents the view of Sahib al-Basa'ir (likely al-Sawi), who argues that a definition must be composed of the actual constituents (muqawwimat) of the thing, which may be genuine genera and species or not, as in the example of 'white body': body and white are both constituents but neither is a genus or species in the strict sense. This contradicts the view of the Shaykh (Ibn Sina). The author resolves this as a verbal dispute: if we restrict 'definition' to hadd, then it must be composed of a proper genus and differentia; otherwise, it is not a hadd. He clarifies that something only becomes a genus for another if it is something from which other things are composed (i.e., a wider category), while a differentia is that from which nothing else is composed (i.e., a unique distinction). He then responds to the possible objection that this contradicts the famous definition of hadd as 'the statement indicating the essence' by noting that the latter includes all constituents, not necessarily genus and species. Thus the dispute hinges on the precise subject of discussion.

Key Takeaway

Defining Definition: Escaping Infinite Regress
Author presents and resolves the objection that defining 'hadd' leads to circularity or infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how defining 'hadd' avoids circularity and infinite regress by appealing to the absolute definition and the self-sufficient relation.

Core Idea
Defining 'definition' without regress requires recognizing its universal form and a relation that is known inherently.

You might think that defining a key term like 'hadd' (definition) leads to an endless chain of definitions. This is a classic objection in the logical tradition. Our author confronts it head on and provides a subtle resolution that hinges on the distinction between the absolute concept of definition and the specific relation that ties it to the term 'hadd'. Let's see how this works.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
The objector argues: if you define 'hadd', that definition is itself a hadd, so it would need a further definition, leading to an infinite chain or circularity.
This objection surfaces a foundational issue: can the tool of definition define itself?
2
Identify the two problematic outcomes
Either circularity (dawr) where the definition depends on itself, or infinite regress (tasalsul) where each definition requires another. Both are considered invalid in logic.
Circularity: A defined by B, B defined by A. Regress: A defined by B, B defined by C, ad infinitum.
3
Locate the author's solution
The author says: when 'hadd' becomes the defined, its definition falls under the 'absolute definition' (al-ḥadd al-muṭlaq) – a statement picturing essence. So its definition is just that absolute definition, like any other thing.
The absolute definition is the universal concept of definition, not specific to any term.
4
Recognize the role of relation
What distinguishes the definition of 'hadd' from other definitions is the relation (iḍāfa) to the term 'hadd'. This relation is known inherently once the relata (the absolute definition and the term) are known. No separate definition needed.
Relations are self-evident when their terms are understood. Thus, no regress occurs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول تعريف الحد بالحد لا يمكن فإنه إذا كان للحد حد كان لحد الحد حد آخر ; وكذلك لذلك الحد فيلزم إما الدور وإما التسلسل. لكنا نقول إنه وهو الحد لما صار محدودًا كان حده داخلا تحت الحد المطلق؛ وهو قول مصور ماهية الشيء في النفس كما مر . فحده إذن هو الحد المطلق كحد غيره أي شيء كان ذلك الغير . وإنه إنما يمتاز عن سائر الحدود من حيث إنه مضاف إلى الحد، والإضافة التي بها يتميز حد الشيء عن سائر الحدود غنية عن التعريف لما أن طبائع النسب معلومة عند العلم بالمنسوبات.
Then one may say that defining definition by definition is impossible, because if definition has a definition, then the definition of definition has another definition, and so on, leading to either circularity or infinite regress. But we say: When definition becomes the defined, its definition falls under the absolute definition – that is, a statement that pictures the essence of a thing in the mind, as mentioned. So its definition is the absolute definition, like the definition of anything else whatever that other is. It is only distinguished from other definitions in that it is related to definition, and the relation by which the definition of a thing is distinguished from other definitions is independent of definition because the natures of relations are known when the relata are known.
Source pages p. 301

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological objection-and-answer. The objector argues that if we try to define 'hadd' (definition) itself, we run into a problem: the definition of hadd would itself be a hadd, requiring a further definition ad infinitum, leading to either circularity (dawr) or infinite regress (tasalsul). The author's answer is subtle: when hadd becomes the object of definition (maḥdūd), its definition falls under the 'absolute definition' (al-ḥadd al-muṭlaq), which is the concept of a statement that pictures the essence of a thing in the mind. So the definition of hadd is just that absolute definition, like the definition of any other thing. What distinguishes it from other definitions is merely a relation (iḍāfa) to the specific term 'hadd', and this relation is known without a separate definition because once you know the relata (the absolute definition and the specific term), you automatically know the relation. Thus no regress occurs.

Key Takeaway
The definition of a term like 'hadd' does not require a separate definition for each instance; it falls under the absolute definition, and its specific relation is inherently known, thus avoiding regress.
